Customary Concomitant Indication in Classical Arabic Poetry
Illustrates customary concomitant indication through classical Arabic poetry.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish customary concomitant indication from rational concomitant indication and identify its use in Islamic textual evidence.

Core Idea
Customary concomitants are based on cultural habit, not logical necessity, yet they shape interpretation in Islamic texts.

In logic, indication by accompaniment (dalāla iltizāmiyya) can be either rational or customary. The passage uses a famous Arabic poetic line to illustrate how customary concomitants work: they rely on repeated cultural associations, not on pure reason. Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating arguments in usul al-fiqh and tafsir where customary indications often carry weight but do not yield certainty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three attributes in the poem
Read the poetic line: 'Long of the sword-belt, lofty of the tent-pole, abundant of ashes.' Note each attribute mentioned.
2
Trace each attribute to its implied meaning
Follow the commentator's explanation: long sword-belt implies tall stature; high tent-pole implies grand house and status; abundant ashes implies generosity.
The generosity implication is mediated by a chain: ashes → cooking → eaters → generosity.
3
Analyze the chain for generosity
Break down the layered implication: abundant ashes → much cooking → many eaters → generous host. This chain relies on cultural associations among the Arabs.
4
Determine the type of implication
The commentator says these are 'customary concomitants, not rational'. Ask: could a rational person deduce these without knowledge of Arab customs? No.
Rational concomitants are necessary and universal; customary ones are contingent on culture.
5
Assess epistemological status
Customary indication yields probable knowledge (zann), not certainty (yaqīn). Therefore, in formal logical arguments, it cannot be used as proof.
This distinction is important in usul al-fiqh where customary indications are accepted in some contexts but not for necessary conclusions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
طويل النجاد رفيع العماد
كثير الرماد إذا ما شتا
فقد دلّ بطول نجاده ـ وهو حمائل سيفه ـ على طول قامته، ودلّ بارتفاع عماده على عظم بيته وارتفاع مكانته، ودلّ بكثرة رماده على جوده، لأن كثرة الرماد عند العرب تدلُّ على كثرة الطبخ، وهذه تدلُّ على كثرة الآكلين، وكثرة الآكلين عنده تدلُّ على جوده، وكل هذه لوازم عرفية لا عقلية.
Long of the sword-belt, lofty of the tent-pole, abundant of ashes when he winter-pastures. So he indicated by his long sword-belt—which is the straps of his sword—the length of his stature, and indicated by the height of his tent-pole the greatness of his house and the loftiness of his status, and indicated by the abundance of his ashes his generosity, because at the Arabs abundance of ashes indicates much cooking, and this indicates many eaters, and many eaters at his place indicates his generosity. And all these are customary concomitants, not rational.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage presents a concrete example of customary concomitant indication (al-dalāla al-iltizāmiyya al-ʿurfiyya) from classical Arabic poetry. The poet describes a man with three attributes: long sword-belt, high tent-pole, and abundant ashes. The commentator explains that each attribute is a customary sign for something else: a long sword-belt indicates a tall stature, a high tent-pole indicates a grand house and high status, and abundant ashes indicate generosity. The reasoning for generosity is layered: among Arabs, many ashes imply much cooking, which implies many eaters, which implies generosity. The key point is that these implications are not rational necessities (luzūm ʿaqlī) but customary (luzūm ʿurfī), based on repeated observation and cultural norms, not pure reason. This distinction is crucial because logicians consider only rational implication as valid for logical inference.

Key Terms
دلالة التزامية
لازم عرفي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the abundance of ashes indicate according to the commentator?
A) The man is a blacksmith
B) The man has many guests, hence generosity
C) The man is wealthy
D) The man likes to cook

Key Takeaway
Customary concomitants are based on cultural patterns, not rational necessity, so they provide probable knowledge, not certainty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify one customary concomitant in the following Islamic ruling: 'If a woman sees blood for more than 15 days, it is istihada (non-menstrual bleeding).' What customary assumption underlies the 15-day limit? Why is it not a rational necessity?
?
Construct a brief example from daily fiqh practice where a customary concomitant is used but not logically binding. Explain why it is customary, not rational.

Concomitant Indication in the Qur'an
Analyzes a Qur'anic verse to show how divine attributes imply forgiveness through concomitant indication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify concomitant indication (dalāla iltizāmiyya) in a Qur'anic verse and distinguish it from explicit meaning (manṭūq al-lafẓ).

Core Idea
Concomitant indication reveals what must necessarily follow from Allah's attributes, even when not stated.

In this lesson, we examine a beautiful Qur'anic example from Surat At-Taghabun. The verse mentions divine attributes in the conditional response, but the intended meaning goes beyond the explicit words. This illustrates how concomitant indication works in divine speech.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional structure
Find the 'if... then...' pattern. The protasis is 'If you pardon, overlook, and forgive', and the apodosis is 'then indeed Allah is Forgiving and Merciful'.
The apodosis does not explicitly state God's forgiveness of the believers.
2
Extract the explicit meaning (manṭūq)
The explicit wording states two attributes: Ghafur (Forgiving) and Rahim (Merciful). No action is mentioned.
3
Determine the necessary implication
From 'Ghafur' it necessarily follows that He forgives those who forgive; from 'Rahim' that He shows mercy to those who show mercy. This implication is rational and necessary.
The implication is not stated but follows from the meaning of the attributes.
4
Recognize the type of indication
This is dalāla iltizāmiyya (concomitant indication). The explicit wording (manṭūq) indicates the attributes, and the implied meaning is a necessary accompaniment. The author notes this is rational (not merely customary).
The Qur'an often uses this to concisely convey promises or warnings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن الشواهد القرآنية قول الله تعالى في سورة (التغابن ٦٤):
﴿وَإِن تَعْفُوا وَتَصْفَحُوا وَتَغْفِرُوا فَإِنَّ اللَّهَ غَفُورٌ رَّحِيمٌ ١٤﴾.
فإن قوله تعالى: ﴿فإن الله غفور رحيم﴾ الواقع في جواب الشرط يدلُّ عن طريق الدلالة الالتزامية على أن الله يغفر لكم ويرحمكم إن أنتم عفوتم وصفحتم وغفرتم، مع أن هذا المعنى غير مدلول عليه بمنطوق اللفظ، ولكن يلزم من كون الله غفوراً رحيماً أن يكافيء أهل العفو والصفح والمغفرة بالرحمة والغفران. ولذلك حصل الاكتفاء في جواب الشرط بذكر هذين الوصفين دون التصريح بلازمهما، ونظير هذا في القرآن الكريم كثير جداً.
And among the Qur'anic evidences is the saying of God Almighty in Surah At-Taghabun (64:14): 'And if you pardon and overlook and forgive, then indeed Allah is Forgiving and Merciful.' For indeed His saying 'then indeed Allah is Forgiving and Merciful' occurring in the conditional response indicates through the path of concomitant indication that Allah will forgive you and have mercy on you if you pardon, overlook, and forgive, even though this meaning is not indicated by the explicit wording, but it follows necessarily from Allah being Forgiving and Merciful that He rewards those who pardon, overlook, and forgive with mercy and forgiveness. Therefore, sufficing in the conditional response by mentioning these two attributes without explicitly stating their necessary consequence, and similar instances in the Noble Qur'an are very many.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage provides a second example of concomitant indication (dalāla iltizāmiyya) from the Qur'an. The verse from Surat At-Taghabun presents a conditional: 'If you pardon, overlook, and forgive, then indeed Allah is Forgiving and Merciful.' The conditional response only states two divine attributes without explicitly saying that God will forgive you. However, the meaning that God will forgive you is necessarily implied by these attributes: from 'Ghafur' (Forgiving) it follows that He forgives those who forgive; from 'Rahim' (Merciful) that He has mercy on those who show mercy. This is concomitant indication: the wording does not stipulate the result, but it is a logical necessary consequence of the attributes. The author highlights that the Qur'an often suffices with mentioning such attributes instead of spelling out the corresponding action, relying on the reader's understanding of the implication. This example illustrates that concomitant indication can be rational (not merely customary) when the implication is clear from the meaning of the words themselves.

Key Terms
دلالة التزامية
منطوق اللفظ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Qur'anic example from Surat At-Taghabun, what does the mention of 'Ghafur Rahim' implicitly indicate?
A) That God is powerful
B) That God will forgive and have mercy on those who pardon
C) That the verse is a metaphor
D) That forgiveness is obligatory

Key Takeaway
Concomitant indication allows the Qur'an to convey more than its explicit wording by relying on necessary implications of attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Absolute Generality and Specificity: Definition and Negation Reversal
Defines absolute generality and specificity with examples and negation reversal rule.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which of two universals is absolutely more general or more specific, and predict the reversal of the relation under negation.

Core Idea
One universal fully contains another’s extension and more—negating both swaps the relation.

In this passage, the author introduces the third relation between universals: absolute generality and specificity (al-umum wa al-khusus al-mutlaq). Understanding this relation is crucial for correctly classifying terms and for logical operations like conversion. The passage also reveals a neat property: negating both terms reverses the direction of generality. Let's unpack it step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two universals being compared
Look for a pair of universal terms, e.g., 'animal' and 'human'. Check whether one universal applies to every individual of the other and also to individuals outside the other's extension.
The author uses concentric circles: the more specific universal's circle lies entirely inside the more general one's.
2
Determine which is absolutely more general and which is absolutely more specific
If universal A applies to every individual of B and also to other individuals, then A is absolutely more general (a'am mutlaqan) and B is absolutely more specific (akhas mutlaqan). For example, 'animal' applies to all humans and also to horses, so animal is absolutely more general.
Remember that 'absolutely' means no exception: the more specific is fully contained.
3
Apply the negation reversal property
The author states that if you take the negations (naqidayn) of two universals that are in absolute generality/specificity, the relation remains absolute generality/specificity but reversed: what was absolutely more general becomes absolutely more specific, and vice versa. For instance, if animal > human, then non-animal < non-human.
This is a form of immediate inference (contraposition-like) useful for checking consistency in definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حيوان إنسان فأحدهما أعم مطلقاً من قرينه والآخر أخص مطلقاً ولذلك سميت النسبة بينهما العموم والخصوص المطلق. الأمثلة : أ ـ مثل كلمتي : (حيوان) و (إنسان) فالأول منهما معنى كلي ينطبق على كل أفراد الثاني منهما، فكل إنسان يقال له حيوان، ولكنه ينطبق أيضاً على أفراد لا ينطبق عليها إنسان، كالفرس والغزال والطير وهكذا. والسبب في هذا أن دائرة الإنسان هي دائرة صغرى تقع ضمن دائرة الحيوان الكبرى، إذن فدائرة (حيوان) أعم مطلقاً من دائرة (إنسان) ودائرة (إنسان) أخص مطلقاً من دائرة (حيوان) فالنسبة بينهما عموم وخصوص مطلق . ب ـ ومثل كلمتي : (ماشي) و (غزال)، فالأول منهما وهو (ماشي) أعم مطلقاً، لأنه ينطبق على كل أفراد الثاني منهما، وينطبق أيضاً على أفراد أخرى لا ينطبق عليها الثاني، أما (غزال) فينطبق فقط على بعض الأفراد التي ينطبق عليها (ماشي)، إذن فدائرة ماشي أعم مطلقاً من دائرة غزال ودائرة غزال أخص مطلقاً، فالنسبة بينهما هي العموم والخصوص المطلق. ونلاحظ في هذا القسم أن النسبة بين نقيضي ما بينهما عموم وخصوص مطلق، هي أيضاً العموم والخصوص المطلق، إلا أن ما كان أعم مطلقاً يصير أخص مطلقاً، وما كان أخص مطلقاً يصير أعم مطلقاً.
Animal and human: so one of them is absolutely more general than its counterpart and the other is absolutely more specific, therefore the relation between them is called absolute generality and specificity. Examples: A) The two terms (animal) and (human): the first of them is a universal meaning that applies to every individual of the second, so every human is called an animal, but it also applies to individuals that human does not apply to, like the horse, gazelle, bird, etc. The reason for this is that the circle of human is a smaller circle that lies within the larger circle of animal. So the circle of animal is absolutely more general than the circle of human, and the circle of human is absolutely more specific than the circle of animal. So the relation between them is absolute generality and specificity. B) The two terms (walker) and (gazelle): the first, (walker), is absolutely more general because it applies to every individual of the second, and also applies to other individuals that the second does not apply to. As for (gazelle), it applies only to some individuals that (walker) applies to. So the circle of walker is absolutely more general than the circle of gazelle, and the circle of gazelle is absolutely more specific. So the relation between them is absolute generality and specificity. We note in this section that the relation between the negations of what have absolute generality and specificity is also absolute generality and specificity, except that what was absolutely more general becomes absolutely more specific, and what was absolutely more specific becomes absolutely more general.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage defines the third logical relation between two universals: absolute generality and specificity (al-umum wa al-khusus al-mutlaq). The author begins by stating the characteristic: one universal is absolutely more general (a'am mutlaqan) than its counterpart, and the other is absolutely more specific (akhas mutlaqan). Two examples illustrate this. First, 'animal' and 'human': animal applies to every human and more (horses, birds), so it is absolutely more general. The explanation uses concentric circles: human's circle lies within animal's larger circle. Second, 'walker' and 'gazelle': walker applies to every gazelle and other moving things, so it is absolutely more general. The passage then notes an important rule: the relation between the negations (naqidayn) of such universals is also absolute generality and specificity, but reversed: the negation of the more general becomes more specific, and vice versa. For example, the negation of animal (non-animal) is more specific than the negation of human (non-human)? Actually, the author states a general property: if A is absolutely more general than B, then non-A is absolutely more specific than non-B. This is a useful logical conversion.

Key Terms
عموم وخصوص مطلق
أعم مطلقاً
أخص مطلقاً
نقيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Intoxicant
B) Wine
C) Both are equal
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Absolute generality/specificity is the relation where one universal's circle fully contains another's, and negating both reverses which is more general.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

The Fourth Relation: Generality and Specificity from One Aspect
Author defines the fourth relation between universals and illustrates with overlapping circles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the relation of umum wa khusus min wajh between two universals using examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Each universal is broader in one way and narrower in another, like overlapping circles.

In this lesson, we explore the fourth relation between universals: generality and specificity from one aspect. The author defines it as each universal applying to some individuals the other applies to, and each applying to individuals the other does not. This relation is crucial for analyzing overlapping concepts in Islamic logic, where concepts like 'animal' and 'white' partially intersect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two universals
Locate the two universal terms being compared. They must be kulliyan (universals) and not individuals.
Example: 'animal' and 'white' are both universals.
2
List the extensions
Determine what individuals each universal applies to. For 'animal', it applies to all animals. For 'white', it applies to all white things (animals, plants, minerals).
Consider the full extension, not just common examples.
3
Find the overlap
Identify individuals that both universals apply to. These are the coinciding part (yatatabiqan).
Example: white animals form the overlap between 'animal' and 'white'.
4
Identify the unique parts
Find individuals that each universal applies to exclusively. For 'animal': non-white animals. For 'white': white non-animals.
These are the diverging parts (yatafariqan).
5
Check for absolute containment
Verify that neither universal is completely contained in the other. If one were fully inside the other, it would be absolute generality/specificity, not min wajh.
In 'animal' and 'white', neither is fully contained, so it is min wajh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النسبة الرابعة : (العموم والخصوص من وجه) وهي النسبة بين معنى كلي ومعنى كلي آخر من جهة انطباق كل منهما على بعض الأفراد التي ينطبق عليها الآخر؛ وانفراد كل منهما بانطباقه على أفراد لا ينطبق عليها الآخر، ومثلهما كمثل دائرتين متلاقيتين في بعض كل منهما، إذ بين دائرتي أفراد كل منهما تطابق من وجه فقط دون وجه آخر. الأمثلة : أ ـ مثل كلمتي : (حيوان) و (أبيض) هذان كليان، الأول منهما وهو حيوان ينطبق على كل فرد من أفراد الحيوانات، سواء أكان أبيض أو أحمر أو أسود أو غير ذلك، فهو أعم من (أبيض) بالنظر إلى هذا الوجه، لأن كلمة (أبيض) لا تطلق على حيوان أسود أو أحمر أو غير ذلك مما ليس بأبيض. وأما الثاني منهما وهو (أبيض) فهو ينطبق على كل ما لونه أبيض سواء أكان حيواناً أو جماداً أو نباتاً أو غير ذلك، فهو أعم من حيوان باعتبار هذا الوجه، لأن كلمة (حيوان) لا تطلق على أبيض من غير الحيوان، كالوردة البيضاء والسكر والجبس وما أشبه ذلك. إذن فكل منهما أعم من جهة وأخص من جهة، فهما يتطابقان في بعض ما صَدَقات كل منهما ويتفارقان في بعض آخر. ب ـ ومثل كلمتي : (ماء) و (حلو) فهذان أيضاً كليان، أما الأول منهما وهو (ماء) فينطبق على كل ماء سواء أكان حلواً أو مالحاً أو مراً، فهو أعم بهذا الاعتبار ـ وفي حدود هذا الوجه ـ من (حلو). وأما الثاني منهما وهو (حلو) فينطبق على كل ذي حلاوة سواء أكان ماءً أو عسلاً أو فاكهة أو سكراً أو غير ذلك، فهو أعم بهذا الاعتبار ـ وفي حدود هذا الوجه ـ من (ماء)، إذن فكل منهما أعم من وجه وأخص من وجه آخر، إذ هما يتطابقان في بعض ما صَدَقات كل منهما، ويتفارقان في بعض آخر، فبينهما عموم وخصوص من وجه.
The fourth relation: generality and specificity from one aspect (al-umum wa al-khusus min wajh). It is the relation between one universal meaning and another universal meaning, with respect to each applying to some individuals that the other applies to, and each uniquely applying to individuals that the other does not apply to. Their example is like two overlapping circles, each overlapping in part, so between the circles of instances of each there is correspondence in one aspect only, not another. Examples: A) The two terms (animal) and (white): these two are universals. The first, animal, applies to every individual animal, whether white, red, black, or otherwise; so it is more general than (white) in this respect, because the term (white) is not applied to a black or red animal or other non-white. The second, white, applies to everything that is white in color, whether animal, inanimate, plant, or otherwise; so it is more general than animal in this respect, because the term (animal) is not applied to a white non-animal, like a white rose, sugar, gypsum, and the like. So each is more general in one respect and more specific in another, thus they coincide in some of their extensions and diverge in others. B) The two terms (water) and (sweet): these are also universals. The first, water, applies to every water, whether sweet, salty, or bitter; so it is more general in this respect (and within this aspect) than (sweet). The second, sweet, applies to everything that has sweetness, whether water, honey, fruit, sugar, or otherwise; so it is more general in this respect (and within this aspect) than (water). So each is more general in one aspect and more specific in another, as they coincide in some of their extensions and diverge in others. Thus between them is generality and specificity from one aspect.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth logical relation between two universals: generality and specificity from one aspect (al-umum wa al-khusus min wajh). The author explains that each universal applies to some individuals of the other, but each also applies to individuals that the other does not. The relation is likened to two overlapping circles: they share a common area but each has a unique area. Two examples are given. First, 'animal' and 'white': animal applies to all animals (white, black, etc.), so it is more general than white from the perspective of covering all animals; but white applies to everything white (animals, plants, minerals), so it is more general than animal from the perspective of covering all white things. They overlap in white animals (e.g., a white horse). Second, 'water' and 'sweet': water applies to all water (sweet, salty, bitter), so it is more general than sweet in terms of water types; sweet applies to all sweet things (water, honey, sugar), so it is more general than water in terms of sweet things. They overlap in sweet water. The key point is that each is broader in one respect and narrower in another, hence the relation is not absolute but from one aspect.

Key Terms
عموم وخصوص من وجه
أعم من وجه
يتطابقان
يتفارقان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Consider the relation between 'human' (insan) and 'white' (abyad). Which statement best describes it?
A) Absolute generality: human is more general
B) Absolute generality: white is more general
C) Generality from one aspect: each is more general in a different respect
D) Complete equality: they apply to exactly the same individuals

Key Takeaway
The fourth relation between universals is a mutual partial overlap where each universal is broader in one respect and narrower in another.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Determine whether the relation between 'book' (kitab) and 'Islamic' (islami) is an instance of umum wa khusus min wajh. List the extensions, overlap, and unique parts, then justify your answer.
?

Distribution of the Predicate in Partial Propositions
Explains predicate distribution in partial propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the distribution status of the predicate in any partial categorical proposition and apply the rule that an indefinite in a negative context is universal.

Core Idea
In a partial negative, the predicate is always distributed; in a partial affirmative, only if explicitly quantified universally.

In categorical logic, a term is 'distributed' when the proposition covers all individuals of that term. In partial propositions (those with 'some'), the subject is never distributed, but the predicate's distribution depends on quality and quantifier. This lesson unpacks that two-state rule and shows why linguists and legal theorists say 'the indefinite in the context of negation is universal'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject's quantifier and distribution
The passage states that in a partial proposition, the subject is not distributed; only 'some' of the subject is referred to. So mark the subject as undistributed.
2
Check the quality: affirmative or negative
If the proposition is affirmative, the predicate may or may not be distributed. If negative, the predicate is always distributed.
3
In an affirmative partial, inspect the predicate's quantifier
If the predicate has a universal quantifier (like 'every' or 'all'), it is distributed. If not quantified, it is not distributed (the passage calls this 'neglected'). Example: 'Some animal is every human' distributes 'human'.
4
In a negative partial, confirm the predicate is distributed
The passage explains that 'some animal is not human' means the subject is not any individual of human (i.e., all humans are excluded). This enforces distribution of the predicate. The grammatical rule 'al-nakira fi siyaq al-nafy ta'umm' supports this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نظرة حول الاستغراق : وضح الآن لدينا أننا حين حملنا الأعم على كل أفراد الأخص كان الأخص مستغرقاً، أي : لم يبق فرد منه لم يتناوله حكم القضية. وحين عكسنا الأمر لم يسغ لنا ـ التزاماً بمبدأ الصدق في القضية ـ أن نستغرق كل أفراد الأعم، بل اضطررنا أن نتحول إلى الحكم الجزئي . وعندئذ نقول : أفراد الموضوع في القضية غير مستغرقة بالحكم عليها . أما المحمول فله حالتان : حالة الإيجاب وحالة السلب . أ ـ ففي حالة الإيجاب في القضية نلاحظ أننا إن سورناه بما يدل على استغراق جميع أفراده دل على الاستغراق، وإلا كان مهملاً، والإهمال بقوة السور الجزئي كما سبق أن علمنا، وحين يكون المحمول مستغرقاً يتضح لنا أن جميع أفراده محكوم بها على الموضوع. فحين نقول : (بعض الحيوان كل إنسان) فقد اقتطعنا من الحيوان قسماً مكافئاً لكل الناس، وحكمنا عليه بأنه كل إنسان . ب ـ وفي حال السلب في القضية نلاحظ أن المحمول لا بد أن يكون مستغرقاً جميع أفراده، وإلا لم تصدق القضية، فحين نقول: (بعض الحيوان ليس بإنسان) فمعناه : هذا البعض المراد من الحيوان ليس أي فرد من أفراد الإنسان . ولذلك يقول اللغويون والأصوليون : النكرة في سياق النفي تعمّ .
A look at distribution: It has now become clear to us that when we predicated the more general on all individuals of the more specific, the more specific was distributed, i.e., no individual of it remained untouched by the ruling of the proposition. And when we reversed the matter, it was not permissible for us - in adherence to the principle of truth in the proposition - to distribute all individuals of the more general, rather we were forced to shift to the partial judgment. And then we say: The individuals of the subject in the proposition are not distributed by the judgment upon them. As for the predicate, it has two states: the state of affirmation and the state of negation. A) In the state of affirmation in the proposition, we notice that if we quantify it with what indicates the distribution of all its individuals, it indicates distribution, otherwise it is neglected, and the neglect is by force of the partial quantifier as we have already learned. And when the predicate is distributed, it becomes clear to us that all its individuals are judged upon the subject. So when we say: 'Some animal is every human', we have cut off from animal a part equivalent to all people, and judged upon it that it is every human. B) And in the state of negation in the proposition, we notice that the predicate must be distributed over all its individuals, otherwise the proposition would not be true. So when we say: 'Some animal is not human', its meaning is: this intended part of animal is not any individual of human. And therefore the linguists and legal theorists say: The indefinite in the context of negation is universal.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage explains the concept of distribution (al-istighraq) as it applies to categorical propositions. It begins by recalling that when the more general term was predicated of all individuals of the more specific, the specific was fully distributed. But when reversed, it was not possible to distribute the general term; instead a partial judgment was necessary. The passage then states that in a partial proposition, the subject is not distributed (i.e., not all individuals of the subject are covered by the judgment). The predicate, however, has two cases: In affirmative propositions, the predicate may be distributed if explicitly quantified universally (e.g., 'some animal is every human'), otherwise it is not distributed. In negative propositions, the predicate must be distributed (i.e., the negation applies to every individual of the predicate), otherwise the proposition would be false. For example, 'some animal is not human' means that the part of animal referred to is not any human individual. This is why linguists and legal theorists say: 'The indefinite in the context of negation is universal' (i.e., it is distributed).

Key Terms
الاستغراق
المحمول
الموضوع
السور
النكرة في سياق النفي تعمّ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Distributed, because it is in a negative context.
B) Not distributed, because the proposition is partial.
C) Distributed, because it is the predicate of an affirmative.
D) Not distributed, because the subject is not distributed.

Key Takeaway
In a partial negative proposition, the predicate is always distributed; in a partial affirmative, it is distributed only if explicitly quantified universally.

Rules of Distribution in Categorical Propositions
Presents two rules: distribution in universal affirmative and universal negative propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether the subject and/or predicate are distributed in any universal categorical proposition by applying the two rules.

Core Idea
Distribution tells you which terms cover all their individuals in a proposition.

In this lesson, we uncover the rules of 'istighraq' (distribution) from the source text. These rules are crucial for evaluating the logical force of universal propositions. You will learn how to identify distribution in universal affirmative and universal negative statements, a skill essential for valid syllogistic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Look at the quantifier and negation. If it begins with 'kull' (all/every) and is affirmative, it is a universal affirmative. If it has 'no' or a universal quantifier with negation, it is a universal negative.
The text distinguishes between kulliyya mujiba (universal affirmative) and kulliyya saliba (universal negative).
2
Apply Rule 1 for universal affirmative
In a universal affirmative, the subject is always distributed. Check the predicate: if it also has a universal quantifier (e.g., 'kull'), then it is distributed; otherwise, it is not.
Example: 'Every human is an animal' – predicate 'animal' has no universal quantifier, so only subject is distributed.
3
Apply Rule 2 for universal negative
In a universal negative, both subject and predicate are always distributed. The text says: 'it follows from it that its predicate is distributed over all its individuals.'
Example: 'No star is dark' – both 'star' and 'dark' are distributed.
4
Handle singular terms
If the subject or predicate is a singular term (like a proper name), it is automatically distributed because it refers to a specific individual.
The text mentions 'shakhsi' (particular) terms are 'necessarily distributed'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ضوابط استغراق الموضوع والمحمول لأفرادهما: من دراستنا لضوابط الحمل في القضايا الحملية تبين لنا أن الموضوع قد يكون كلياً مستغرقاً لجميع أفراده، وقد يكون كلياً غير مستغرق، وقد يكون شخصياً، والشخصي مستغرق لا محالة، وأن المحمول كذلك قد يكون كلياً مستغرقاً لجميع أفراده، وقد يكون كلياً غير مستغرق لجميع أفراده، وقد يكون شخصياً، والشخصي مستغرق لا محالة، وحول الاستغراق وعدمه نستخلص الضوابط التالية: الضابط الأول: الكلية الموجبة تفيد استغراق موضوعها لجميع أفراده، ولا تفيد استغراق محمولها إلا إذا كان المحمول مسوّراً بسور كلي أيضاً. الأمثلة: كل إنسان حيوان: كل نجم مضيء: كلية موجبة تفيد استغراق موضوعها فقط لجميع أفراده، ولا تفيد استغراق محمولها لجميع أفراده. كل نجوم السماء وكواكبها هي كل زينتها: كل ما لدينا من مال هو كل ما أنفقنا في الرحلة: وبقوة هذه شخصية الموضوع والمحمول. مثل: أبو حفص عمر: كلية موجبة تفيد استغراق موضوعها لجميع أفراده، وتفيد استغراق محمولها لجميع أفراده، لأن المحمول مسوّر أيضاً بسور كلي. الضابط الثاني: الكلية السالبة تفيد استغراق موضوعها لجميع أفراده، ويلزم منه استغراق محمولها لجميع أفراده.
Rules of distribution of the subject and predicate over their individuals: From our study of the rules of predication in categorical propositions, it became clear to us that the subject may be universal distributed over all its individuals, may be universal not distributed, and may be particular, and the particular is necessarily distributed; and similarly the predicate may be universal distributed over all its individuals, may be universal not distributed over all its individuals, and may be particular, and the particular is necessarily distributed. Concerning distribution and its absence, we extract the following rules: The first rule: The universal affirmative proposition indicates distribution of its subject over all its individuals, and does not indicate distribution of its predicate except if the predicate is also quantified by a universal quantifier. Examples: 'Every human is an animal': 'Every star is luminous': a universal affirmative indicates distribution of its subject only over all its individuals, and does not indicate distribution of its predicate over all its individuals. 'All the stars of the sky and their planets are all its adornment': 'All that we have of wealth is all that we spent on the trip': and by force of this, the subject and predicate are particular. Like: 'Abu Hafs Umar': a universal affirmative indicates distribution of its subject over all its individuals, and indicates distribution of its predicate over all its individuals, because the predicate is also quantified by a universal quantifier. The second rule: The universal negative indicates distribution of its subject over all its individuals, and it follows from it that its predicate is distributed over all its individuals.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage presents the rules of distribution (istighraq) for subject and predicate in categorical propositions. The author first establishes that terms can be universal distributed (covering all individuals), universal not distributed, or particular (which is always distributed). Then he gives two specific rules. Rule 1: Universal affirmative propositions distribute only the subject, unless the predicate itself has a universal quantifier (like 'all' or 'every'). Examples clarify: 'Every human is an animal' distributes 'human' but not 'animal'. In contrast, 'All the stars of the sky are all its adornment' has a predicate also quantified by 'all' (kull), so both subject and predicate are distributed. When the subject and predicate are singular terms (like 'Abu Hafs Umar'), the proposition is effectively universal and both terms are distributed because they are singular (particular terms are inherently distributed). Rule 2: Universal negative propositions distribute both subject and predicate. This is a standard logical property: 'No S is P' implies that S is not in any P and P is not in any S. The passage succinctly states these foundational rules essential for understanding conversion and syllogistic validity.

Key Terms
استغراق
كلية موجبة
كلية سالبة
سور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) كل إنسان حيوان (Every human is an animal)
B) كل نجم مضيء (Every star is luminous)
C) كل ما لدينا من مال هو كل ما أنفقنا (All our wealth is all we spent)
D) كلية موجبة (universal affirmative)

Key Takeaway
Distribution is determined by proposition type: universal affirmative distributes subject only (unless predicate also universal), universal negative distributes both.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the proposition: 'Every fajr prayer is two rak'ahs.' Determine whether the subject and predicate are distributed, citing the relevant rule.
?

One-Way Logical Necessity
Explains one-sided necessity with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify one-way logical necessity (luzum min tariq wahid) in Islamic arguments and distinguish it from mutual necessity.

Core Idea
One-way necessity: A implies B, but B does not imply A—like crow implies blackness.

In Islamic logic, understanding whether an implication is one-way or two-way is crucial for valid reasoning. This lesson focuses on one-way necessity (luzum min tariq wahid), where one thing's existence or non-existence necessarily requires another, but not vice versa. The source illustrates this with clear examples from existence and non-existence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two terms
In a one-way necessity, there are two terms: A (the antecedent) and B (the consequent). For example, in 'existence of crow entails existence of blackness', crow is A, blackness is B.
2
Check existence implication
Determine if the existence of A necessarily brings B. In the example, whenever a crow exists, blackness exists with it.
This must be a necessary, not accidental, relation.
3
Check reverse implication
Now check if the existence of B necessarily brings A. Here, blackness can exist without a crow (e.g., a black stone), so the reverse does not hold.
4
Check non-existence implication
The same structure applies to non-existence: if life is absent, thought is absent, but thought can be absent even if life exists (e.g., plants).
The author gives this as the second type of one-way necessity.
5
Apply to Islamic examples
Consider the Islamic legal example: the existence of the 'illah (effective cause) 'intoxication' implies the prohibition of that substance, but prohibition does not imply intoxication, as other things can be prohibited for different reasons.
Practice identifying such relations in fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صور اللزوم:
١ - قد يكون اللزوم من طرف واحد، فيلاحظ أن أحد الشيئين لازم للآخر غير أن صاحبه غير لازم له.
• فقد يلزم من وجود الشيء وجود الشيء الآخر، ولكن لا يلزم من وجود الثاني وجود الأول، مثال ذلك:
يلزم من وجود الغراب وجود السواد معه، ولكن لا يلزم من وجود السواد وجود الغراب معه، لاحتمال أن يكون سواداً لشيء آخر.
• وقد يلزم من انعدام الشيء انعدام الشيء الآخر، ولكن لا يلزم من انعدام الثاني انعدام الأول، مثال ذلك:
يلزم من انعدام الحياة انعدام التفكير، ولكن لا يلزم من انعدام التفكير انعدام الحياة لاحتمال وجود الحياة من غير وجود التفكير.
٢ - وقد يكون التلازم من الطرفين بين وجوديهما وبين عدميهما، فيلاحظ أن وجود أحدهما يستلزم وجود الآخر، وانعدام أحدهما يستلزم انعدام الآخر.
مثال ذلك:
• التلازم بين وجود النهار في الأرض ووجود الشمس في مكان امتداد أشعتها إلى مكان وجود النهار.
فوجود كل منهما يستلزم وجود الآخر، وانعدام كل منهما يستلزم انعدام الآخر.
• والتلازم بين الأبوة والبنوة.
فوجود أبوّة أبي بكر لعبد الرحمن يلزم منه وجود بنوّة عبد الرحمن لأبي بكر، كذلك العكس، فوجود بنوّة عبد الرحمن لأبي بكر يلزم منه وجود أبوّة أبي بكر لعبد الرحمن.
وانعدام أي واحد منهما يستلزم انعدام الآخر.
Forms of necessity:
1 - Necessity may be one-sided, so that one of the two things is necessary for the other but its counterpart is not necessary for it.
• It may follow from the existence of a thing the existence of another thing, but it does not follow from the existence of the second the existence of the first, example:
From the existence of a crow follows the existence of blackness with it, but it does not follow from the existence of blackness the existence of a crow with it, because it is possible that it is blackness of something else.
• And it may follow from the non-existence of a thing the non-existence of another thing, but it does not follow from the non-existence of the second the non-existence of the first, example:
From the non-existence of life follows the non-existence of thinking, but it does not follow from the non-existence of thinking the non-existence of life, because life may exist without thinking.
2 - And there may be mutual necessity between the two in regard to both their existences and their non-existences, so that the existence of each entails the existence of the other, and the non-existence of each entails the non-existence of the other.
Example:
• The mutual necessity between the existence of daytime on earth and the existence of the sun in the place where its rays extend to the place of daytime.
So the existence of each entails the existence of the other, and the non-existence of each entails the non-existence of the other.
• And the mutual necessity between fatherhood and sonship.
So the existence of Abu Bakr's fatherhood of Abd al-Rahman entails the existence of Abd al-Rahman's sonship of Abu Bakr, and likewise the opposite, so the existence of Abd al-Rahman's sonship of Abu Bakr entails the existence of Abu Bakr's fatherhood of Abd al-Rahman.
And the non-existence of either entails the non-existence of the other.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage classifies the forms of logical necessity into two types: one-sided (min tariq wahid) and mutual (talazum min al-tarafayn). The author first describes one-sided necessity, which may occur in two ways: (a) existence implication: the existence of A implies the existence of B, but not vice versa (e.g., crow implies blackness, but blackness does not imply crow); (b) non-existence implication: the non-existence of A implies the non-existence of B, but not vice versa (e.g., absence of life implies absence of thought, but absence of thought does not imply absence of life, since life could exist without thought). Then the author describes mutual necessity, where existence and non-existence are reciprocal: the existence of each implies the existence of the other, and the non-existence of each implies the non-existence of the other. Examples given include the mutual dependence of daytime and sunlight (daytime exists if and only if sunlight reaches that area), and the relation of fatherhood and sonship (if Abu Bakr is father of Abd al-Rahman, then Abd al-Rahman is son of Abu Bakr, and vice versa; also the non-existence of one implies the non-existence of the other). This clarifies the two structural possibilities for logical relations.

Key Terms
لزوم من طرف واحد
تلازم من الطرفين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which pair is an example of one-way necessity (min tariq wahid) according to the passage?
A) Existence of daytime and existence of sunlight
B) Existence of fatherhood and existence of sonship
C) Existence of crow and existence of blackness
D) Existence of cause and existence of effect

Key Takeaway
One-way necessity is a logical relation where implication holds in one direction only, applicable to both existence and non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify whether the following relation is one-way or mutual: In Islamic law, the existence of 'wudhu (ablution)' implies 'ritual purity (tahara)'. Does ritual purity imply wudhu? (Consider that ghusl and tayammum also produce purity.)
?

Mutual Logical Necessity
Explains mutual necessity with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify mutual logical necessity (talazum min al-tarafayn) and give an Islamic example where two concepts are logically equivalent.

Core Idea
Mutual necessity: A and B imply each other in both existence and non-existence—they are logically equivalent.

This lesson explores mutual necessity (talazum min al-tarafayn), where two terms are so intertwined that each necessarily entails the other in both existence and non-existence. The source provides examples from the natural world and from relational concepts like fatherhood and sonship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two terms
Select two terms that are claimed to have mutual necessity, e.g., 'daytime' and 'sunlight reaching the area'.
2
Verify existence implication both ways
Check if the existence of A implies existence of B, and if existence of B implies existence of A. For daytime and sunlight, if it is daytime, sunlight must be reaching; if sunlight is reaching, it must be daytime.
3
Verify non-existence implication both ways
Check if non-existence of A implies non-existence of B, and vice versa. If it is not daytime, sunlight is not reaching; if sunlight is not reaching, it is not daytime.
4
Recognize reciprocal relationships
Mutual necessity often occurs in definitions (e.g., father-son) or conceptual pairs where each term is defined by the other.
5
Apply to an Islamic couple
Consider the concepts of 'risalah' (prophethood) and 'wahy' (revelation) are mutually necessary: a prophet receives revelation, and revelation implies a prophet.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صور اللزوم:
١ - قد يكون اللزوم من طرف واحد، فيلاحظ أن أحد الشيئين لازم للآخر غير أن صاحبه غير لازم له.
• فقد يلزم من وجود الشيء وجود الشيء الآخر، ولكن لا يلزم من وجود الثاني وجود الأول، مثال ذلك:
يلزم من وجود الغراب وجود السواد معه، ولكن لا يلزم من وجود السواد وجود الغراب معه، لاحتمال أن يكون سواداً لشيء آخر.
• وقد يلزم من انعدام الشيء انعدام الشيء الآخر، ولكن لا يلزم من انعدام الثاني انعدام الأول، مثال ذلك:
يلزم من انعدام الحياة انعدام التفكير، ولكن لا يلزم من انعدام التفكير انعدام الحياة لاحتمال وجود الحياة من غير وجود التفكير.
٢ - وقد يكون التلازم من الطرفين بين وجوديهما وبين عدميهما، فيلاحظ أن وجود أحدهما يستلزم وجود الآخر، وانعدام أحدهما يستلزم انعدام الآخر.
مثال ذلك:
• التلازم بين وجود النهار في الأرض ووجود الشمس في مكان امتداد أشعتها إلى مكان وجود النهار.
فوجود كل منهما يستلزم وجود الآخر، وانعدام كل منهما يستلزم انعدام الآخر.
• والتلازم بين الأبوة والبنوة.
فوجود أبوّة أبي بكر لعبد الرحمن يلزم منه وجود بنوّة عبد الرحمن لأبي بكر، كذلك العكس، فوجود بنوّة عبد الرحمن لأبي بكر يلزم منه وجود أبوّة أبي بكر لعبد الرحمن.
وانعدام أي واحد منهما يستلزم انعدام الآخر.
Forms of necessity:
1 - Necessity may be one-sided, so that one of the two things is necessary for the other but its counterpart is not necessary for it.
• It may follow from the existence of a thing the existence of another thing, but it does not follow from the existence of the second the existence of the first, example:
From the existence of a crow follows the existence of blackness with it, but it does not follow from the existence of blackness the existence of a crow with it, because it is possible that it is blackness of something else.
• And it may follow from the non-existence of a thing the non-existence of another thing, but it does not follow from the non-existence of the second the non-existence of the first, example:
From the non-existence of life follows the non-existence of thinking, but it does not follow from the non-existence of thinking the non-existence of life, because life may exist without thinking.
2 - And there may be mutual necessity between the two in regard to both their existences and their non-existences, so that the existence of each entails the existence of the other, and the non-existence of each entails the non-existence of the other.
Example:
• The mutual necessity between the existence of daytime on earth and the existence of the sun in the place where its rays extend to the place of daytime.
So the existence of each entails the existence of the other, and the non-existence of each entails the non-existence of the other.
• And the mutual necessity between fatherhood and sonship.
So the existence of Abu Bakr's fatherhood of Abd al-Rahman entails the existence of Abd al-Rahman's sonship of Abu Bakr, and likewise the opposite, so the existence of Abd al-Rahman's sonship of Abu Bakr entails the existence of Abu Bakr's fatherhood of Abd al-Rahman.
And the non-existence of either entails the non-existence of the other.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage classifies the forms of logical necessity into two types: one-sided (min tariq wahid) and mutual (talazum min al-tarafayn). The author first describes one-sided necessity, which may occur in two ways: (a) existence implication: the existence of A implies the existence of B, but not vice versa (e.g., crow implies blackness, but blackness does not imply crow); (b) non-existence implication: the non-existence of A implies the non-existence of B, but not vice versa (e.g., absence of life implies absence of thought, but absence of thought does not imply absence of life, since life could exist without thought). Then the author describes mutual necessity, where existence and non-existence are reciprocal: the existence of each implies the existence of the other, and the non-existence of each implies the non-existence of the other. Examples given include the mutual dependence of daytime and sunlight (daytime exists if and only if sunlight reaches that area), and the relation of fatherhood and sonship (if Abu Bakr is father of Abd al-Rahman, then Abd al-Rahman is son of Abu Bakr, and vice versa; also the non-existence of one implies the non-existence of the other). This clarifies the two structural possibilities for logical relations.

Key Terms
لزوم من طرف واحد
تلازم من الطرفين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Existence of life and existence of thought
D) Existence of knowledge and existence of the known object

Key Takeaway
Mutual necessity is a two-way logical equivalence where two terms are inseparable in both existence and non-existence.

One-Sided Entailment (al-Luzum min Taraf Wahid)
Defines one-sided entailment and gives two examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify one-sided entailment in a logical relation and distinguish it from two-sided entailment using Islamic examples.

Core Idea
One-sided entailment: if A then B, but not necessarily if B then A.

In logic, when we say 'A entails B', we often assume the reverse also holds. But that is not always true. One-sided entailment (al-luzum min taraf wahid) is a fundamental concept that teaches us to check the directionality of implication. This passage from the Islamic logical tradition defines it and gives clear examples. Mastering this will sharpen your reasoning about causes and effects, especially in juristic and theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two things in the entailment
In each example, the author gives two items: crow and blackness (wujud), life and thought ('adam). Write down the pair.
2
Check the direction of the entailment
See which item's existence or non-existence is said to imply the other. Note: it is not reversible.
Here, existence of crow implies existence of blackness, but not vice versa.
3
Test the reverse direction
Ask yourself: if the second obtains, does the first necessarily obtain? The passage says no, and gives a reason (e.g., blackness could be of another object).
This is the key to one-sided entailment.
4
Apply to an Islamic ruling
Consider the ruling: intoxicating (muskir) entails prohibition (haram), but not everything haram is intoxicating (e.g., pork is haram but not intoxicating). This is one-sided entailment.
Use this pattern in usul al-fiqh and kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صور اللزوم:
١ - قد يكون اللزوم من طرف واحد، فيلاحظ أن أحد الشيئين لازم للآخر غير أن صاحبه غير لازم له.
• فقد يلزم من وجود الشيء وجود الشيء الآخر، ولكن لا يلزم من وجود الثاني وجود الأول، مثال ذلك:
يلزم من وجود الغراب وجود السواد معه، ولكن لا يلزم من وجود السواد وجود الغراب معه، لاحتمال أن يكون سواداً لشيء آخر.
• وقد يلزم من انعدام الشيء انعدام الشيء الآخر، ولكن لا يلزم من انعدام الثاني انعدام الأول، مثال ذلك:
يلزم من انعدام الحياة انعدام التفكير، ولكن لا يلزم من انعدام التفكير انعدام الحياة لاحتمال وجود الحياة من غير وجود التفكير.
Forms of entailment: 1 - Entailment may be one-sided: one of the two things is necessary for the other but the other is not necessary for it. - It may be that from the existence of a thing follows the existence of another thing, but from the existence of the second does not follow the existence of the first. Example: from the existence of a crow follows the existence of blackness with it, but from the existence of blackness does not follow the existence of a crow with it, because it may be blackness of something else. - And it may be that from the non-existence of a thing follows the non-existence of another thing, but from the non-existence of the second does not follow the non-existence of the first. Example: from the non-existence of life follows the non-existence of thought, but from the non-existence of thought does not follow the non-existence of life, because life may exist without thought.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage defines one-sided entailment (al-luzum min taraf wahid). It is a definitional passage where the author classifies entailment into types. The text begins with the general heading 'Forms of entailment' and then gives the first type. The author provides two subtypes: entailment between existences and entailment between non-existences. For each, an example is given. The examples are logical: crow/blackness (an accidental entailment) and life/thought (thought is a necessary concomitant of life but not vice versa). The key point is that in one-sided entailment, the relationship is not reversible. This guards against assuming that if A implies B, then B implies A. In Islamic law, for example, if something is intoxicating (khamr), it is forbidden (haram), but not everything forbidden is intoxicating.

Key Terms
اللزوم من طرف واحد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) If it is a human, it is mortal; if it is mortal, it is a human.
C) If it is intoxicating, it is forbidden; but not everything forbidden is intoxicating.
D) If it is a parent, it has a child; if it has a child, it is a parent.

Key Takeaway
One-sided entailment is non-reversible; always check if the converse holds when reasoning from implication.

Mutual Entailment: When Existence and Non-existence Both Imply Each Other
Defines mutual entailment with examples of day/sun and fatherhood/sonship

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify mutual entailment and distinguish it from one-sided entailment using Islamic examples.

Core Idea
Mutual entailment means each implies the other in both existence and non-existence.

This passage introduces a key concept in Islamic logic: mutual entailment. Unlike one-sided entailment where only one direction holds, mutual entailment is a two-way street: if A exists, B exists, and if B exists, A exists; similarly, if A is absent, B is absent, and vice versa. The author gives two powerful examples: day and sun, and fatherhood and sonship, using a historical Islamic figure to ground the concept.

Guided Path
1
Extract the two conditions of mutual entailment
Note that the author states two conditions: (1) existence of one entails existence of the other, and (2) non-existence of one entails non-existence of the other. Both must hold in both directions.
This is the core definition.
2
Verify the day/sun example
Does the existence of daytime entail the sun's rays reaching that area? Yes. Does the sun's rays reaching the area entail daytime? Yes. Does the absence of daytime entail the sun's rays not reaching? Yes. Does the absence of the sun's rays entail no daytime? Yes. This satisfies mutual entailment.
3
Verify the fatherhood/sonship example
Using Abu Bakr and his son Abd al-Rahman: If Abu Bakr is a father to Abd al-Rahman, then Abd al-Rahman is a son to Abu Bakr. Conversely, if Abd al-Rahman is a son to Abu Bakr, then Abu Bakr is a father to Abd al-Rahman. Non-existence similarly holds.
This shows the mutual relationship clearly.
4
Test a non-example
Consider: 'If it is a human, it is mortal.' Does mortality entail humanity? No, because animals are also mortal. So this is a one-sided entailment, not mutual. Contrast with the father/son example where the relationship is bidirectional.
This helps solidify the concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٢ - وقد يكون التلازم من الطرفين بين وجوديهما وبين عدميهما، فيلاحظ أن وجود أحدهما يستلزم وجود الآخر، وانعدام أحدهما يستلزم انعدام الآخر.
مثال ذلك:
• التلازم بين وجود النهار في الأرض ووجود الشمس في مكان امتداد أشعتها إلى مكان وجود النهار.
فوجود كل منهما يستلزم وجود الآخر، وانعدام كل منهما يستلزم انعدام الآخر.
• والتلازم بين الأبوة والبنوة.
فوجود أبوّة أبي بكر لعبد الرحمن يلزم منه وجود بنوّة عبد الرحمن لأبي بكر، كذلك العكس، فوجود بنوّة عبد الرحمن لأبي بكر يلزم منه وجود أبوّة أبي بكر لعبد الرحمن.
وانعدام أي واحد منهما يستلزم انعدام الآخر.
2 - And co-extensiveness may be from both sides, between their existences and between their non-existences. So one observes that the existence of one entails the existence of the other, and the non-existence of one entails the non-existence of the other. Example: the co-extensiveness between the existence of daytime on earth and the existence of the sun in the place where its rays extend to the place of daytime. So the existence of each entails the existence of the other, and the non-existence of each entails the non-existence of the other. And the co-extensiveness between fatherhood and sonship. So the existence of fatherhood of Abu Bakr to 'Abd al-Rahman entails the existence of sonship of 'Abd al-Rahman to Abu Bakr, and likewise the opposite: the existence of sonship of 'Abd al-Rahman to Abu Bakr entails the existence of fatherhood of Abu Bakr to 'Abd al-Rahman. And the non-existence of either entails the non-existence of the other.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage defines mutual entailment (al-talazum min al-tarafayn). It is a definitional passage. The author states that in this type, existence entails existence and non-existence entails non-existence in both directions. Two examples are given: day/sun and fatherhood/sonship. The fatherhood/sonship example is particularly clear from Islamic history (Abu Bakr and his son Abd al-Rahman). The key point is that this is a strict bi-conditional relationship. In Islamic logic, this is like the relationship between cause and effect when they are inseparable, but note that the examples are not causal but correlational. This guards against assuming that all entailments are non-reversible.

Key Terms
التلازم من الطرفين (al-talazum min al-tarafayn): Mutual entailment where existence of each implies existence of the other, and non-existence of each implies non-existence of the other.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, mutual entailment means:
A) Existence of one entails existence of the other, but non-existence does not.
B) Existence and non-existence both entail each other in both directions.
C) Existence entails non-existence.
D) Non-existence entails existence.

Key Takeaway
Mutual entailment is a two-way relationship: each implies the other in both existence and absence.

Three Subtypes of Entailment between Existence and Non-existence
Enumerates three subtypes of entailment between existence and non-existence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the three subtypes of entailment between existence and non-existence (tard and 'aks) and identify which subtype applies to a given pair of contradictory or contrary propositions.

Core Idea
Not all opposites are symmetric: entailment can be one-way.

This lesson introduces three ways existence and non-existence can be linked. Understanding these subtypes sharpens your ability to analyze logical relationships in Islamic sciences, from kalam to fiqh. The source passage breaks down the possibilities systematically.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two sides
For any two opposing concepts, label one as 'existence' and the other as 'non-existence' of a certain property. For example, truth and falsehood: truth of a report is existence of truth, falsehood is existence of falsehood (but also non-existence of truth).
Ensure you clearly define which side corresponds to existence and which to non-existence.
2
Check the 'tard' direction
Ask: Does existence of the first entail non-existence of the second? If yes, there is tard (positive entailment). Record whether this holds.
Tard is the forward direction from one side to the other.
3
Check the 'aks' direction
Ask: Does non-existence of the first entail existence of the second? If yes, there is 'aks (converse entailment). Record whether this holds.
'Aks is the reverse direction from non-existence to existence.
4
Classify the subtype
If both tard and 'aks hold, it is subtype (a) complete mutual exclusion. If only tard holds, it is subtype (b). If only 'aks holds, it is subtype (c). Use the examples from the passage to verify your classification.
The passage gives examples: truth/falsehood for (a), life/stone for (b). No explicit example for (c), but try to think of one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٣ - وقد يكون التلازم بين وجود من طرف وعدم من الطرف الآخر.
وفي هذه الحالة :
أ ـ فقد يكون تلازماً تاماً طرداً وعكساً، كما في النقيضين المتنافيين، فوجود أحدهما يستلزم انعدام الآخر، وانعدام أحدهما يستلزم وجود الآخر.
كالصدق والكذب بالنسبة إلى الخبر.
فإن كان الخبر صدقاً لم يكن كذباً بمقتضى التنافي العقلي .
وإن كان الخبر كذباً لم يكن صدقاً بمقتضى التنافي العقلي .
ب ـ وقد يكون لزوماً بين الوجود والعدم في جانب الطرد فقط لا في جانب العكس .
فوجود أحد الطرفين يستلزم انعدام الآخر، لكن انعدام أحدهما لا يستلزم وجود الآخر.
مثال ذلك :
التلازم بالتنافي بين كون الشيء ذا حياة وكونه حجراً، الآتي عن طريق طرح احتمال الوجود.
فوجود الحياة لشيء يستلزم انعدام كونه حجراً، وكون الشيء حجراً يستلزم انعدام الحياة عنه .
ولكن حين نأتي عن طريق طرح احتمال العدم نلاحظ :
أن انعدام الحياة عنه لا يستلزم كونه حجراً.
وانعدام كونه حجراً لا يستلزم وجود الحياة فيه .
لاحتمال أن يكون في كلا الانعدامين لا حجراً ولا حياً كالخُشُبِ المسندة .
جـ ـ وقد يكون لزوماً بالتنافي بين الوجود والعدم في جانب العكس لا الطرد.
فانعدام أحدهما يستلزم وجود الآخر، لكن وجود أحدهما لا يستلزم انعدام الآخر.
3 - And co-extensiveness may be between existence from one side and non-existence from the other side. In this case: a) It may be complete co-extensiveness in positive and converse, as in the two contradictory opposites. So the existence of one entails the non-existence of the other, and the non-existence of one entails the existence of the other. Like truth and falsehood with respect to a report. For if the report is true, it is not false, by virtue of rational opposition. And if the report is false, it is not true, by virtue of rational opposition. b) And it may be entailment between existence and non-existence in the positive direction only, not in the converse direction. So the existence of one of the two sides entails the non-existence of the other, but the non-existence of one does not entail the existence of the other. Example: the co-extensiveness by opposition between a thing being alive and being a stone, arrived at by the method of removing the possibility of existence. For the existence of life in a thing entails its not being a stone, and a thing being a stone entails the absence of life from it. But when we proceed by the method of removing the possibility of non-existence, we observe that the non-existence of life does not entail its being a stone, and its not being a stone does not entail the existence of life in it, because it is possible that it is neither a stone nor alive, like supported pieces of wood. c) And it may be entailment by opposition between existence and non-existence in the converse direction only, not the positive direction. So the non-existence of one entails the existence of the other, but the existence of one does not entail the non-existence of the other.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage deals with the third type of entailment: co-extensiveness between existence and non-existence. It is a definitional passage that enumerates three subcases. The first subcase (a) is complete mutual exclusion, like truth and falsehood regarding a report — they cannot both be true or false at the same time. This is a paradigm of logical opposition. The second subcase (b) is one-sided from existence to non-existence: if something is alive, it cannot be a stone, but not being alive does not make it a stone (it could be wood). The third subcase (c) is the reverse: non-existence of one entails existence of the other, but existence does not entail non-existence. No example is given for (c) in the text. These distinctions refine the understanding of contradictory and contrary relationships.

Key Terms
النقيضين المتنافيين
طرد
عكس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which pair demonstrates complete mutual exclusion (subtype a)?
A) Life and stone
B) Existence of day and existence of sun
C) Truth and falsehood of a report
D) Fatherhood and sonship

Key Takeaway
Entailment between existence and non-existence has three subtypes: complete reciprocal, one-way from existence to non-existence, and one-way from non-existence to existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Three Types of Logical Correlation (Talazum)
Author classifies three types of logical correlation between existence and non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of logical correlation (talazum) between existence and non-existence and identify them in Islamic examples.

Core Idea
Logical correlation between existence and non-existence can be complete, forward-only, or reverse-only.

In logic, understanding how the existence or non-existence of one thing relates to another is crucial for valid arguments. The author classifies these correlations into three precise types, using examples from truth-telling and Islamic guidance. This lesson will equip you to recognize which type of correlation is at play in any given pair of concepts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two sides
Determine what is being compared: often existence of one thing (A) and non-existence of another (B), or vice versa.
The sides may be qualities, states, or categories.
2
Check forward implication (ṭard)
Does the existence of one side necessarily entail the non-existence of the other? If yes, forward implication holds.
3
Check reverse implication (ʿaks)
Does the non-existence of one side necessarily entail the existence of the other? If yes, reverse implication holds.
4
Classify the correlation
If both forward and reverse hold, it is complete (طرداً وعكساً). If only forward holds, it is forward-only. If only reverse holds, it is reverse-only.
The passage gives examples for each case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

فوجود أحدهما يستلزم انعدام الآخر، وانعدام أحدهما يستلزم وجود الآخر.

جانب العكس .

يستلزم وجود الآخر.

انعدام الآخر.

العقل، ولو انعدمت الهداية عن طريق العقل لوجب أن توجد عن طريق

And correlation may be between the existence of one side and the non-existence of the other side.
In this case:
A) It may be a complete correlation, coextensive and converse, as in the two contradictory opposites. For the existence of one entails the non-existence of the other, and the non-existence of one entails the existence of the other. Like truth and falsehood with respect to a statement. For if the statement is true, it is not false, by virtue of rational contradiction. And if the statement is false, it is not true, by virtue of rational contradiction.
B) And it may be a necessary implication between existence and non-existence in the coextensive side only, not in the converse side. So the existence of one of the two sides entails the non-existence of the other, but the non-existence of one of them does not entail the existence of the other. An example of that: The correlation by contradiction between a thing having life and its being a stone, arrived at by setting aside the possibility of existence. For the existence of life for a thing entails that it is not a stone, and a thing being a stone entails the absence of life from it. But when we proceed by setting aside the possibility of non-existence, we note that the non-existence of life from it does not entail its being a stone, and the non-existence of its being a stone does not entail the existence of life in it, due to the possibility that in both non-existences it is neither a stone nor alive, like a supported piece of wood.
C) And it may be a necessary implication by contradiction between existence and non-existence in the converse side only, not the coextensive. So the non-existence of one of them entails the existence of the other, but the existence of one of them does not entail the non-existence of the other. An example of that: The correlation by contradiction between the non-existence of guidance from revelation and the non-existence of guidance from reason. This correlation appears by way of setting aside the possibility of non-existence. For if guidance from revelation is absent, it must exist from reason; and if guidance from reason is absent, it must exist from revelation. But the existence of guidance from revelation does not entail the absence of guidance from reason, and the existence of guidance from reason does not entail the absence of guidance from revelation; rather, they may coincide, as is actually the case.
[Table summarizing the enumeration of necessary implication between two things: the implication in coextension, the thing with the thing, the implication in converse: Yes Yes Yes Yes; existence < existence <; Yes No No Yes; Yes Yes Yes Yes; non-existence < non-existence <; Yes No No Yes; Yes Yes Yes Yes; existence < non-existence <; Yes No No Yes.]
And the coextensive and converse overlap in these twelve possibilities, yielding the six possibilities that we have explained.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage defines three types of logical correlation (talazum) between the existence and non-existence of two things. The author first states that correlation can be between existence of one and non-existence of the other, then enumerates three cases.

Case A: Complete correlation (طرداً وعكساً) meaning both forward and reverse implication hold. If existence of A implies non-existence of B, and non-existence of A implies existence of B, then the two are contradictory opposites. The example given is truth and falsehood of a statement: if a statement is true, it cannot be false; if it is false, it cannot be true — by rational contradiction. This is a perfect correlation.

Case B: Correlation only in the forward direction (طرد فقط). Existence of A implies non-existence of B, but non-existence of A does not imply existence of B. The example: being alive and being a stone. If something is alive, it is not a stone; if it is a stone, it is not alive. However, the absence of life does not imply it is a stone, as it could be something else (like wood).

Case C: Correlation only in the reverse direction (عكس فقط). Non-existence of A implies existence of B, but existence of A does not imply non-existence of B. The example: guidance from revelation and guidance from reason. If one is absent, the other must be present, but both can coexist.

Finally, the author presents a summary table showing all logical possibilities of implication between two things (existence/existence, non-existence/non-existence, existence/non-existence) with 'yes' or 'no' for forward and reverse implication, concluding that from twelve combinations only six are meaningful.

All examples are drawn from rational and Islamic concepts, illustrating how these correlations function in logical reasoning.

Key Terms
تلازم
طرد
عكس
تنافي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The three types of talāzum (complete, forward-only, reverse-only) provide a precise framework for analyzing logical relationships between existence and non-existence.

Using the Tard and 'Aks Table to Analyze Entailment
Author explains the table of possibilities for entailment between two things in both directions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to use the tard and 'aks table to determine the type of entailment between two things in an Islamic context.

Core Idea
Entailment between two things is fully captured by a table of 12 possibilities, reduced to 6.

In this passage, the author introduces a systematic method for analyzing whether two things entail each other in both directions. Using the Shari'a and reason guidance example, he shows how to map all combinations of presence and absence. By mastering this table, you will be able to assess logical relations in Kalam and Usul al-Fiqh arguments.

Guided Path
1
Understand entailment and its directions
Entailment (luzum) is a necessary relation between two things. The direct direction (tard) is from thing A to thing B; the converse ('aks) is from B to A. You need to examine both.
This is not the same as logical conversion of a proposition.
2
Analyze the concrete example
The author gives: absence of Shari'a guidance entails presence of reason guidance, and vice versa. But existence of one does not entail absence of the other. This shows a specific pattern in the table.
Focus on how the example maps to the table's rows.
3
Learn the four possible scenarios
For two things A and B, there are four combos of existence (E) and absence (A): (E,E), (A,A), (E,A), (A,E). Each row in the table represents one scenario.
These are the rows in the table after the header.
4
Check tard and 'aks for each scenario
For each scenario, ask: does A entail B (tard)? does B entail A ('aks)? Mark yes or no. The table shows the results for each scenario.
The table uses 'Yes' and 'No' and symbols like '<' to indicate the direction.
5
Recognize the reduction to six distinct cases
The twelve possibilities (4 scenarios × 3 truth values? Actually 4×3? The text says 12 reduce to 6 because some combinations are logically equivalent or impossible. Understand that the method is exhaustive.
The exact reduction depends on logical constraints.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مثال ذلك :
التلازم بالتنافي بين انعدام هداية الشرع وانعدام هداية العقل، ويظهر هذا التلازم عن طريق طرح احتمال العدم .
فلو انعدمت الهداية عن طريق الشرع لوجب أن توجد عن طريق العقل، ولو انعدمت الهداية عن طريق العقل لوجب أن توجد عن طريق الشرع .
لكن وجود الهداية عن طريق الشرع لا يستلزم انعدام الهداية عن طريق العقل، ووجود الهداية عن طريق العقل لا يستلزم انعدام الهداية عن طريق الشرع، بل قد يجتمعان كما هو الواقع .

Example of that: The entailment by opposition between the absence of guidance of the Shari'a and the absence of guidance of reason, and this entailment becomes apparent by the method of removing the possibility. For if guidance is absent through Shari'a, it must exist through reason; and if guidance is absent through reason, it must exist through Shari'a. But the existence of guidance through Shari'a does not entail the absence of guidance through reason, and the existence of guidance through reason does not entail the absence of guidance through Shari'a; rather, they may both exist as is the reality. The form of determining entailment between two things: The entailment in the direct (tard) [as] something with something; the entailment in the converse ('aks). Yes Yes Yes Yes; Existence < Existence <; Yes No No Yes; Yes Yes Yes Yes; Absence < Absence <; Yes No No Yes; Yes Yes Yes Yes; Existence < Absence <; Yes No No Yes. And the direct and converse intertwine in these twelve possibilities, so their outcome becomes the six possibilities that we have explained.

Commentary
This passage defines a method for analyzing entailment (luzum) between two things. The author uses a concrete example from guidance: the absence of guidance from Shari'a necessarily implies existence of guidance from reason, and vice versa, but they do not negate each other (both can coexist). Then a table is given showing the logical possibilities for the presence or absence of two items, considering both the direct (tard) and converse ('aks) directions. The table has four rows corresponding to scenarios: both present, both absent, first present second absent, etc. The columns indicate whether the entailment holds in the direct and converse. The author notes that after combining possibilities, the twelve reduce to six distinct cases. This is a foundational tool for determining logical relations in dialectical contexts.

The Two Types of Immediate Inference
Chapter heading enumerating two types of immediate inference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list and distinguish the two types of immediate inference: opposition (taqābul) and conversion (ʿaks).

Core Idea
Immediate inference has only two forms: opposition and conversion.

This short chapter heading serves as a roadmap for the entire chapter. Before delving into details, you need to fix in your mind that immediate inference—reasoning directly from a single proposition—comes in precisely two varieties. Memorizing this taxonomy is the first step to mastering the material.

Guided Path
1
Read the chapter title
Notice that the title is 'الفصل الأول في الاستدلال المباشر' (Chapter One on Immediate Inference). This tells you the general topic.
2
Identify the two enumerated types
The text then numbers two items: ١ - التقابل (opposition) and ٢ - العكس (conversion). These are the two divisions of immediate inference.
3
Understand that these are the only two
The author lists only these two, meaning all subsequent discussion in the chapter will fall under either opposition or conversion. No other form is mentioned.
4
Prepare to explore each type
Recognize that the rest of the chapter will elaborate on opposition first, then conversion. Your task is to learn the rules for each.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الأول
الاستدلال المباشر
١ - التقابل
٢ - العكس
Chapter One: Immediate Inference. 1 - Opposition. 2 - Conversion.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage is the opening of a chapter on immediate inference (al-istidlal al-mubashir). It declares that the chapter will cover two topics: opposition (taqabul) and conversion ('aks). In Islamic logic, immediate inference is a type of argument that does not require a middle term; it draws a conclusion directly from a single proposition. Opposition deals with the relationship between contrary, contradictory, subcontrary, and subaltern propositions. Conversion involves swapping the subject and predicate of a proposition while preserving truth (under specific rules). This enumeration serves as an overview for the student to memorize the two main divisions of immediate inference that will be elaborated in the following pages.
