Deriving Immediate Inferences by Opposition
Deriving opposite propositions by changing quality or quantity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive an immediate inference by opposition from any given categorical proposition, changing either its quality or its quantity while preserving subject and predicate.

Core Idea
From any proposition, flip quality or quantity to get an immediate opposite.

In Islamic logic, the relation of taqābul (opposition) allows the mind to instantly derive a new proposition from a given one by simply changing its quality (affirmative to negative or vice versa) or its quantity (universal to particular or vice versa). This tool is foundational for constructing the square of opposition and verifying truth conditions in theological and jurisprudential arguments. The passage illustrates this with clear examples from singular and universal propositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the given proposition's quality and quantity
Look at the proposition and note whether it is affirmative (positive) or negative, and whether it is universal (covering all individuals) or particular (covering at least one).
2
Decide which change to make: quality or quantity
You can derive an opposite by either flipping the quality (affirmative ↔ negative) or flipping the quantity (universal ↔ particular). You may also combine both, but the passage focuses on single changes.
3
Construct the new proposition by applying the chosen change
Keeping the subject and predicate exactly the same, rewrite the proposition with the opposite quality or quantity. For example, from 'Every human is an animal' (universal affirmative) changing quality gives 'No human is an animal' (universal negative).
4
Verify the relation between original and derived proposition
The derived proposition is opposed to the original. In the examples, a singular affirmative and its negation are contradictory (cannot both be true or both false). For universal propositions, the universal negative derived from universal affirmative is contrary (cannot both be true but can both be false).
The author emphasizes preserving all restrictions, conditions, and attachments of the subject and predicate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التقابل بين القضايا
كل قضية موجبة أو سالبة باستطاعة الفكر أن يشتق منها مباشرة قضية أخرى مخالفة لها في الكيف (الإيجاب أو السلب) أو في الكم (الكلية والجزئية).
فإذا كانت موجبة اشتق منها بمقتضى التقابل العقلي قضية سالبة.
وإذا كانت سالبة اشتق منها بمقتضى التقابل العقلي قضية موجبة.
وإذا كانت كلية اشتق منها بمقتضى التقابل العقلي قضية جزئية.
وإذا كانت جزئية اشتق منها بمقتضى التقابل العقلي قضية كلية.
كل ذلك مع المحافظة على موضوع القضية ومحمولها وكل قيودهما وشروطهما ومتعلقاتهما.
أمثلة:
فيشتق مثلاً من القضية الشخصية الموجبة التالية: (إبليس مخلوق موجود) القضية السالبة التالية: (ليس إبليس بمخلوق موجود).
فنلاحظ أنه تحصل لدينا قضيتان متناقضتان، إذا كانت إحداهما صادقة كانت الأخرى كاذبة لا محالة.
ويشتق مثلاً من القضية الكلية الموجبة التالية: (كل إنسان حيوان) ثلاث قضايا تقابلها:
الأولى: كلية سالبة (لا شيء من الإنسان بحيوان).
Opposition between propositions. Every proposition, affirmative or negative, has the capacity for the mind to derive directly another proposition differing from it in quality (affirmation or negation) or in quantity (universality or particularity). So if it is affirmative, a negative proposition is derived from it by virtue of logical opposition. And if it is negative, an affirmative proposition is derived from it by virtue of logical opposition. And if it is universal, a particular proposition is derived from it by virtue of logical opposition. And if it is particular, a universal proposition is derived from it by virtue of logical opposition. All of that while preserving the subject and predicate of the proposition and all their restrictions, conditions, and attachments. Examples: For instance, from the following singular affirmative proposition: (Iblis is an existing creature) is derived the following negative proposition: (Iblis is not an existing creature). So we observe that we obtain two contradictory propositions; if one of them is true, the other is necessarily false. And for instance, from the following universal affirmative proposition: (Every human is an animal) are derived three propositions opposing it: First: universal negative (No human is an animal).
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage explains the concept of opposition between propositions and how immediate inferences are derived by changing either the quality (affirmative/negative) or the quantity (universal/particular) of a given proposition, while keeping the subject and predicate identical. The author first states the general rule: from any proposition, one can derive another differing in quality or quantity. Then he details four cases: affirmative yields negative, negative yields affirmative, universal yields particular, particular yields universal. All this is done while preserving the subject, predicate, and all their qualifications. The passage then provides two examples. The first example takes a singular affirmative proposition: 'Iblis is an existing creature' and derives its contradictory negative: 'Iblis is not an existing creature.' The author notes that these two propositions are contradictory (mutanaqidatan), meaning they cannot both be true or both false. The second example begins with a universal affirmative: 'Every human is an animal' and states that three opposing propositions can be derived from it. The first of these is given: the universal negative 'No human is an animal.' (The other two, presumably the particular affirmative and particular negative, are not shown due to truncation.) This passage is foundational for understanding the square of opposition and the rules of immediate inference in Aristotelian logic.

القضية (al-qaḍiyya)
الموجبة (al-mūjaba)
السالبة (al-sāliba)
الكلية (al-kulliyya)
الجزئية (al-juz'iyya)
المتناقضتان (al-mutanāqiḍatān)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
By changing quality or quantity of a proposition while preserving subject and predicate, we can immediately derive an opposing proposition, enabling logical checks of truth and falsity.

The Concept of Opposition and Its Derivation
Author defines opposition and lists basic derivation rules.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the opposite of any categorical proposition by changing its quality, quantity, or both, and identify which type of opposition results.

Core Idea
One proposition yields up to three opposites by toggling quality, quantity, or both.

This lesson introduces the foundational operation of logical opposition. The author explains that from any affirmative proposition we derive a negative, from any negative an affirmative, from a universal a particular, and from a particular a universal—all while preserving the subject and predicate. This operation is the basis for immediate inference in categorical logic, a tool essential for checking consistency in arguments across Islamic sciences such as Kalam and Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition's quality and quantity
Determine if the proposition is affirmative or negative (quality) and universal or particular (quantity). For example, 'Every human is an animal' is universal affirmative.
2
Apply the quality rule
If affirmative, change to negative; if negative, change to affirmative. This yields a proposition with opposite quality.
3
Apply the quantity rule
If universal, change to particular; if particular, change to universal. This yields a proposition with opposite quantity.
4
Combine changes (optional)
You can apply both rules simultaneously to change both quality and quantity. This yields a contradictory opposite (if both changed).
The contradictory is the most important opposition for arguments.
5
Preserve subject and predicate
Throughout the derivation, keep the subject and predicate exactly the same, including any restrictions or conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التقابل بين القضايا
كل قضية موجبة أو سالبة باستطاعة الفكر أن يشتق منها مباشرة قضية أخرى مخالفة لها في الكيف (الإيجاب أو السلب) أو في الكم (الكلية والجزئية).
فإذا كانت موجبة اشتق منها بمقتضى التقابل العقلي قضية سالبة.
وإذا كانت سالبة اشتق منها بمقتضى التقابل العقلي قضية موجبة.
وإذا كانت كلية اشتق منها بمقتضى التقابل العقلي قضية جزئية.
وإذا كانت جزئية اشتق منها بمقتضى التقابل العقلي قضية كلية.
كل ذلك مع المحافظة على موضوع القضية ومحمولها وكل قيودهما وشروطهما ومتعلقاتهما.
أمثلة:
فيشتق مثلاً من القضية الشخصية الموجبة التالية: (إبليس مخلوق موجود) القضية السالبة التالي: (ليس إبليس بمخلوق موجود).
فنلاحظ أنه تحصل لدينا قضيتان متناقضتان، إذا كانت إحداهما صادقة كانت الأخرى كاذبة لا محالة.
ويشتق مثلاً من القضية الكلية الموجبة التالية: (كل إنسان حيوان) ثلاث قضايا تقابلها:
الأولى: كلية سالبة (لا شيء من الإنسان بحيوان).
الثانية: جزئية موجبة (بعض الإنسان حيوان).
الثالثة: جزئية سالبة (بعض الإنسان ليس بحيوان).
وهذه القضايا نتجت من التقابل في الكم والكيف، وذلك بتبديل الكيف بمقابله، أو بتبديل الكم بمقابله، أو بتبديل الكيف والكم معاً بما يقابل كلاً منهما.
ولدى تبديل الكيف والكم في القضايا بمقابلاتها مع المحافظة التامة على الموضوع والمحمول وكل قيودهما وشروطهما ومتعلقاتهما تنتج التقابلات التاليات:
١ - شخصية موجبة، مثال: دمشق عاصمة الأمويين. تقابلها شخصية سالبة: دمشق ليست عاصمة للأمويين.
٢ - كلية موجبة، مثال: كل إنسان حيوان. تقابلها - كلية سالبة: لا شيء من الإنسان بحيوان؛ جزئية موجبة: بعض الإنسان حيوان؛ جزئية سالبة: بعض الإنسان ليس بحيوان.
٣ - كلية سالبة، مثال: لا شيء من الإنسان بفرس. تقابلها - كلية موجبة: كل إنسان فرس؛ جزئية موجبة: بعض الإنسان فرس؛ جزئية سالبة: بعض الإنسان ليس بفرس.
٤ - جزئية موجبة، مثال: بعض الإنسان كاتب. تقابلها - كلية موجبة: كل إنسان كاتب؛ كلية سالبة: لا شيء من الإنسان بكاتب؛ جزئية سالبة: بعض الإنسان ليس بكاتب.
٥ - جزئية سالبة، مثال: بعض الإنسان ليس بأبيض البشرة. تقابلها - كلية موجبة: كل إنسان أبيض البشرة؛ كلية سالبة: لا شيء من الإنسان بأبيض البشرة؛ جزئية موجبة: بعض الإنسان أبيض البشرة.
Opposition between propositions: Every affirmative or negative proposition allows the mind to directly derive another proposition that differs from it in quality (affirmation or negation) or quantity (universality or particularity). So if it is affirmative, a negative proposition is derived by virtue of rational opposition. If it is negative, an affirmative proposition is derived by virtue of rational opposition. If it is universal, a particular proposition is derived by virtue of rational opposition. If it is particular, a universal proposition is derived by virtue of rational opposition. All of this while preserving the subject of the proposition and its predicate and all their restrictions, conditions, and attachments. Examples: For instance, from the following particular affirmative proposition: 'Iblis is a created existent,' the following negative proposition is derived: 'Iblis is not a created existent.' We notice that we have obtained two contradictory propositions; if one is true, the other is necessarily false. And for instance, from the following universal affirmative proposition: 'Every human is an animal,' three propositions opposing it are derived: first: universal negative 'No human is an animal'; second: particular affirmative 'Some human is an animal'; third: particular negative 'Some human is not an animal.' These propositions result from opposition in quantity and quality, that is, by replacing quality with its opposite, or replacing quantity with its opposite, or replacing both quality and quantity with their respective opposites. And upon replacing quality and quantity in propositions with their opposites, while fully preserving the subject and predicate and all their restrictions, conditions, and attachments, the following oppositions result: 1. Particular affirmative, example: Damascus is the capital of the Umayyads. Its opposite: particular negative: Damascus is not the capital of the Umayyads. 2. Universal affirmative, example: Every human is an animal. Its opposites: universal negative: No human is an animal; particular affirmative: Some human is an animal; particular negative: Some human is not an animal. 3. Universal negative, example: No human is a horse. Its opposites: universal affirmative: Every human is a horse; particular affirmative: Some human is a horse; particular negative: Some human is not a horse. 4. Particular affirmative, example: Some human is a writer. Its opposites: universal affirmative: Every human is a writer; universal negative: No human is a writer; particular negative: Some human is not a writer. 5. Particular negative, example: Some human is not white-skinned. Its opposites: universal affirmative: Every human is white-skinned; universal negative: No human is white-skinned; particular affirmative: Some human is white-skinned.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of opposition (taqabul) between categorical propositions. It begins by stating that from any proposition, whether affirmative or negative, the mind can directly derive another proposition that differs either in quality (affirmation vs. negation) or in quantity (universal vs. particular). The author then gives four rules: affirmative yields negative, negative yields affirmative, universal yields particular, particular yields universal. All this is done while keeping the subject, predicate, and all their conditions intact. The author then provides examples. First, from the particular affirmative 'Iblis is a created existent,' we derive its contradictory 'Iblis is not a created existent.' This pair is a contradiction; if one is true, the other is false. Second, from the universal affirmative 'Every human is an animal,' three propositions are derived: the universal negative, particular affirmative, and particular negative. The author explains that these results come from changing quality alone (yielding the universal negative), changing quantity alone (yielding the particular affirmative), or changing both (yielding the particular negative). The passage concludes with a systematic enumeration of five types of propositions and all their opposite propositions: (1) particular affirmative has one opposite (particular negative); (2) universal affirmative has three opposites (universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative); (3) universal negative has three (universal affirmative, particular affirmative, particular negative); (4) particular affirmative has three (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular negative); (5) particular negative has three (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative). The examples provided are all within an Islamic conceptual context: Iblis, human, animal, horse, writing, white skin.

Systematic Enumeration of Opposites for Each Proposition Type
Author enumerates all opposite propositions for each type.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list all logical opposites (contradictory, contrary, subcontrary) for each of the four types of categorical propositions (A, E, I, O) and for singular propositions.

Core Idea
A universal proposition has three opposites; a particular has three; a singular has one.

After establishing the basic rules of opposition, the author provides a complete enumeration of all possible opposites for each type of categorical proposition. This systematic listing is essential for understanding the full logical relations between propositions, enabling one to construct valid arguments and detect fallacies in Islamic dialectical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Recall the four types of categorical propositions
A = universal affirmative (كل س ب), E = universal negative (لا شيء من س ب), I = particular affirmative (بعض س ب), O = particular negative (بعض س ليس ب). Also singular propositions (شخصية) which are like particulars but refer to an individual.
2
List opposites for universal affirmative (A)
From 'Every human is an animal' (A), the opposites are: universal negative (E) by changing quality, particular affirmative (I) by changing quantity, and particular negative (O) by changing both.
The contradictory is O (differ in both).
3
List opposites for universal negative (E)
From 'No human is a horse' (E), the opposites are: universal affirmative (A) by changing quality, particular affirmative (I) by changing both, and particular negative (O) by changing quantity.
The contradictory is I.
4
List opposites for particular affirmative (I)
From 'Some human is a writer' (I), the opposites are: universal affirmative (A) by changing quantity, universal negative (E) by changing both, and particular negative (O) by changing quality.
The contradictory is E.
5
List opposites for particular negative (O)
From 'Some human is not white-skinned' (O), the opposites are: universal affirmative (A) by changing both, universal negative (E) by changing quantity, and particular affirmative (I) by changing quality.
The contradictory is A.

Opposition between propositions: Every affirmative or negative proposition allows the mind to directly derive another proposition that differs from it in quality (affirmation or negation) or quantity (universality or particularity). So if it is affirmative, a negative proposition is derived by virtue of rational opposition. If it is negative, an affirmative proposition is derived by virtue of rational opposition. If it is universal, a particular proposition is derived by virtue of rational opposition. If it is particular, a universal proposition is derived by virtue of rational opposition. All of this while preserving the subject of the proposition and its predicate and all their restrictions, conditions, and attachments. Examples: For instance, from the following particular affirmative proposition: 'Iblis is a created existent,' the following negative proposition is derived: 'Iblis is not a created existent.' We notice that we have obtained two contradictory propositions; if one is true, the other is necessarily false. And for instance, from the following universal affirmative proposition: 'Every human is an animal,' three propositions opposing it are derived: first: universal negative 'No human is an animal'; second: particular affirmative 'Some human is an animal'; third: particular negative 'Some human is not an animal.' These propositions result from opposition in quantity and quality, that is, by replacing quality with its opposite, or replacing quantity with its opposite, or replacing both quality and quantity with their respective opposites. And upon replacing quality and quantity in propositions with their opposites, while fully preserving the subject and predicate and all their restrictions, conditions, and attachments, the following oppositions result: 1. Particular affirmative, example: Damascus is the capital of the Umayyads. Its opposite: particular negative: Damascus is not the capital of the Umayyads. 2. Universal affirmative, example: Every human is an animal. Its opposites: universal negative: No human is an animal; particular affirmative: Some human is an animal; particular negative: Some human is not an animal. 3. Universal negative, example: No human is a horse. Its opposites: universal affirmative: Every human is a horse; particular affirmative: Some human is a horse; particular negative: Some human is not a horse. 4. Particular affirmative, example: Some human is a writer. Its opposites: universal affirmative: Every human is a writer; universal negative: No human is a writer; particular negative: Some human is not a writer. 5. Particular negative, example: Some human is not white-skinned. Its opposites: universal affirmative: Every human is white-skinned; universal negative: No human is white-skinned; particular affirmative: Some human is white-skinned.

How to Find the Contradictory of a Singular Proposition
Defines contradiction rule for singular propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the contradictory of any singular proposition by flipping its quality while preserving all other elements.

Core Idea
Contradiction flips quality only, keeping subject and predicate identical.

In this lesson, we explore the rule for contradiction in singular (shakhsiyya) propositions. The author provides clear definitions and Islamic examples to show how the quality changes while subject, predicate, and all qualifications remain identical. This is a foundational tool for checking truth in theological and legal arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the singular proposition
Look for a proposition that refers to a specific individual, e.g., 'Muhammad is a messenger' or 'Allah has no partner'.
Singular propositions are also called 'makhsusa' or 'shakhsiyya'.
2
Note its quality
Determine whether the proposition is affirmative (ijab) or negative (salab). For example, 'Allah has no partner' is negative.
3
Keep the subject and predicate identical
Ensure that the contradictory has the exact same subject and predicate, including all qualifications, conditions, and relations.
If any of these change, it is no longer a true contradictory.
4
Flip the quality
If the original is negative, change to affirmative; if affirmative, change to negative. For 'Allah has no partner', the contradictory is 'Allah has a partner'.
5
Verify contradiction
Check that the two propositions cannot both be true or both false. One must be true and the other false.
This is the defining property of tanaqud.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القضية مقابلتها
ب - الله ليس له شريك يقابلها الله له شريك
هاتان قضيتان اتحد موضوعهما ومحمولهما من كل الوجوه، ولكن اختلف الكيف فيهما إيجاباً وسلباً.
فالأولى سالبة - ومقابلتها موجبة.
وهنا أيضاً نلاحظ بداهة أنه إذا كانت الأولى صادقة حتماً، فالقضية الثانية كاذبة حتماً، إذ لا يمكن أن تكونا صادقتين معاً، ولا يمكن أن تكونا كاذبتين معاً للتناقض الذي بينهما، أي: إن صدق إحداهما ينقض حتماً صدق الأخرى ويجعلها كاذبة.

لا تأتينا الساعة تقابلها بلى وربي لتأتينكم

The proposition and its counterpart: B) 'Allah has no partner' is countered by 'Allah has a partner.' These are two propositions whose subject and predicate are identical in all respects, but their quality differs in affirmation and negation. So the first is negative, and its counterpart is affirmative. Here too we note as obvious that if the first is necessarily true, then the second proposition is necessarily false, for they cannot both be true together, nor can both be false together, due to the contradiction between them; that is, the truth of one necessarily negates the truth of the other and makes it false. And by this we note that the contradictory of a singular (particular) proposition is exactly like it with a change of quality: if it is affirmative, a negative is placed in its place in the corresponding proposition; if it is negative, an affirmative is placed in its place, while fully preserving the two extremes of the proposition (subject and predicate) with all their qualifications, conditions, and relations. So the contradictory of an affirmative singular (particular) proposition is a singular negative: (A) is (B) – its contradictory is (A) is not (B). And the contradictory of a negative singular (particular) proposition is a singular affirmative: (A) is not (B) – its contradictory is (A) is (B). Therefore those who said to Muhammad (peace be upon him): 'You are not a messenger' (a singular negative) disbelieved, in opposition to the saying of Allah Almighty: 'Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah' (a singular affirmative). The proposition and its counterpart: F: 'You are not a messenger, O Muhammad' is countered by 'Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah.' These are two propositions that are contradictories. Since the second is necessarily true – because it is a testimony from Allah for His Messenger – the first is necessarily false, and its speaker is a disbeliever, because he denies Allah in His testimony. And Allah the Exalted said in Surah Saba (34:3): 'Those who disbelieve say: The Hour will not come to us. Say: Yes, by my Lord, it will surely come to you.' The proposition and its counterpart: 'The Hour will not come to us' is countered by 'Yes, by my Lord, it will surely come to you.' The subject and predicate in both are identical in all respects: the subject is 'the Hour', and the predicate is 'its coming' – for the speakers in the first, they themselves are addressed that it comes to them in its counterpart. The only difference between them is the difference in quality only (negation and affirmation): 'does not come to us' – 'comes to you.' The two propositions are expressed in the following form: 'The Hour does not come to us' is countered by 'The Hour comes to you.' The word 'bala' (yes) in the answer is a denial of their proposition and an affirmation of its contradictory, and the oath is for emphasis. The two propositions are contradictory as is apparent, and one of them is necessarily false, namely their statement 'The Hour will not come to us' because it is the contradictory of the necessarily true proposition: 'It will certainly come to you.' From this explanation it becomes clear to us that contradiction in singular (particular) propositions is accomplished by changing the quality only, by bringing the corresponding proposition as negative instead of affirmative, and affirmative instead of negative, while fully preserving all other elements of the proposition, and all its basic qualifications, conditions, and relations. If the subject and predicate are not identical or something of their qualifications differs or
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage defines the rule of contradiction (al-tanaqud) for singular (shakhsiyya/makhsusa) propositions. It begins by giving an example: the negative singular 'Allah has no partner' and its affirmative contradictory 'Allah has a partner.' The author emphasizes that these two propositions share the same subject and predicate in all respects and differ only in quality (affirmation versus negation). He notes the obvious logical property: they cannot both be true nor both false; one truth necessarily entails the other's falsehood. The author then generalizes: the contradictory (naqid) of a singular proposition is formed by flipping the quality while keeping the subject and predicate fully identical in all their qualifications. Symbolically, 'A is B' is contradicted by 'A is not B', and vice versa. The author provides two Islamic examples to illustrate the rule and its serious implications. The first example: those who said to Prophet Muhammad 'You are not a messenger' (a singular negative) disbelieved because this proposition is the contradictory of Allah's declaration 'Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah' (a singular affirmative). Since Allah's statement is necessarily true, the other is necessarily false, and the speaker is a disbeliever for denying Allah's testimony. The second example is from Surah Saba: the disbelievers said 'The Hour will not come to us' (singular negative), and Allah commanded the Prophet to respond 'Yes, by my Lord, it will surely come to you' (singular affirmative). The author explains that these two are contradictories: same subject (the Hour) and same predicate (its coming) but different addressees ('us' vs. 'you'), yet they are unified because the addressees refer to the same people. Only the quality differs. The word 'bala' functions as a denial of the negative and affirmation of the positive; the oath emphasizes. The author concludes by restating the rule: contradiction in singular propositions is achieved by changing only the quality, while preserving all other components and qualifications. The passage ends with a caution: if the subject and predicate are not identical, or if any of their qualifications differ, the contradiction condition fails.

التناقض
الكيف

Definition and Condition of Contradictory Opposition
Defines contradictory opposition and its conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to verify that two propositions are contradictory by confirming they differ only in quality while sharing identical subject, predicate, and all conditions.

Core Idea
Contradiction requires unity in everything except affirmation/negation.

In this passage, the author establishes the core rule for logical contradiction between two propositions: they must share the same subject, predicate, and every condition, differing solely in whether they affirm or deny. This principle is demonstrated with a powerful Quranic example and generalized to all particular propositions. Understanding this rule is essential for avoiding false contradictions in argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two propositions
Find the pair of statements that are claimed to be contradictory. In the author's example, they are 'The Hour will not come to us' and 'It will surely come to you.'
2
Check the subject identity
Ensure the subject (al-mawdu') is identical in both propositions. Here both are 'the Hour.'
Subject must refer to the same thing, not just share the same wording.
3
Check the predicate identity
Ensure the predicate (al-mahmul) is identical in both, including any relations. Here both concern 'coming,' though the second adds an oath for emphasis (which does not change the predicate meaning).
4
Check all conditions and restrictions
Verify that time, place, conditions, and any other qualifications are the same for both propositions. The author emphasizes this as crucial.
5
Verify quality differs
Confirm that one proposition is affirmative and the other negative (salb vs. ijab). In the example, one is negative ('will not come') and the other affirmative ('will come').
If they were both affirmative or both negative, they could not be contradictory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هاتان قضيتان متقابلتان تقابل تناقض، ولما كانت الثانية منهما هي الصادقة حتماً، لأنها شهادة من الله لرسوله، كانت الأولى كاذبة حتماً، وكان قائلها كافراً، لأنه يكذِّب الله في شهادته.
وقال الله تعالى في سورة (سبأ ٣٤): ﴿وَقَالَ الَّذِينَ كَفَرُوا لَا تَأْتِينَا السَّاعَةُ قُلْ بَلَىٰ وَرَبِّي لَتَأْتِيَنَّكُمْ﴾.
القضية مقابلتها
لا تأتينا الساعة تقابلها بلى وربي لتأتينكم
الموضوع والمحمول فيهما متحدان من كل الوجوه، فالموضوع هو الساعة، والمحمول هو إتيانها للمتكلمين في الأولى وهم أنفسهم المخاطبون بأنها تأتيهم في مقابلتها الثانية.
والاختلاف الوحيد بينهما هو الاختلاف في الكيف فقط (السلب والإيجاب) لا تأتينا - تأتيكم - وضبط القضيتين في التعبير التالي:
الساعة لا تأتينا تقابلها الساعة تأتيكم
وكلمة (بلى) في الجواب تكذيب لقضيتهم وإثبات لنقيضها، والقسم للتأكيد، والقضيتان متناقضتان كما هو ظاهر، وإحداهما كاذبة حتماً وهي قولهم: (لا تأتينا الساعة) لأنها نقيض الصادقة حتماً وهي: (لتأتينكم).
من هذا البيان يتضح لنا أن التناقض في القضية المخصوصة (الشخصية) يتم بتبديل الكيف فقط، وذلك بإتيان القضية المقابلة سالبة بدل موجبة، وموجبة بدل سالبة، مع المحافظة التامة على كل العناصر الأخرى للقضية، وكل قيودها وشروطها ومتعلقاتها الأساسية.
فإذا لم يتحد الموضوع والمحمول أو اختلف شيء من قيودهما أو شروطهما أو متعلقاتهما في القضيتين لم تكونا متقابلتين أصلاً، ومن ثم فالاختلاف بينهما بالسلب والإيجاب لا يفضي إلى التناقض بينهما، وعندئذ فقد يصدقان وقد يكذبان، وقد تصدق إحداهما وتكذب الأخرى، كقضيتين مختلفتين لا نسبة بينهما ولا علاقة .
فلا تناقض مثلاً في الأمثلة التالية :
أ - مدينة طرابلس تقع على الساحل السوري . مدينة طرابلس لا تقع على الساحل السوري . إذا قصد بالأولى طرابلس الشام، وبالثانية طرابلس الغرب . فالموضوع في القضيتين مختلف في المعنى وإن اتحد اللفظ .
ب - كافور الإخشيدي كريم . كافور الإخشيدي ما هو كريم . إذا قصد بـ (كريم) الأولى أنه كثير العطاء، وقصد بـ (كريم) الثانية أنه يشبه ريماً أي غزالاً . فالمحمول في القضيتين مختلف في المعنى وإن اتحد اللفظ فلا تناقض .
ونظير ذلك :
خادمي مسرور - خادمي ليس بمسرور . إذا قصد بأحدهما أن اسمه مسرور وقصد بالآخر أن صفته النفسية غير مسرور . فلا تناقض لاختلاف المحمول في القضيتين وإن اتحد اللفظ .
جـ - قول الشاعر :
ينام بإحدى مقلتيه ويتقي بأخرى الأعادي فهو يقظان نائم
فقوله :
هو يقظان - هو نائم .
These two propositions are opposite in contradictory opposition. Since the second of them is necessarily true, because it is a testimony from Allah to His Messenger, the first is necessarily false, and its utterer is a disbeliever, because he denies Allah in His testimony.
And Allah Almighty said in Surah Saba' (34): "And those who disbelieve say, 'The Hour will not come to us.' Say, 'Yes, by my Lord, it will surely come to you.'"
The proposition and its counterpart:
"The Hour will not come to us" is opposed by "Yes, by my Lord, it will surely come to you."
The subject and predicate in both are identical in all respects: the subject is the Hour, and the predicate is its coming—in the first it is to the speakers, and in its counterpart it is to the addressees themselves that it will come to them.
The only difference between them is in quality (negation and affirmation): "[it] will not come to us" vs. "[it] will come to you." The formulation of the two propositions in the following expression:
"The Hour will not come to us" is opposed by "The Hour will come to you."
The word "Yes" in the response is a denial of their proposition and an affirmation of its contradictory, and the oath is for emphasis. The two propositions are contradictory as is apparent, and one of them is necessarily false, namely their statement: "The Hour will not come to us" because it is the contradictory of the necessarily true one: "It will surely come to you."
From this exposition it becomes clear to us that contradiction in a particular (individual) proposition is achieved by changing only the quality, by bringing the opposite proposition negative instead of affirmative, and affirmative instead of negative, while preserving completely all the other elements of the proposition, and all its restrictions, conditions, and essential relations.
If the subject and predicate are not identical or if any of their restrictions, conditions, or relations differ in the two propositions, they are not opposite at all, and then the difference between them in negation and affirmation does not lead to contradiction between them. In that case, they may both be true or both false, or one may be true and the other false, like two different propositions with no relation or connection between them.
For example, there is no contradiction in the following examples:
(A) "The city of Tripoli is located on the Syrian coast." "The city of Tripoli is not located on the Syrian coast." If by the first is meant Tripoli of Syria, and by the second Tripoli of the West (Libya). So the subject in the two propositions differs in meaning even if the wording is the same.
(B) "Kafur al-Ikhshidi is generous." "Kafur al-Ikhshidi is not generous." If by 'generous' in the first is meant that he is abundant in giving, and by 'generous' in the second is meant that he resembles a deer, i.e., a gazelle. So the predicate in the two propositions differs in meaning even if the wording is the same, hence no contradiction.
Analogous to that:
"My servant Masrur" - "My servant is not Masrur." If by one is meant that his name is Masrur, and by the other that his psychological state is not happy. So no contradiction due to the difference in the predicate in the two propositions even if the wording is the same.
(C) The poet's saying:
"He sleeps with one eye and guards against enemies with the other, so he is awake and asleep."
His saying: "He is awake" - "He is asleep."
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage defines the conditions for logical contradiction between two propositions. The author begins with a concrete example from the Quran: the disbelievers deny the coming of the Hour, and Allah commands a response that affirms it with an oath. The author notes that these two statements are contradictory (taqabul al-tanaqud) because they share the same subject (the Hour) and predicate (its coming), and differ only in quality (negation vs affirmation). 

He then generalizes: for any particular proposition, contradiction is achieved by switching the quality while preserving all other elements—subject, predicate, and every restriction, condition, or relation attached to them. 

Crucially, he warns that if any of these elements differ—even if the wording is identical but the meaning differs—the two propositions are not opposites at all. In such cases, they can both be true, both false, or one true and one false, with no logical relation. 

To illustrate, he gives several examples: (A) 'Tripoli is on the Syrian coast' contradicts 'Tripoli is not on the Syrian coast' only if both refer to the same Tripoli; if one is Tripoli in Syria and the other in Libya, no contradiction because the subject differs in meaning. (B) 'Kafur al-Ikhshidi is generous' contradicts its negation only if 'generous' has the same sense; if in one it means 'liberal' and in the other 'resembling a deer' (karim as a homonym), no contradiction. (C) 'My servant Masrur' vs 'My servant is not Masrur' depends on whether 'Masrur' is a name or an adjective for 'happy'. (D) A poet describes someone as both 'awake' and 'asleep' due to different eyes—this is not a logical contradiction because the predicates apply to different parts.

The overall lesson: genuine contradiction requires unity of the proposition's constituents in every respect except affirmation/negation. Any variation—even homonymy—destroys the contradictory relation.

Key Terms
تقابل التناقض
الكيف
نقيض
قضية مخصوصة (شخصية)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following pairs of propositions is logically contradictory according to the passage?
A) 'Zayd is standing' and 'Zayd is not standing' (same Zayd, same time).
B) 'Zayd is standing' and 'Zayd is not standing' (but 'Zayd' in the first refers to one person, in the second to another).
C) 'The rain is falling' and 'The rain is not falling' (first refers to today, second to tomorrow).
D) 'The Khalifa is just' and 'The Khalifa is not just' (first 'just' means fair, second means righteous).

Key Takeaway
True contradictory opposition requires the two propositions to be identical in every respect except their quality (affirmation/negation).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify two propositions from a Quranic dialogue (e.g., the Pharaoh's claim 'I am your Lord most high' and Musa's response). Check if they are truly contradictory according to the condition of identical subject/predicate and differing only in quality.
?
Given the pair 'Zakat is obligatory' and 'Zakat is not obligatory,' determine if they are logically contradictory, assuming the same definition and conditions of zakat are intended.

False Contradictions: When Opposite Wording Fails the Condition
Explains cases where opposite wording does not yield contradiction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify cases where two propositions with opposite quality are not contradictory due to a difference in the meaning of the subject, predicate, or their conditions.

Core Idea
Opposite wording does not guarantee contradiction if meaning differs.

This passage warns that contradictory opposition requires not just opposite affirm/negate wording, but also that the subject and predicate refer to the same thing with the same meaning and conditions. The author provides several examples where homonymy, equivocation, or differing parts of the subject break the contradiction. Mastering this nuance prevents false claims of contradiction in reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two propositions with opposite quality
Find the pair of propositions that appear to be contradictory because one is affirmative and the other negative. For example: 'The city of Tripoli is on the Syrian coast' and 'The city of Tripoli is not on the Syrian coast.'
2
Examine the subject for identical meaning
Check if the subject term refers to the exact same entity. In the Tripoli example, if one refers to Tripoli in Syria and the other to Tripoli in Libya, the subject is different in meaning (homonymy). Thus, no contradiction.
The wording may be identical but the referent differs.
3
Examine the predicate for identical meaning
Check if the predicate term has the same sense in both propositions. In the Kafur example, 'generous' (karim) in one means 'liberal' and in the other 'resembling a deer' (a homonym). Different meanings break the contradiction.
4
Check for differing conditions or parts
Look for differences in conditions, relations, or parts of the subject. The poet's example 'He is awake' and 'He is asleep' are not contradictory because they apply to different eyes (different conditions of the subject).
5
Determine that no logical relation exists
If any of the above differences exist, the two propositions are not in any logical opposition. They can both be true, both false, or one true and one false with no necessity. The author says they have 'no relation or connection.'

When Contradiction Is Not Real
Author provides examples where contradiction is avoided due to differences in meaning, time, aspect, or qualification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish genuine contradictions from apparent contradictions caused by differences in predicate meaning, time, object, or aspect.

Core Idea
Not every verbal opposition is a logical contradiction; unity of all conditions is required.

In logic, a contradiction occurs when two statements cannot both be true and cannot both be false under the exact same conditions. This passage drives home that condition through vivid examples. You will learn how seemingly conflicting claims can coexist once we account for differences in time, aspect, or the real meaning of the predicate, and you will be able to spot such cases in Islamic texts.

Guided Path
1
Examine the eye example
The first example describes being awake in one eye and asleep in the other. Notice that the subject (the person) is the same, but the predicate differs by aspect (eye). This shows that without unity of the aspect, there is no contradiction.
The principle: the attribute must attach to the same part or aspect.
2
Analyze the predicate shift in example (د)
Khālid is larger than Saʿīd in body, but not larger in age. The word 'larger' has a different meaning in each statement. This illustrates that the predicate must be identical in reality, even if the word is the same.
Also note the analogous example of Ṣāliḥ in knowledge vs. chest (patience).
3
Consider the temporal difference in example (هـ)
Drowsiness overcame me at midnight, but not after sunrise. The same predicate applies at different times, removing the contradiction. Time is a condition for unity.
This is a clear case of different times.
4
Study the Quranic verse example (و)
In Sūrat al-Anfāl, Allah negates and affirms throwing for the Prophet. The negation refers to the result (hitting and killing), while the affirmation refers to the physical act. The same word 'throwing' has different real meanings.
This shows that even in revelation, apparent conflicts dissolve when the intended meaning is clarified.
5
Generalize the principle
From all examples, the author concludes that any difference in objects, conditions, restrictions, attributes, states, accidents, times, or places that disrupts complete unity removes the contradiction. You should apply this test whenever two statements seem to conflict.
The key is to check: are subject, predicate, and all conditions identical?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا تناقض بينهما لأن المراد هو يقظان بالإضافة إلى إحدى عينيه، ونائم بالإضافة إلى الأخرى.
د ـ خالد أكبر من سعيد (أي : جسماً). خالد ليس أكبر من سعيد (أي : عمراً). فلا تناقض بينهما لاختلاف المحمول في الحقيقة، لأن الأولى : جسمه أكبر، والثانية : عمره أكبر. ونظير ذلك : صالح أوسع من ربيح (أي : علماً). صالح ليس أوسع من ربيح (أي : صدراً).
هـ ـ النعاس استولى عليّ (أي : عند منتصف الليل). النعاس لم يستولِ عليّ (أي : بعد طلوع الشمس). فلا تناقض بينهما لاختلاف الزمان.
و ـ قول الله تعالى في سورة الأنفال: {فَلَمْ تَقْتُلُوهُمْ وَلَكِنَّ اللَّهَ قَتَلَهُمْ وَمَا رَمَيْتَ إِذْ رَمَيْتَ وَلَكِنَّ اللَّهَ رَمَى} فلا تناقض بين نفي الرمي وإثباته للرسول، لأن المراد ما أنت الذي أصبت فقتلت إذ رميت، ولكن الله هو الذي أصاب فقتل. فالمحمول في الحقيقة مختلف بين القضيتين، وإن اتحد اللفظ في الظاهر، فالرمي الأول منصرف إلى النتيجة والحقيقة، والرمي الثاني منصرف إلى صورة العمل فقط دون آثاره.
ز ـ أنا آكل اللحم (أي : لحم الغنم). أنا لا آكل اللحم (أي : لحم الضب أو الخنزير). فلا تناقض لاختلاف متعلق المحمول، وهو يرجع في الحقيقة إلى اختلاف المحمول. ونظير ذلك يقال في كل المتعلقات والشروط والقيود التي تغير اتحاد الموضوع أو المحمول تغييراً كلياً أو جزئياً، أو في الصفات أو الأحوال أو الأعراض أو الأزمنة أو الأمكنة أو غير ذلك. فمتى اختل هذا الاتحاد بوجه من الوجوه سقط التقابل وانحل التناقض.
There is no contradiction between them because what is meant is 'awake' with respect to one eye, and 'asleep' with respect to the other.
D: Khālid is larger than Sa‘īd (i.e., in body). Khālid is not larger than Sa‘īd (i.e., in age). So there is no contradiction between them due to the difference in the predicate in reality, because the first: his body is larger, and the second: his age is larger. And analogous to that: Ṣāliḥ is more knowledgeable than Rabīḥ (i.e., in knowledge). Ṣāliḥ is not more knowledgeable than Rabīḥ (i.e., in chest [patience]).
E: Drowsiness overcame me (i.e., at midnight). Drowsiness did not overcome me (i.e., after sunrise). So there is no contradiction between them due to difference in time.
F: The saying of Allah Most High in Sūrat al-Anfāl: 'So you did not kill them, but Allah killed them; and you did not throw when you threw, but Allah threw.' So there is no contradiction between the negation and affirmation of throwing for the Messenger, because what is meant is: you are not the one who hit and killed when you threw, but Allah is the one who hit and killed. So the predicate in reality differs between the two propositions, even if the wording is apparently the same. So the first 'throwing' refers to the result and reality, and the second 'throwing' refers to the form of the act only without its effects.
G: I eat meat (i.e., sheep meat). I do not eat meat (i.e., lizard meat or pork). So there is no contradiction due to the difference in the object of the predicate, which in reality reverts to a difference in the predicate. And analogous to that is said concerning all objects, conditions, and restrictions that change the unity of subject or predicate completely or partially, or in attributes, states, accidents, times, places, or other things. So whenever this unity is disrupted in any way, the opposition falls and the contradiction is dissolved.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage is a practical illustration of a key condition for genuine contradiction (tanāquḍ). The author lists several examples where two statements that appear contradictory are actually not, because they differ in some aspect—whether it be the intended meaning of the predicate, the time, the specific object, or the aspect being considered. The unifying thread is that contradiction requires complete identity of subject, predicate, and all relevant conditions. The author begins with an example about being awake in one eye and asleep in the other, then proceeds systematically. Example (د) shows that even though the same words are used, the predicate is different in reality (body vs. age). Example (هـ) shows temporal difference. Example (و) is a sophisticated theological example from the Qur’an: the negation of throwing refers to the effect (hitting and killing), while the affirmation refers to the physical act. Example (ز) shows difference in the object (lamb meat vs. lizard/pork). The author then generalizes: any difference in the objects, conditions, restrictions, attributes, states, accidents, times, places, etc., that disrupts the complete unity of subject and predicate negates the contradiction. The final sentence is the principle: 'Whenever this unity is disrupted in any way, the opposition falls and the contradiction is dissolved.' This passage is thus definitional in nature, clarifying the conditions of valid contradiction.

المحمول

Key Takeaway

Contradictory of Universal Affirmative: The Rule of Opposite Quality and Quantity
Author derives the rule that the contradictory of a universal affirmative is a particular negative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the contradictory of any universal affirmative proposition and produce the corresponding particular negative.

Core Idea
The contradictory of 'Every A is B' is 'Some A is not B' — change both quantity and quality.

In the previous lessons you learned about contradiction between singular propositions. Now we scale up to quantified propositions. The key insight is that merely negating the copula is not enough; you must also change the quantifier. This lesson uses a vivid example to derive the rule that the contradictory of a universal affirmative is a particular negative.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Recognize that 'Every A is B' is a universal affirmative (U-A). Note its quantity (universal) and quality (affirmative).
Example: 'Every animal is a human' is a false U-A used in the lesson.
2
Understand the requirement of contradiction
Recall that two propositions are contradictory iff they cannot both be true nor both false. They must always have opposite truth values.
3
Test the naive approach
Consider negating only the quality to get 'No A is B' (universal negative). Check if it is always opposite in truth value. In the example, 'No animal is a human' is also false, so both are false – not contradictory.
This demonstrates that only changing quality is insufficient.
4
Discover the correct transformation
Change both quality and quantity: convert to a particular negative 'Some A is not B'. In the example, 'Some animal is not human' is true, opposite to the false original. This satisfies contradiction.
Both aspects of the proposition (quantifier and copula) are transformed.
5
Generalize the rule
State the rule: The contradictory of a universal affirmative (U-A) is always a particular negative (P-N). Formally: 'Every A is B' ↔ 'Some A is not B'.
This holds regardless of truth values of the specific terms.

التناقض في القضايا المحصورة
(الكلية - الجزئية، وتساويها المهملة)
بعد أن عرفت التناقض في القضايا المخصوصة (الشخصية) ستسألني كيف يكون التناقض في القضايا المحصورة، أي: المسوّرة بسور كلي أو جزئي، والمهملة تلازم كما عرفنا سابقاً المسوّرة بسور جزئي؟.
وقبل أن نقرر القاعدة النهائية في الإجابة على هذا السؤال، يحسن أن نسير في البحث عن طريق الأمثلة، ملاحظين أن التناقض كما سبق أن أوضحنا لا بد فيه حتماً من صدق إحدى القضيتين وكذب الأخرى.
وحين نلتزم الشكل القانوني للقضية الكلية والقضية الجزئية، سنلغي اعتبارات المادة الخاصة التي يأتي بها موضوع معين أو محمول معين، وننظر إلى الهيكل الرياضي، ثم نأتي بالأمثلة تطبيقاً عليه، أو نستخلص الهيكل الرياضي الثابت عن طريق استقصاء مختلف الأمثلة، ولا شك أن هذا هو الأقرب للتصور، والأبعد عن التجريد الكامل.
كيف يكون نقيض القضية الكلية؟
لنطرح عدداً من القضايا الكلية ولننظر في نقيضها:
١ - (كل حيوان إنسان) هذه قضية كلية موجبة (كاذبة). فما هو نقيضها الصادق حتماً؟.
إذا قلنا في مقابلها: (لا شيء من الحيوان بإنسان)، فحافظنا على الكمية (أي: السور الكلي) وأجرينا التغيير في الكيف فقط، كانت هذه القضية كاذبة أيضاً، فلم تختلف القضيتان صدقاً وكذباً، ولم تنقض إحداهما الأخرى، بل اتفقتا في الكذب.
هذا ينبهنا إلى أن القضية الكلية الموجبة إذا حوّلنا إيجابها إلى سلب، فلا يلزم من ذلك أن نحصل على نقيضها، بدليل هذا المثال.
إذاً فكيف نحصل على النقيض؟.
قلنا: فلنغير السور الكلي ولنجعله سوراً جزئياً، عندئذ نقول بعد هذا التغيير: (بعض الحيوان ليس بإنسان)، وبهذا نكون قد أجرينا التغيير في (الكيف) فحولنا الإيجاب إلى سلب، وفي (الكم) فحولنا السور الكلي إلى سور جزئي.
فالقضية التي حصلنا عليها : (بعض الحيوان ليس بإنسان) قضية صادقة حتماً، وهي في مقابل قضيتنا الكاذبة الأولى : (كل حيوان إنسان) تعتبر نقيضاً صحيحاً لها.
ومن هذا نستطيع أن نستخلص القانون التالي :
[نقيض الموجبة الكلية سالبة جزئية].
وبالصياغة التجريدية نقول :
كل (أ) - هو - (ب) نقيضها بعض (أ) هو ليس (ب).
Contradiction in Quantified Propositions (Universal - Particular, and their equivalent the absolute). After you have known contradiction in singular propositions, you will ask me: How is contradiction in quantified propositions, i.e., those quantified by a universal or particular quantifier, while the absolute, as we previously knew, is equivalent to the particular quantifier? Before we settle the final rule in answering this question, it is good to proceed in the investigation by way of examples, noting that contradiction, as we have previously clarified, necessarily requires the truth of one of the two propositions and the falsehood of the other. And when we adhere to the legal form of the universal proposition and the particular proposition, we will cancel considerations of specific material brought by a particular subject or predicate, and we look at the mathematical structure, then we bring examples as a practical application, or we extract the fixed mathematical structure by surveying various examples. There is no doubt that this is closer to comprehension and further from complete abstraction. How is the contradictory of a universal proposition? Let us present a number of universal propositions and look at their contradictory: 1- (Every animal is a human) This is a universal affirmative proposition (false). So what is its necessarily true contradictory? If we say in contrast: (No animal is a human), and we preserved the quantity (i.e., the universal quantifier) and only changed the quality, then this proposition would also be false. So the two propositions did not differ in truth and falsehood, and neither contradicted the other, but both agreed in falsehood. This alerts us that when we convert an affirmative universal proposition to a negative, we do not necessarily obtain its contradictory, as this example shows. So how do we obtain the contradictory? We said: Let us change the universal quantifier and make it a particular quantifier. Then we say after this change: (Some animal is not human). By this we have made a change in (quality) by converting affirmation to negation, and in (quantity) by converting the universal quantifier to a particular quantifier. The proposition we obtained: (Some animal is not human) is necessarily true, and it is in contrast to our first false proposition: (Every animal is a human) considering a correct contradictory to it. From this we can extract the following rule: [The contradictory of universal affirmative is particular negative]. In abstract formulation we say: Every (A) is (B) its contradictory is some (A) is not (B).
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage is a logical exposition that teaches how to find the contradictory of a universal affirmative proposition. The author begins by situating the discussion within quantified propositions, distinguishing them from singular propositions. He emphasizes that contradiction requires one proposition true and the other false. He then proposes to use examples while abstracting from specific content to focus on logical form. The key example is 'Every animal is a human' (false). Attempting to contradict it with 'No animal is a human' (also false) fails because both are false; contradiction requires opposite truth values. This demonstrates that merely negating the copula (changing quality) while keeping the universal quantifier does not produce a contradictory. The correct contradictory is obtained by both negating the copula and changing the quantifier from universal to particular: 'Some animal is not human' (true). The author then generalizes this into a rule: the contradictory of a universal affirmative is a particular negative. He formulates it abstractly with variables: 'Every A is B' has its contradictory as 'Some A is not B'. This rule is fundamental in the square of opposition and in logical reasoning, especially in dialectical argumentation where establishing contradiction is crucial.

The contradictory of a universal affirmative is always a particular negative; both quantity and quality must change.

Obtaining the Contradictory of Universal Propositions
Author explains the correct method for obtaining contradiction of universal propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to correctly obtain the contradictory of any universal categorical proposition by applying the rules of changing both quality and quantity.

Core Idea
Changing only the quality of a universal never guarantees a true contradictory.

In this lesson, we learn how to correctly find the contradictory (naqid) of a universal proposition. The key insight is that simply flipping affirmation to negation (or vice versa) is insufficient; we must also change the quantifier from universal to particular. This rule is derived from examples and generalised into two laws: for universal affirmative, the contradictory is particular negative; for universal negative, the contradictory is particular affirmative.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition's quality and quantity
Determine whether the proposition is universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, or particular negative. For this lesson, focus on universal propositions (affirmative or negative).
2
Test whether simply changing quality gives a contradictory
Take the original universal proposition and change its quality (affirmative to negative or vice versa) without changing quantity. Check if the resulting proposition is opposite in truth value. The author shows this fails when the original is false.
Example: 'Every animal is human' (false) -> 'No animal is human' (also false).
3
When it fails, change both quality and quantity
If changing quality alone fails, change both: for universal affirmative, the contradictory is particular negative; for universal negative, the contradictory is particular affirmative. This always yields the correct contradictory.
4
State the two general rules
Rule 1: The contradictory of a universal affirmative is a particular negative. Rule 2: The contradictory of a universal negative is a particular affirmative. This is derived from the abstract formulations given in the passage.
Abstractly: Every A is B -> Some A is not B; No A is B -> Some A is B.
5
Recognise that counterexamples where only quality works do not form a general law
The author acknowledges that in some cases (e.g., 'Every human is animal' -> 'No human is animal'), changing only quality yields a contradictory. However, this depends on the specific subject-predicate relationship and cannot be generalised into a universally valid rule.
The universal law must work for all cases, not just some.

ونجيب بأن هذا هو نقيض فعلاً، لأن مادة الموضوع والمحمول في هذا المثال ونحوه قد ساعدت على تحصيل هذا النقيض، باعتبار أن المحمول أعم من الموضوع، ولكن هل نستطيع أن نستخلص من ذلك قانوناً عاماً نجريه على كل مثال آخر، بحيث لا ينخرم معنا القانون في أي مثال؟ .

إذا قلنا في مقابلها: (كل حيوان إنسان) فحافظنا على الكمية وأجرينا التغيير في الكيف فقط، كانت هذه القضية كاذبة أيضاً، فلم تختلف القضيتان صدقاً وكذباً، ولم تنقض إحداهما الأخرى بل اتفقتا في الكذب.

فلنفعل مثلما فعلنا في نقيض الموجبة الكلية، فلنغير هنا الكلية إلى جزئية، إضافة إلى تغيير الكيف من سلب إلى إيجاب، عندئذ نحصل على القضية التالية: (بعض الحيوان إنسان).
هذه القضية التي حصلنا عليها قضية صادقة حتماً، وهي في مقابل قضيتنا الكاذبة الأولى (لا شيء من الحيوان بإنسان) تعتبر نقيضاً صحيحاً لها.

وأما الأمثلة التي يأتي فيها النقيض بتغيير الكيف فقط فلا يستخلص منها قانون تجريدي عام، يطبق على كل مثال، كما ذكرنا في نقيض الكلية الموجبة.
This alerts us that the universal affirmative proposition, if we convert its affirmation into negation, it does not necessarily follow from that that we obtain its contradictory, as evidenced by this example. So how do we obtain the contradictory? We said: let us change the universal quantifier and make it a particular quantifier. Then we say after this change: (Some animal is not human). And by this we have made a change in (quality) by converting affirmation into negation, and in (quantity) by converting the universal quantifier into a particular quantifier. So the proposition we obtained: (Some animal is not human) is necessarily true, and it, in contrast to our first false proposition: (Every animal is human), is considered a correct contradictory of it. From this we can extract the following rule: [The contradictory of universal affirmative is particular negative]. In abstract formulation we say: Every A is B, its contradictory is Some A is not B. Someone might say: We can obtain a correct contradictory without a change in quantity, in many examples, such as our saying: (Every human is an animal). This is a true universal affirmative. If we say in contrast to it: (No human is an animal) by changing only quality, we have obtained a complete contradictory of the first proposition. So why would this not be a contradictory? We answer: This is indeed a contradictory, because the matter of subject and predicate in this example and its like have helped in obtaining this contradictory, given that the predicate is more general than the subject. But can we extract from that a general law that we apply to every other example, such that our law is not violated in any example? The reality is that we cannot, as evidenced by what we saw in the first example. B. (No animal is human). This is a universal negative (false). So what is its necessarily true contradictory? If we say in contrast to it: (Every animal is human), we preserved the quantity and made a change in quality only, this proposition would also be false. So the two propositions do not differ in truth and falsity, and one does not contradict the other; rather they agree in falsity. Therefore, the universal negative proposition, if we convert its negation into affirmation, it does not necessarily follow that we obtain its contradictory, as evidenced by this example. So let us do as we did in the contradictory of universal affirmative: let us change here the universal to particular, in addition to changing the quality from negation to affirmation. Then we obtain the following proposition: (Some animal is human). This proposition we obtained is necessarily true, and it, in contrast to our first false proposition (No animal is human), is considered a correct contradictory of it. From this we can extract the following rule: [The contradictory of universal negative is particular affirmative]. In abstract mathematical formulation we say: No A is B, its contradictory is Some A is B. As for examples where the contradictory comes by changing only quality, a general abstract law cannot be extracted from them that applies to every example, as we mentioned in the case of the universal affirmative.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of how to correctly obtain the contradictory (naqid) of universal categorical propositions. The author begins by demonstrating that simply flipping the quality (e.g., from affirmative to negative) of a universal proposition does not guarantee a true contradictory. He uses the example 'Every animal is human' (false) and shows that its universal negation 'No animal is human' is also false, so they agree in falsity and are not contradictories. The correct method is to change both the quality (from affirmative to negative) and the quantity (from universal to particular), yielding 'Some animal is not human', which is true and contradicts the original. From this, the rule is extracted: the contradictory of a universal affirmative is a particular negative. The author then addresses an objection: what about cases like 'Every human is animal' (true) whose universal negative 'No human is animal' is false and indeed a contradictory? He explains that this works only because the predicate is broader than the subject, but it is not a universal law—as the first example showed, it fails. Next, he treats the universal negative proposition. For 'No animal is human' (false), changing only quality to 'Every animal is human' yields another false proposition, so not a contradictory. Changing both quality (to affirmative) and quantity (to particular) gives 'Some animal is human' (true), establishing the rule: the contradictory of a universal negative is a particular affirmative. He concludes that cases where only quality change works cannot be generalized into a law. All examples used are from the natural world and are fully in line with the Islamic logical tradition.

نقيض
سور
كيف

A) Change only the quality from affirmative to negative.
B) Change only the quantity from universal to particular.
C) Change both the quality and quantity to obtain a particular negative.
D) The contradictory is always the universal negative.

Key Takeaway
The correct contradictory of a universal proposition requires changing both its quality and quantity, not just the quality.

The Contradictory of a Universal Negative Proposition
Deriving the contradictory of a universal negative proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the contradictory of a universal negative categorical proposition by applying the required changes in quality and quantity.

Core Idea
The contradictory of a universal negative is a particular affirmative.

In this lesson, we work through the example of 'No animal is a human' to understand why simply changing the quality (to 'Every animal is a human') fails to produce a contradiction. The author demonstrates that for quantified propositions, a change in quantity is also necessary. This leads to the law that a universal negative contradicts a particular affirmative.

Guided Path
1
Observe the initial proposition
Start with the universal negative: 'No animal is a human' (لا شيء من الحيوان بإنسان). Recognize that it is false. The task is to find its contradictory, which must be true.
By definition, contradictory propositions cannot both be false.
2
Test a simple quality change
Try converting to affirmation while preserving universality: 'Every animal is a human'. Note that this proposition is also false. Therefore, it is not a contradictory.
Two false propositions cannot be contradictories.
3
Apply both quality and quantity changes
Change the quality from negative to affirmative, and change the quantity from universal to particular: 'Some animal is a human' (بعض الحيوان إنسان). This proposition is true. It is the correct contradictory.
This combination always works for universal negatives.
4
Generalize the law
From this example, we deduce: The contradictory of a universal negative is a particular affirmative. Symbolically: 'No A is B' has contradictory 'Some A is B'.
This is a general law applicable to all universal negative propositions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الواقع أننا لا نستطيع ذلك بدليل ما شهدنا في المثال الأول.
ب ـ (لا شيء من الحيوان بإنسان) هذه قضية كلية سالبة (كاذبة). فما هو نقيضها الصادق حتماً؟
إذا قلنا في مقابلها: (كل حيوان إنسان) فحافظنا على الكمية وأجرينا التغيير في الكيف فقط، كانت هذه القضية كاذبة أيضاً، فلم تختلف القضيتان صدقاً وكذباً، ولم تنقض إحداهما الأخرى بل اتفقتا في الكذب.
إذاً: فالقضية السالبة الكلية إذا حوّلنا سلبها إلى إيجاب، فلا يلزم من ذلك أن نحصل على نقيضها، بدليل هذا المثال.
فلنفعل مثلما فعلنا في نقيض الموجبة الكلية، فلنغير هنا الكلية إلى جزئية، إضافة إلى تغيير الكيف من سلب إلى إيجاب، عندئذ نحصل على القضية التالية: (بعض الحيوان إنسان).
هذه القضية التي حصلنا عليها قضية صادقة حتماً، وهي في مقابل قضيتنا الكاذبة الأولى (لا شيء من الحيوان بإنسان) تعتبر نقيضاً صحيحاً لها.
ومن هذا نستطيع أن نستخلص القانون التالي :
[نقيض السالبة الكلية موجبة جزئية].
وبالصياغة التجريدية الرياضية نقول :
لا شيء من (أ) هو (ب) نقيضها بعض (أ) هو (ب).
وأما الأمثلة التي يأتي فيها النقيض بتغيير الكيف فقط فلا يستخلص منها قانون تجريدي عام، يطبق على كل مثال، كما ذكرنا في نقيض الكلية الموجبة.
وباعتبار التناظر التام يظهر لنا : أن [نقيض الموجبة الجزئية سالبة كلية]، وأنّ [نقيض السالبة الجزئية موجبة كلية]، فتكون مجموعة القوانين لدينا في التناقض وفق الجدول التالي :
١	المخصوصة (الشخصية) الموجبة	نقيضها	مخصوصة سالبة
	(أ) هو (ب)	نقيضها	(أ) هو ليس (ب)
٢	المخصوصة (الشخصية) السالبة	نقيضها	مخصوصة موجبة
	(أ) هو ليس (ب)	نقيضها	(أ) هو (ب)
٣	الكلية الموجبة	نقيضها	جزئية سالبة
	كل (أ) هو (ب)	نقيضها	بعض (أ) ليس (ب)
٤	الكلية السالبة	نقيضها	جزئية موجبة
	لا شيء من (أ) هو (ب)	نقيضها	بعض (أ) هو (ب)
٥	الجزئية الموجبة	نقيضها	كلية سالبة
	بعض (أ) هو (ب)	نقيضها	لا شيء من (أ) هو (ب)
٦	الجزئية السالبة	نقيضها	كلية موجبة
	بعض (أ) ليس (ب)	نقيضها	كل (أ) هو (ب)
ومن هذا يتضح لنا أنه لا يحصل التناقض في القضايا ما لم يحصل فيها اختلاف في الكيف (السلب والإيجاب).
فإذا كانت محصورة (أي مسورة) وجب مع ذلك حصول اختلاف في الكم (الكلية والجزئية).
(١) وإذا كانت القضية موجهة وجب مع ذلك أيضاً حصول اختلاف في الجهة، فنقيض الضرورية هي الممكنة، ونقيض الدائمة هي المطلقة، والعكس بالعكس، فلا تناقض ما بين ضروريتين، أو ممكنتين، أو دائمتين، أو مطلقتين .
The reality is that we cannot do that, as evidenced by what we saw in the first example.
B. "No animal is a human" is a universal negative proposition (false). So what is its necessarily true contradictory?
If we say in its place: "Every animal is a human" — we preserved the quantity and only changed the quality — this proposition would also be false. So the two propositions do not differ in truth and falsity; one does not contradict the other, but rather both agree in falsehood.
Therefore: For a universal negative proposition, if we convert its negation to affirmation, it does not necessarily follow that we obtain its contradictory, as this example shows.
So let us do as we did with the contradictory of the universal affirmative: we change the universality to particularity, in addition to changing the quality from negation to affirmation. Then we obtain the following proposition: "Some animal is a human."
This proposition we have obtained is necessarily true, and it stands in opposition to our first false proposition ("No animal is a human") as a correct contradictory to it.
From this we can derive the following law:
[The contradictory of a universal negative is a particular affirmative.]
And in formal mathematical formulation:
"No A is B" — its contradictory is "Some A is B."
As for examples where the contradictory is produced by only changing the quality, no general abstract law applicable to every example can be derived from them, as we mentioned with the contradictory of the universal affirmative.
And by considering complete symmetry, it appears to us that [the contradictory of a particular affirmative is a universal negative], and that [the contradictory of a particular negative is a universal affirmative]. So the set of laws for contradiction is according to the following table:
1. Singular (particular) affirmative — its contradictory: singular negative
   (A is B) — its contradictory: (A is not B)
2. Singular (particular) negative — its contradictory: singular affirmative
   (A is not B) — its contradictory: (A is B)
3. Universal affirmative — its contradictory: particular negative
   Every A is B — its contradictory: Some A is not B
4. Universal negative — its contradictory: particular affirmative
   No A is B — its contradictory: Some A is B
5. Particular affirmative — its contradictory: universal negative
   Some A is B — its contradictory: No A is B
6. Particular negative — its contradictory: universal affirmative
   Some A is not B — its contradictory: Every A is B
From this it is clear that contradiction does not occur in propositions unless there is a difference in quality (negation and affirmation).
And if they are quantified (i.e., quantified), then along with that there must also be a difference in quantity (universality and particularity).
(1) And if the proposition is modal, then along with that there must also be a difference in modality: the contradictory of a necessary is a possible, and the contradictory of a permanent is an absolute, and vice versa. So there is no contradiction between two necessary propositions, or two possible, or two permanent, or two absolute.

Commentary
This passage is an extended explanation of how to find the contradictory (naqīḍ) of categorical propositions. The author begins with an example: the universal negative 'No animal is a human' is false. Its contradictory must be true. Simply changing the quality to 'Every animal is a human' yields another false proposition, so that is not a contradictory. The author then combines a change in quality (negation to affirmation) with a change in quantity (universal to particular), obtaining 'Some animal is a human' which is true and thus a correct contradictory. From this, the law is stated: the contradictory of a universal negative is a particular affirmative. Symbolically: 'No A is B' has contradictory 'Some A is B'. The author notes that examples where only quality changes do not yield a general law. Then, by symmetry, the laws for other quantified propositions are derived: particular affirmative contradicts universal negative, and particular negative contradicts universal affirmative. The passage culminates in a table listing contradictions for six types: singular affirmative, singular negative, universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, and particular negative. The key conditions for contradiction are: difference in quality (affirmation/negation) is always required; for quantified propositions (those with a quantifier), a difference in quantity (universal/particular) is also required. A footnote adds that for modal propositions, a difference in modality (e.g., necessary vs. possible) is also necessary. The examples used are drawn from animal and human, which are neutral but acceptable in Islamic logical discourse.

Laws of Contradiction for Quantified Propositions
Deriving the full set of contradiction laws for quantified propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the contradictory pairs for all four types of quantified categorical propositions (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative).

Core Idea
Contradiction in quantified propositions requires both opposite quality and opposite quantity.

After establishing the law for universal negatives, the author uses symmetry to derive the remaining laws: universal affirmative contradicts particular negative, particular affirmative contradicts universal negative, and particular negative contradicts universal affirmative. These are organized in a table. The key condition is that for any two propositions to be contradictories, they must differ in both quality (affirmation/negation) and quantity (universal/particular).

Guided Path
1
Recall the law for universal affirmative
From previous lesson, the contradictory of 'Every A is B' is 'Some A is not B' (particular negative). Write this down.
This is a direct application of the same rule.
2
Derive the law for particular affirmative
By symmetry, the contradictory of 'Some A is B' must be its opposite: 'No A is B' (universal negative). Check: if 'Some A is B' is false, then 'No A is B' is true, and vice versa.
Symmetry works because contradiction is mutual.
3
Derive the law for particular negative
The contradictory of 'Some A is not B' is 'Every A is B' (universal affirmative). Verify with an example: 'Some human is not a stone' (true) contradicts 'Every human is a stone' (false).
This completes the four quantified types.
4
State the condition for contradiction
From the table, we see that for quantified propositions, contradiction requires a difference in both quality and quantity. Changing only quality (e.g., from universal affirmative to universal negative) gives contrariety, not contradiction.
Two universals of opposite quality can both be false, so they are not contradictories.

Contradiction for Singular and Modal Propositions
Rules for contradiction of singular and modal propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the contradictory of singular (makhsusa) and modal propositions using the rules provided.

Core Idea
Singular propositions contradict by simply changing quality; modal propositions require a change in modality.

The passage also covers singular propositions (e.g., 'Zayd is standing') and includes a footnote on modal propositions. For singulars, contradiction is straightforward: the contradictory of 'A is B' is 'A is not B' (change quality only). For modals, the contradictory of a necessary proposition is a possible proposition, not another necessary. Understanding these nuances completes the picture of contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Identify singular propositions
Singular propositions refer to an individual, e.g., 'Zayd is standing'. They are not quantified, so the rule for contradiction is simply to negate the predicate: 'Zayd is not standing'.
There is no quantity to change.
2
State the two laws for singulars
From the table: The contradictory of a singular affirmative is a singular negative, and vice versa.
These are immediate and require only a change in quality.
3
Understand modal propositions
Modal propositions specify necessity, possibility, etc. The footnote says the contradictory of a necessary proposition (e.g., 'Human is necessarily an animal') is a possible proposition ('Human is possibly not an animal'), not another necessary proposition.
Similarly, permanent and absolute are modal opposites.
4
Apply the condition for modals
For contradiction, modal propositions must differ in modality. So two necessary propositions cannot be contradictories; they might be both true.
This is analogous to requiring a difference in quality and quantity for quantified propositions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[نقيض السالبة الكلية موجبة جزئية].
