لا شيء من (أ) هو (ب) نقيضها بعض (أ) هو (ب).
وأما الأمثلة التي يأتي فيها النقيض بتغيير الكيف فقط فلا يستخلص منها قانون تجريدي عام، يطبق على كل مثال، كما ذكرنا في نقيض الكلية الموجبة.
وباعتبار التناظر التام يظهر لنا : أن [نقيض الموجبة الجزئية سالبة كلية]، وأنّ [نقيض السالبة الجزئية موجبة كلية]، فتكون مجموعة القوانين لدينا في التناقض وفق الجدول التالي :
١	المخصوصة (الشخصية) الموجبة	نقيضها	مخصوصة سالبة
	(أ) هو (ب)	نقيضها	(أ) هو ليس (ب)
٢	المخصوصة (الشخصية) السالبة	نقيضها	مخصوصة موجبة
	(أ) هو ليس (ب)	نقيضها	(أ) هو (ب)
٣	الكلية الموجبة	نقيضها	جزئية سالبة
	كل (أ) هو (ب)	نقيضها	بعض (أ) ليس (ب)
٤	الكلية السالبة	نقيضها	جزئية موجبة
	لا شيء من (أ) هو (ب)	نقيضها	بعض (أ) هو (ب)
٥	الجزئية الموجبة	نقيضها	كلية سالبة
	بعض (أ) هو (ب)	نقيضها	لا شيء من (أ) هو (ب)
٦	الجزئية السالبة	نقيضها	كلية موجبة
	بعض (أ) ليس (ب)	نقيضها	كل (أ) هو (ب)
ومن هذا يتضح لنا أنه لا يحصل التناقض في القضايا ما لم يحصل فيها اختلاف في الكيف (السلب والإيجاب).
فإذا كانت محصورة (أي مسورة) وجب مع ذلك حصول اختلاف في الكم (الكلية والجزئية).
(١) وإذا كانت القضية موجهة وجب مع ذلك أيضاً حصول اختلاف في الجهة، فنقيض الضرورية هي الممكنة، ونقيض الدائمة هي المطلقة، والعكس بالعكس، فلا تناقض ما بين ضروريتين، أو ممكنتين، أو دائمتين، أو مطلقتين .
The reality is that we cannot do that, as evidenced by what we saw in the first example.
B. "No animal is a human" is a universal negative proposition (false). So what is its necessarily true contradictory?
If we say in its place: "Every animal is a human" — we preserved the quantity and only changed the quality — this proposition would also be false. So the two propositions do not differ in truth and falsity; one does not contradict the other, but rather both agree in falsehood.
Therefore: For a universal negative proposition, if we convert its negation to affirmation, it does not necessarily follow that we obtain its contradictory, as this example shows.
So let us do as we did with the contradictory of the universal affirmative: we change the universality to particularity, in addition to changing the quality from negation to affirmation. Then we obtain the following proposition: "Some animal is a human."
This proposition we have obtained is necessarily true, and it stands in opposition to our first false proposition ("No animal is a human") as a correct contradictory to it.
From this we can derive the following law:
[The contradictory of a universal negative is a particular affirmative.]
And in formal mathematical formulation:
"No A is B" — its contradictory is "Some A is B."
As for examples where the contradictory is produced by only changing the quality, no general abstract law applicable to every example can be derived from them, as we mentioned with the contradictory of the universal affirmative.
And by considering complete symmetry, it appears to us that [the contradictory of a particular affirmative is a universal negative], and that [the contradictory of a particular negative is a universal affirmative]. So the set of laws for contradiction is according to the following table:
1. Singular (particular) affirmative — its contradictory: singular negative
   (A is B) — its contradictory: (A is not B)
2. Singular (particular) negative — its contradictory: singular affirmative
   (A is not B) — its contradictory: (A is B)
3. Universal affirmative — its contradictory: particular negative
   Every A is B — its contradictory: Some A is not B
4. Universal negative — its contradictory: particular affirmative
   No A is B — its contradictory: Some A is B
5. Particular affirmative — its contradictory: universal negative
   Some A is B — its contradictory: No A is B
6. Particular negative — its contradictory: universal affirmative
   Some A is not B — its contradictory: Every A is B
From this it is clear that contradiction does not occur in propositions unless there is a difference in quality (negation and affirmation).
And if they are quantified (i.e., quantified), then along with that there must also be a difference in quantity (universality and particularity).
(1) And if the proposition is modal, then along with that there must also be a difference in modality: the contradictory of a necessary is a possible, and the contradictory of a permanent is an absolute, and vice versa. So there is no contradiction between two necessary propositions, or two possible, or two permanent, or two absolute.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage is an extended explanation of how to find the contradictory (naqīḍ) of categorical propositions. The author begins with an example: the universal negative 'No animal is a human' is false. Its contradictory must be true. Simply changing the quality to 'Every animal is a human' yields another false proposition, so that is not a contradictory. The author then combines a change in quality (negation to affirmation) with a change in quantity (universal to particular), obtaining 'Some animal is a human' which is true and thus a correct contradictory. From this, the law is stated: the contradictory of a universal negative is a particular affirmative. Symbolically: 'No A is B' has contradictory 'Some A is B'. The author notes that examples where only quality changes do not yield a general law. Then, by symmetry, the laws for other quantified propositions are derived: particular affirmative contradicts universal negative, and particular negative contradicts universal affirmative. The passage culminates in a table listing contradictions for six types: singular affirmative, singular negative, universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, and particular negative. The key conditions for contradiction are: difference in quality (affirmation/negation) is always required; for quantified propositions (those with a quantifier), a difference in quantity (universal/particular) is also required. A footnote adds that for modal propositions, a difference in modality (e.g., necessary vs. possible) is also necessary. The examples used are drawn from animal and human, which are neutral but acceptable in Islamic logical discourse.

موجهة
ضرورية
ممكنة

Key Takeaway
Singular propositions contradict by simply negating the predicate; modal propositions require a change in modality.

The Rule of Contradiction and the Table of Pairs
Author presents a table of contradictory pairs for personal, universal, and particular propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the contradictory of any categorical proposition by applying the rules of quality and quantity.

Core Idea
Contradictories always differ in quality; quantified ones also differ in quantity.

In Islamic logic, knowing the exact contradictory of a proposition is essential for constructing valid arguments and exposing falsehoods. The table presented by the author gives a clear, memorisable pattern for personal, universal, and particular propositions. Once you internalise the two conditions—difference in quality and, where applicable, difference in quantity—you can instantly determine whether two propositions are true contradictories. This skill is the foundation of immediate inference and is used daily in theological and juristic debates.

Guided Path
1
Examine the proposition's type
First, determine whether the proposition is personal (مخصوصة), universal (كلية), or particular (جزئية). Also note its quality: affirmative (موجبة) or negative (سالبة).
Personal propositions are about a single individual; they are treated as quantified but with quantity not applicable.
2
Apply the quality rule
No matter what, the contradictory must have the opposite quality. If the original is affirmative, its contradictory is negative, and vice versa.
3
Apply the quantity rule for quantified propositions
For universal and particular propositions, the quantity must also flip. A universal affirmative becomes a particular negative; a universal negative becomes a particular affirmative; a particular affirmative becomes a universal negative; a particular negative becomes a universal affirmative.
Personal propositions do not have quantity; only quality flips.
4
Verify with the table
Check the provided table to confirm your result. Each row gives the contradictory explicitly.

١	المخصوصة (الشخصية) الموجبة	نقيضها	مخصوصة سالبة
	(أ) هو (ب)	نقيضها	(أ) هو ليس (ب)
٢	المخصوصة (الشخصية) السالبة	نقيضها	مخصوصة موجبة
	(أ) هو ليس (ب)	نقيضها	(أ) هو (ب)
٣	الكلية الموجبة	نقيضها	جزئية سالبة
	كل (أ) هو (ب)	نقيضها	بعض (أ) ليس (ب)
٤	الكلية السالبة	نقيضها	جزئية موجبة
	لا شيء من (أ) هو (ب)	نقيضها	بعض (أ) هو (ب)

	بعض (أ) هو (ب)	نقيضها	لا شيء من (أ) هو (ب)

ومن هذا يتضح لنا أنه لا يحصل التناقض في القضايا ما لم يحصل فيها اختلاف في الكيف (السلب والإيجاب) . فإذا كانت محصورة (أي مسورة) وجب مع ذلك حصول اختلاف في الكم (الكلية والجزئية)(١).

The following table: [table with six rows]. From this it becomes clear to us that contradiction does not occur in propositions unless there is a difference in quality (negation and affirmation). If the proposition is quantified, there must also be a difference in quantity (universality and particularity). (1) And if the proposition is modal, there must also be a difference in modality: the contradictory of a necessary proposition is a possible proposition, and the contradictory of a permanent proposition is an absolute proposition, and vice versa. So there is no contradiction between two necessary, two possible, two permanent, or two absolute propositions.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage provides a compact table of contradictory pairs for six types of propositions: personal affirmative, personal negative, universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, and particular negative. The table shows that the contradictory of each is determined by flipping both quality and (if quantified) quantity. The author then explains the general rule: contradiction requires at least a difference in quality (one affirmative, one negative). For quantified propositions (i.e., universal or particular), a difference in quantity is also required. The footnote extends this to modal propositions, where the contradictory must differ in modality: the contradictory of a necessary proposition is a possible proposition, not another necessary one. For example, 'Every human is necessarily rational' and 'Some human is possibly not rational' are contradictories. This rule ensures that contradictory propositions cannot both be true or both false. The table is a standard tool used in Islamic logic for immediate inference.

Key Takeaway
The contradictory of a proposition is obtained by flipping its quality, and for quantified propositions also flipping its quantity.

Understanding Contradiction (Tanāquḍ): Conditions and Definition
Author lists what does not constitute contradiction and gives the formal definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether two propositions are contradictories by checking identity in subject, predicate, and all conditions, and by applying the formal definition of tanāquḍ.

Core Idea
Contradiction requires identity in all respects except quality and quantity.

In Islamic logic, contradiction (tanāquḍ) is a fundamental relation between propositions. Before giving the formal definition, logicians clarify what does NOT count as contradiction. Understanding these exclusions is essential to correctly apply the concept in theological and legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
List non-contradiction cases
Identify the five explicit categories the author lists where contradiction does not occur: two affirmatives, two negatives, two universals, two particulars, and cases where subject or predicate differ.
These are based on the four logical squares of opposition.
2
Understand subject and predicate identity
Note that if the subject or predicate differs between the two propositions, contradiction is dissolved. For example, 'Zayd is praying' and 'Amr is not praying' have different subjects, so no contradiction.
3
Examine ancillary conditions
The author lists many conditions that must be identical: time, place, state, instrument, object, potentiality/actuality, condition, part/whole, relation, cause, specification, etc. If any differs, no contradiction.
This ensures that the opposition is purely in quality and quantity.
4
Synthesize the formal definition
The definition combines all exclusions: contradiction is the difference of two propositions in affirmation and negation such that, by itself, it entails one true and one false.
The key is 'by itself'—the truth must follow from the logical form alone.
5
Apply to examples
Test pairs of propositions from Islamic sciences to see if they meet the conditions. For instance, 'Every intoxicant is haram' contradicts 'Some intoxicant is not haram'?
Check identity in subject, predicate, and all conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلا تناقض ما بين موجبتين.
ولا تناقض ما بين سالبتين.
ولا تناقض ما بين كليتين.
ولا تناقض ما بين جزئيتين.
ولا تناقض حين يختلف الموضوع أو المحمول في القضيتين، أو يختلف شيء من قيودهما، كاختلاف الزمان، أو المكان، أو الحال، أو الآلة، أو المفعول، أو القوة والفعل، أو الشرط، أو الجزء والكل، أو الإضافة، أو العلة، أو التمييز، أو غير ذلك مما ينحلّ به التناقض.
فتعريف التناقض بين القضيتين بالاستناد إلى التحليل السابق يكون على الوجه التالي:
هو اختلاف القضيتين بالإيجاب والسلب على وجه يقتضي لذاته أن تكون إحداهما صادقة والأخرى كاذبة.
والقضيتان المتناقضتان هما المختلفتان بالإيجاب والسلب على الوجه المذكور.
So there is no contradiction between two affirmative propositions. Nor is there contradiction between two negative propositions. Nor is there contradiction between two universal propositions. Nor is there contradiction between two particular propositions. Nor is there contradiction when the subject or predicate differs between the two propositions, or something from their conditions differs, such as difference in time, place, state, instrument, object, potentiality and actuality, condition, part and whole, relation, cause, specification, or other things that dissolve the contradiction. The definition of contradiction between two propositions based on the previous analysis is as follows: It is the difference of the two propositions in affirmation and negation in a way that necessarily entails by itself that one of them is true and the other false. And the two contradictory propositions are those that differ in affirmation and negation in the mentioned manner.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage first clarifies what does NOT produce contradiction. The author lists several cases: two affirmatives, two negatives, two universals, two particulars, and any case where the subject, predicate, or any condition (like time, place, etc.) differs. This is crucial because contradiction only occurs when all aspects except quality and quantity (if quantified) are identical. For example, 'Zayd is in the house' and 'Zayd is not in the house' are contradictories, but 'Zayd is in the house' and 'Amr is not in the house' are not, because the subject differs. Similarly, 'Water is boiling now' and 'Water is not boiling at noon' are not contradictories due to time difference. The author then gives the formal definition: contradiction is an opposition in affirmation and negation such that one must be true and the other false by virtue of the opposition itself. This definition is the standard in Islamic logic, emphasizing that the truth/falsity is inherent to the logical form, not dependent on external factors.

Key Terms
تناقض
قضية

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) 'Every sin is forbidden' and 'Some sin is not forbidden'

Key Takeaway
Contradiction requires identity in subject, predicate, and all conditions, and consists solely in opposing affirmation and negation such that truth and falsehood are necessitated by the logical form itself.

Rule 1: Coextensive Subject and Predicate
First rule on contradictory of universal with equal extension.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a universal proposition's subject and predicate are coextensive and construct its contradictory by changing only the quality.

Core Idea
When subject and predicate denote the same set, the contradictory is simply the universal negative.

The author begins his refinement by considering the simplest case: when the subject and predicate of a universal proposition have identical denotation (extension). In this case, the standard rule of contradictories (changing both quality and quantity) is unnecessarily complex. Instead, merely negating the quality yields a true contradictory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the denotation (maṣdaq) of subject and predicate
Determine the set of objects that the subject term refers to and the set that the predicate term refers to. Are they exactly the same, e.g., 'human' and 'rational'?
In Islamic theology, 'prophet' and 'sinless' (ma'sum) are often considered coextensive.
2
Check for coextension
If every individual that is the subject is also the predicate, and vice versa, then the terms are coextensive. For example, 'every human is rational' and 'every rational is human' are both true.
3
Apply the rule: change quality only
If coextensive, the contradictory of 'Every S is P' is 'No S is P'. Verify that the two cannot both be true or both false.
Example: 'Every human is rational' (true) and 'No human is rational' (false) are contradictories.
4
Practice with an Islamic example
Consider 'Every ʿālim (scholar) is one who knows (ʿārif)'. If these are coextensive in your school, what is its contradictory?
Answer: 'No ʿālim is one who knows' – which is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(١) تعليق عام على ما قرره المناطقة في موضوع التناقض بين المحصورات: نرى أن باستطاعتنا أن نوجد ضوابط نحافظ بها على ما أهملناه من تناقض بين القضايا المحصورة، لاستخراج قانون رياضي عام، وذلك بأن نراعي الضوابط التالية:
الأول: كل قضية كلية موضوعها ومحمولها متساويان في الماصدق، فنقيضها يكون بتبديل الكيف فقط دون الحاجة إلى تبديل الكم.
مثل: كل إنسان ناطق، لا شيء من الإنسان بناطق. والعكس. فالأولى صادقة والثانية كاذبة، فهما متناقضتان تماماً.
الثاني: كل قضية كلية موضوعها أخص من محمولها، فنقيضها يكون أيضاً بتبديل الكيف فقط دون الحاجة إلى تبديل الكم.
مثل: كل إنسان حيوان، لا شيء من الإنسان بحيوان. والعكس. فالأولى صادقة والثانية كاذبة، فهما متناقضتان تناقضاً تاماً.
الثالث: كل قضية كلية موضوعها أعم من محمولها أو بينهما عموم وخصوص من وجه، فنقيضها يكون بتبديل الكيف والكم معاً.
(1) A general comment on what the logicians have established on the topic of contradiction among quantified propositions: We see that we can create rules by which we preserve what we have neglected of contradiction among quantified propositions, in order to extract a general mathematical law, by observing the following rules: First: Every universal proposition whose subject and predicate are equal in denotation (extension), then its contradictory is by changing the quality only without needing to change the quantity. Example: 'Every human is rational', 'No human is rational', and vice versa. The first is true, the second false, so they are entirely contradictory. Second: Every universal proposition whose subject is more specific than its predicate, then its contradictory is also by changing the quality only without needing to change the quantity. Example: 'Every human is an animal', 'No human is an animal', and vice versa. The first is true, the second false, so they are completely contradictory. Third: Every universal proposition whose subject is more general than its predicate or there is a general-specific relation from one aspect, then its contradictory is by changing both quality and quantity together.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement by the author, who notes that the logicians' standard rule (that a universal's contradictory is always the particular with opposite quality) neglects important cases. The author proposes three rules based on the relation between subject and predicate in terms of denotation (extension). If the subject and predicate are coextensive (equal in extension), as in 'Every human is rational' (since rationality is the definition of human), then simply negating the quality gives a true contradictory: 'No human is rational' is false, and the pair cannot both be true or false. If the subject is narrower (e.g., 'Every human is an animal'), the same applies: 'No human is an animal' is false, so the pair is contradictory. However, if the subject is broader (e.g., 'Every animal is human') or there is a partial overlap (e.g., 'Every writer is a human' but not all humans are writers), then the simple negation 'No animal is human' would be false but the original 'Every animal is human' is also false, so they could both be false. Therefore, the correct contradictory is the particular: 'Some animal is not human', which differs in both quality and quantity. This refinement demonstrates a deeper understanding of the logical relations beyond the simple rules.

Key Terms
ماصدق

B) No scholar is knowledgeable.
C) Every scholar is not knowledgeable.
D) Some scholar is knowledgeable.

Key Takeaway
For a universal proposition with coextensive subject and predicate, the contradictory is formed by negating the quality only.

Rule 2: Subject Narrower than Predicate
Second rule on contradictory of universal with narrower subject.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the contradictory of a universal proposition when the subject is narrower in extension than the predicate.

Core Idea
When the subject is a subset of the predicate, the contradictory is still just the universal negative.

The second case covers universal propositions where the subject's denotation is narrower than the predicate's. For example, 'Every human is an animal' – humans are a subset of animals. Surprisingly, the same rule as the first case applies: change only the quality.

Guided Path
1
Compare extensions
Determine if every individual of the subject is included in the predicate, but not vice versa. Example: 'human' is narrower than 'animal'.
2
Apply the rule: change quality only
Despite the subject being narrower, the contradictory is still 'No S is P'. Verify: 'Every human is animal' is true, 'No human is animal' is false, so they contradict.
This works because the subject is a subset, so the universal affirmative is true, and its denial is false.
3
Check if simple negation works universally
Why does this rule hold? Because the universal affirmative is true, so its simple negation must be false. The pair cannot both be false.
This is different from the standard rule which would make the contradictory a particular negative.
4
Islamic example
Consider 'Every Muslim believes in Allah'. Since Muslims are a subset of believers (assuming all believers includes non-Muslims?), but if 'believer in Allah' is broader, then this rule applies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(١) تعليق عام على ما قرره المناطقة في موضوع التناقض بين المحصورات: نرى أن باستطاعتنا أن نوجد ضوابط نحافظ بها على ما أهملناه من تناقض بين القضايا المحصورة، لاستخراج قانون رياضي عام، وذلك بأن نراعي الضوابط التالية:
الأول: كل قضية كلية موضوعها ومحمولها متساويان في الماصدق، فنقيضها يكون بتبديل الكيف فقط دون الحاجة إلى تبديل الكم.
مثل: كل إنسان ناطق، لا شيء من الإنسان بناطق. والعكس. فالأولى صادقة والثانية كاذبة، فهما متناقضتان تماماً.
الثاني: كل قضية كلية موضوعها أخص من محمولها، فنقيضها يكون أيضاً بتبديل الكيف فقط دون الحاجة إلى تبديل الكم.
مثل: كل إنسان حيوان، لا شيء من الإنسان بحيوان. والعكس. فالأولى صادقة والثانية كاذبة، فهما متناقضتان تناقضاً تاماً.
الثالث: كل قضية كلية موضوعها أعم من محمولها أو بينهما عموم وخصوص من وجه، فنقيضها يكون بتبديل الكيف والكم معاً.
(1) A general comment on what the logicians have established on the topic of contradiction among quantified propositions: We see that we can create rules by which we preserve what we have neglected of contradiction among quantified propositions, in order to extract a general mathematical law, by observing the following rules: First: Every universal proposition whose subject and predicate are equal in denotation (extension), then its contradictory is by changing the quality only without needing to change the quantity. Example: 'Every human is rational', 'No human is rational', and vice versa. The first is true, the second false, so they are entirely contradictory. Second: Every universal proposition whose subject is more specific than its predicate, then its contradictory is also by changing the quality only without needing to change the quantity. Example: 'Every human is an animal', 'No human is an animal', and vice versa. The first is true, the second false, so they are completely contradictory. Third: Every universal proposition whose subject is more general than its predicate or there is a general-specific relation from one aspect, then its contradictory is by changing both quality and quantity together.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement by the author, who notes that the logicians' standard rule (that a universal's contradictory is always the particular with opposite quality) neglects important cases. The author proposes three rules based on the relation between subject and predicate in terms of denotation (extension). If the subject and predicate are coextensive (equal in extension), as in 'Every human is rational' (since rationality is the definition of human), then simply negating the quality gives a true contradictory: 'No human is rational' is false, and the pair cannot both be true or false. If the subject is narrower (e.g., 'Every human is an animal'), the same applies: 'No human is an animal' is false, so the pair is contradictory. However, if the subject is broader (e.g., 'Every animal is human') or there is a partial overlap (e.g., 'Every writer is a human' but not all humans are writers), then the simple negation 'No animal is human' would be false but the original 'Every animal is human' is also false, so they could both be false. Therefore, the correct contradictory is the particular: 'Some animal is not human', which differs in both quality and quantity. This refinement demonstrates a deeper understanding of the logical relations beyond the simple rules.

Key Terms
ماصدق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When subject is narrower than predicate, what is the contradictory of 'Every Muslim is a human'?
A) Some Muslim is not a human.
B) No Muslim is a human.
C) Every non-Muslim is a human.
D) No human is a Muslim.

Key Takeaway
When the subject is a subset of the predicate, the contradictory is still the universal negative.

Task: In fiqh, 'Every intoxicant is prohibited (haram)'. Assuming intoxicants are a subset of prohibited things, construct the contradictory using this lesson's rule.

Rule 3: Subject Broader or Mutual Overlap
Third rule on contradictory of universal with broader subject or partial overlap.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the contradictory of a universal proposition when the subject is broader in extension than the predicate or there is a partial overlap.

Core Idea
When the subject includes the predicate or they partially overlap, the contradictory requires changing both quality and quantity.

The third case covers universal propositions where the subject's extension is broader than the predicate's, or they have partial overlap. In these situations, simply negating the quality does not yield a contradictory; the resulting pair could both be false. The correct contradictory is the particular negative, affecting both quality and quantity.

Guided Path
1
Recognize broader or overlapping extension
Determine if the subject term includes the predicate term (e.g., 'animal' is broader than 'human'), or there is mutual overlap (e.g., 'writer' and 'human': not all writers are human? Actually all writers are human, but not all humans are writers – that's broader subject? Wait, 'writer' is narrower. The author says subject is broader: 'Every animal is human' – subject broader. Also, mutual overlap: 'Every writer is human' but subject=writer, predicate=human, writer is narrower. Actually the third case says 'subject is more general than its predicate' or 'general-specific from one aspect' (عموم وخصوص من وجه). Need to clarify. The example in the passage: 'Every writer is human' – writer is narrower. But the author says subject is broader? Let's check: 'Every animal is human' – subject broader. And 'Every writer is human' – subject narrower, but that doesn't fit. Actually the third rule says: 'Every universal proposition whose subject is more general than its predicate or there is a general-specific relation from one aspect' – then contradictory by changing both quality and quantity. So for subject broader: 'Every animal is human' – false. Its contradictory should be 'Some animal is not human' (true). That works. For mutual overlap: example 'Every writer is human' – but writer is actually narrower. Maybe 'Every human is writer' is false because not all humans are writers. That's subject broader? No, subject=human is broader than writer. So 'Every human is writer' is false, its contradictory 'Some human is not writer' is true. So that's the case. So the third rule handles two subcases: subject broader, and where there is partial overlap (like 'writer' and 'human'? Actually writer and human have inclusion: all writers are human, not all humans are writers – that's one-way inclusion. The phrase 'general-specific from one aspect' might mean a relation like 'educated human' and 'human' – that's inclusion. I think mutual overlap means two terms where neither is completely included in the other, e.g., 'writer' and 'scholar' – some writers are scholars, some are not, some scholars are writers, some are not. That is عموم وخصوص من وجه. Example: 'Every writer is a scholar' is false, its contradictory 'Some writer is not scholar' is true.
In the source, the example for mutual overlap is not given, but the author says it follows the same rule as broader subject.
2
Check that simple negation fails
If subject is broader, 'Every animal is human' is false, but 'No animal is human' is also false (since some animals are human). So they are not contradictories. Thus both quality and quantity must change.
3
Apply the rule: change both quality and quantity to particular negative
The contradictory of 'Every S is P' in this case is 'Some S is not P'.
Example: 'Every animal is human' (false) and 'Some animal is not human' (true) are contradictions.
4
Islamic example
Consider 'Every scholar (ʿālim) is a faqīh (jurist)'. Since not all scholars are jurists, the subject (scholar) is broader? Actually scholar is broader than jurist. So the contradictory is 'Some scholar is not a faqīh'.

Rules for Contradictory of Universal Propositions
Author presents four rules for universal contradictory based on term extension relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the correct contradictory of a universal categorical proposition based on the relation between its subject and predicate.

Core Idea
Contradiction of universals depends on term extension, not just flipping quality.

In this passage, the author expands on the standard logical definition of contradiction by providing a set of rules that account for the different ways subject and predicate terms can relate in extension. These rules are essential for correctly identifying contradictory pairs in scientific and theological arguments, ensuring that one is true and the other false.

Guided Path
1
Identify the relation between subject and predicate extension
Determine if subject and predicate are equal, subject more specific, subject more general, partial overlap, or disjoint/opposed/contradictory.
Use the terms: equality (tasāwuq), more specific (akhaṣṣ), more general (a‘amm), overlap (min wajh), disjunction (mubāyana), opposition (taḍādd), contradiction (tanāquḍ).
2
Apply the appropriate rule
Based on the relation, decide whether to change only quality or both quality and quantity. For equal, subject more specific, or disjoint/opposed/contradictory: change quality only. For subject more general or partial overlap: change both quality and quantity.
The rule for disjoint/opposed/contradictory is explicitly given as rule four.
3
Check the resulting pair
Verify that the two propositions are opposite in truth value: one true, one false.
The examples in the text demonstrate this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعليق عام على ما قرره المناطقة في موضوع التناقض بين المحصورات: نرى أن باستطاعتنا أن نوجد ضوابط نحافظ بها على ما أهملناه من تناقض بين القضايا المحصورة، لاستخراج قانون رياضي عام، وذلك بأن نراعي الضوابط التالية:
الأول: كل قضية كلية موضوعها ومحمولها متساويان في الماصدق، فنقيضها يكون بتبديل الكيف فقط دون الحاجة إلى تبديل الكم.
مثل: كل إنسان ناطق، لا شيء من الإنسان بناطق. والعكس. فالأولى صادقة والثانية كاذبة، فهما متناقضتان تماماً.
الثاني: كل قضية كلية موضوعها أخص من محمولها، فنقيضها يكون أيضاً بتبديل الكيف فقط دون الحاجة إلى تبديل الكم.
مثل: كل إنسان حيوان، لا شيء من الإنسان بحيوان. والعكس. فالأولى صادقة والثانية كاذبة، فهما متناقضتان تناقضاً تاماً.
الثالث: كل قضية كلية موضوعها أعم من محمولها أو بينهما عموم وخصوص من وجه، فنقيضها يكون بتبديل الكيف والكم معاً.
مثل: بعض الحيوان ليس بإنسان، كل حيوان إنسان، فالأولى صادقة والثانية كاذبة.
ومثل: كل تفاح حامض، بعض التفاح ليس حامضاً فالأولى كاذبة والثانية صادقة.
الرابع: كل قضية كلية موضوعها مباين لمحمولها أو مضاد له أو مناقض، فنقيضها يكون بتبديل الكيف فقط دون الحاجة إلى تبديل الكم.
مثل: كل إنسان فرس ـ لا شيء من الإنسان بفرس (مباين).
مثل: كل أبيض أسود ـ لا شيء من الأبيض بأسود (مضاد).
مثل: كل موجود معدوم ـ لا شيء مما هو موجود بمعدوم (مناقض).
فالأوليات كاذبات، والأخريات صادقات، وهي نقائض صواحبها.
الخامس: أما القضايا الجزئية فلا تنقض إلا بكلية مع تبديل الكيف، لاحتمال عدم تطابق البعضين على ما صدق واحد، فيصدقان معاً أو يكذبان معاً.
فنقول: بعض أهل مكة هاجر إلى المدينة ـ بعض أهل مكة لم يهاجر إلى المدينة، كلاهما صادقتان، ولكن ما صدق موضوع الأولى غير ما صدق موضوع الثانية.
A general comment on what the logicians have established on the subject of contradiction between categorical propositions: we see that we can establish rules by which we preserve what we have neglected of contradiction among categorical propositions, to extract a general mathematical law, by observing the following rules:
First: Every universal proposition whose subject and predicate are equal in extension, then its contradictory is by changing the quality only without needing to change the quantity. Example: 'Every human is rational', 'No human is rational'. And the reverse. The first is true and the second false, so they are completely contradictory.
Second: Every universal proposition whose subject is more specific than its predicate, then its contradictory is also by changing the quality only without needing to change the quantity. Example: 'Every human is an animal', 'No human is an animal'. And the reverse. The first is true and the second false, so they are completely contradictory.
Third: Every universal proposition whose subject is more general than its predicate, or between them there is generality and specificity from some aspect, then its contradictory is by changing both quality and quantity together. Example: 'Some animal is not human', 'Every animal is human'; the first is true and the second false. And example: 'Every apple is sour', 'Some apple is not sour'; the first is false and the second true.
Fourth: Every universal proposition whose subject is disjoined from its predicate, or opposite to it, or contradictory to it, then its contradictory is by changing the quality only without needing to change the quantity. Example: 'Every human is a horse' — 'No human is a horse' (disjoined). Example: 'Every white is black' — 'No white is black' (opposite). Example: 'Every existent is non-existent' — 'No existent is non-existent' (contradictory). The first ones are false, and the last ones are true, and they are contradictories of their companions.
Fifth: As for particular propositions, they are not contradicted except by a universal with change of quality, due to the possibility of the two parts not matching the same referent, so they may both be true or both false. So we say: 'Some people of Mecca migrated to Medina' — 'Some people of Mecca did not migrate to Medina', both are true, but the referent of the subject of the first is not the referent of the subject of the second.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage is a commentary expanding the definition of contradiction by providing practical rules for finding the contradictory of categorical propositions (qadaya muhsara). The author notes that logicians have neglected some cases, and these rules aim to provide a complete mathematical law. The rules are based on the relation between subject and predicate extension (tasāwuq, akhaṣṣ, a‘amm, mubāyin, etc.). For universals, if subject and predicate are equal or subject is more specific or they are disjoined/opposed/contradictory, the contradictory is simply change quality (affirmative to negative universal). If subject is more general or they have partial overlap, change both quality and quantity (universal affirmative becomes particular negative). For particulars, the contradictory must be a universal with changed quality because two particulars can be simultaneously true if they refer to different subsets. Examples from everyday categories illustrate each case.

According to the fourth rule, what is the contradictory of 'Every white is black'?
A) 'Some white is not black'
B) 'No white is black'
C) 'Every white is not black' (i.e., 'No white is black' is the same)
D) 'Some black is white'

Key Takeaway
To find the contradictory of a universal proposition, first determine the extension relation between subject and predicate, then apply the corresponding rule: if equal, subject more specific, or disjoint/opposed/contradictory, change quality only; if subject more general or partial overlap, change both quality and quantity.

Rule for Contradictory of Particular Propositions
Author states that particulars are only contradicted by universals with changed quality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to correctly identify the contradictory of a particular categorical proposition and explain why two particulars cannot be contradictories.

Core Idea
A particular proposition is only contradicted by a universal of opposite quality, not another particular.

The author concludes the rules by addressing particular propositions, which require a different treatment. Because two particular propositions can both be true if they refer to different subsets of the subject, they cannot be contradictories. The only proper contradictory for a particular is a universal of opposite quality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition as particular
Note the quantity: 'some' (ba‘ḍ).
2
Recall that two particulars can both be true
The author gives an example: 'Some people of Mecca migrated to Medina' and 'Some people of Mecca did not migrate to Medina' are both true because they refer to different individuals.
This violates the condition of contradiction that they cannot both be true.
3
Apply the rule: contradict with a universal of opposite quality
For 'Some A is B', the contradictory is 'No A is B'. For 'Some A is not B', the contradictory is 'Every A is B'.
This ensures exactly one is true and one false.

A general comment on what the logicians have established on the subject of contradiction between categorical propositions: we see that we can establish rules by which we preserve what we have neglected of contradiction among categorical propositions, to extract a general mathematical law, by observing the following rules:
First: Every universal proposition whose subject and predicate are equal in extension, then its contradictory is by changing the quality only without needing to change the quantity. Example: 'Every human is rational', 'No human is rational'. And the reverse. The first is true and the second false, so they are completely contradictory.
Second: Every universal proposition whose subject is more specific than its predicate, then its contradictory is also by changing the quality only without needing to change the quantity. Example: 'Every human is an animal', 'No human is an animal'. And the reverse. The first is true and the second false, so they are completely contradictory.
Third: Every universal proposition whose subject is more general than its predicate, or between them there is generality and specificity from some aspect, then its contradictory is by changing both quality and quantity together. Example: 'Some animal is not human', 'Every animal is human'; the first is true and the second false. And example: 'Every apple is sour', 'Some apple is not sour'; the first is false and the second true.
Fourth: Every universal proposition whose subject is disjoined from its predicate, or opposite to it, or contradictory to it, then its contradictory is by changing the quality only without needing to change the quantity. Example: 'Every human is a horse' — 'No human is a horse' (disjoined). Example: 'Every white is black' — 'No white is black' (opposite). Example: 'Every existent is non-existent' — 'No existent is non-existent' (contradictory). The first ones are false, and the last ones are true, and they are contradictories of their companions.
Fifth: As for particular propositions, they are not contradicted except by a universal with change of quality, due to the possibility of the two parts not matching the same referent, so they may both be true or both false. So we say: 'Some people of Mecca migrated to Medina' — 'Some people of Mecca did not migrate to Medina', both are true, but the referent of the subject of the first is not the referent of the subject of the second.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage is a commentary expanding the definition of contradiction by providing practical rules for finding the contradictory of categorical propositions (qadaya muhsara). The author notes that logicians have neglected some cases, and these rules aim to provide a complete mathematical law. The rules are based on the relation between subject and predicate extension (tasāwuq, akhaṣṣ, a‘amm, mubāyin, etc.). For universals, if subject and predicate are equal or subject is more specific or they are disjoined/opposed/contradictory, the contradictory is simply change quality (affirmative to negative universal). If subject is more general or they have partial overlap, change both quality and quantity (universal affirmative becomes particular negative). For particulars, the contradictory must be a universal with changed quality because two particulars can be simultaneously true if they refer to different subsets. Examples from everyday categories illustrate each case.

كلية
نقيض
كيف

The contradictory of a particular proposition must be a universal of opposite quality; two particulars cannot be contradictories because they can both be true.

Contrariety between Universal Affirmative and Universal Negative
Author defines contrariety between universal propositions with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify contrariety between universal affirmative and universal negative propositions and determine their truth-value relationship using the rule that they cannot both be true but may both be false.

Core Idea
Universal affirmative and universal negative are contraries: they cannot both be true, but can both be false.

In Islamic logic, understanding the relationships between propositions is essential for valid reasoning. The author explains one key relation: contrariety (التقابل بالتضاد) between universal affirmative and universal negative. Using an analogy of 'ladders' of opposites, he shows why falsehood of one does not imply truth of the other, and vice versa. This lesson will help you identify and apply this rule in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the ladder analogy
The author uses 'ladders' of opposites (e.g., colors, magnitudes, grammatical categories) to illustrate that in a group of mutually exclusive alternatives, if one is false, it does not force any other to be true, but if one is true, all others are false.
This analogy sets the groundwork for understanding contrariety between propositions.
2
Identify the two proposition types
A universal affirmative (كليّة موجبة) asserts that every S is P, e.g., 'Every human is an animal'. A universal negative (كليّة سالبة) asserts that no S is P, e.g., 'No human is an animal'.
They must have the same subject and predicate under the same conditions.
3
Examine the examples
Look at examples (a)-(d). In (a) and (b), when one is true, the other is false. In (c) and (d), both are false (e.g., 'Every apple is sour' and 'No apple is sour' are both false).
Notice that falsehood of one does not guarantee truth of the other.
4
Extract the rule
From the examples, deduce that contrariety means the two propositions cannot both be true but can both be false. Confirm with the author's concluding statement.
This is the defining characteristic of contrariety (تضاد).
5
Apply to Islamic sciences
Think of a real Islamic example: In Fiqh, the universal affirmative 'Every intoxicant is haram' and the universal negative 'No intoxicant is haram' are contraries. They cannot both be true, but both could be false if some intoxicants are not haram (e.g., permissible substances that are intoxicating in large quantities).
This tool is crucial for evaluating theological and juristic arguments.

فإذا كذب في سلم أصول الألوان كون الشيء أحمر، فلا يقتضي ذلك أن يصدق كونه أخضر، لاحتمال أن يكون على خلافهما جميعاً، فيكون أزرق مثلاً أو لوناً آخر.
وإذا كذب كون هذا أكبر من ذاك، فلا يقتضي أن يكون أصغر منه، لاحتمال أن يكون لا أصغر ولا أكبر، بل ربما كان مساوياً له.

والتضاد يقتضي أن تكذب إحدى القضيتين المتقابلتين إذا صدقت الأخرى، ولكن لا يقتضي أن تصدق إحداهما إذا كذبت الأخرى.
Every single one in these ladders is the contradictory of all others in the same ladder if they are multiple, but it is the contrary of each individual. So if in the ladder of primary colors the statement 'the thing is red' is false, that does not require that it be true that it is green, because it is possible that it is opposite to both, such as being blue or another color. And if the statement 'this is greater than that' is false, it does not require that it be smaller than it, because it is possible that it is neither smaller nor greater, but perhaps equal to it. And if the statement that the word 'la' is a noun is false, it does not require that it be a verb, because it is possible that it is opposite to both, so it is a particle, which is the case, and so on. But whenever one of the contraries is true, then all other contraries in its ladder are necessarily false, because two contraries cannot be together in truth, although they may be together in falsehood. After this explanatory introduction, we say: The opposition of contrariety in quantified propositions is between the universal affirmative and the universal negative, with the subject and predicate being the same in all their constraints, conditions, and attachments, as previously explained in the rules of opposition. And conversely, i.e., between the universal negative and the universal affirmative. Examples: Proposition: a - Every human is an animal (true) - No human is an animal (false); universal affirmative - universal negative. b - No human is a stone (true) - Every human is a stone (false); universal negative - universal affirmative. c - Every apple is sour (false) - No apple is sour (false); universal affirmative - universal negative. d - No color is black (false) - Every color is black (false); universal negative - universal affirmative. From these examples we note that whenever one of the two opposing propositions is true, the other that opposes it is necessarily false. But falsehood of one does not require the truth of the other, because in examples (c) and (d) we note that the two opposing propositions in each are both false. Therefore we deduce the following rule: [The opposition between the universal affirmative and the universal negative is a type of contrariety opposition]. And contrariety requires that one of the two opposing propositions be false if the other is true, but it does not require that one be true if the other is false.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage defines the logical relationship of contrariety (تضاد) as it applies specifically to universal affirmative and universal negative propositions. The author begins with an illustrative analogy using 'ladders' (سلالم) of opposites, such as colors, magnitudes, and grammatical categories. In a ladder of multiple options (e.g., red, green, blue), each item is a 'contradictory' (نقيض) of all others collectively, but a 'contrary' (ضد) of each individually. The key point: if one option is false, that does not force any other to be true, because the reality may be opposite to both (e.g., neither red nor green but blue). However, if one is true, then all others in that ladder must be false because two contraries cannot be true simultaneously, though they may both be false. Applying this to propositions, the author states that the contrariety opposition exists between the universal affirmative (e.g., 'Every human is an animal') and the universal negative ('No human is an animal'), provided the subject and predicate are identical in all respects. The author then presents four examples: Example (a): true universal affirmative vs. false universal negative. Example (b): true universal negative vs. false universal affirmative. These illustrate that when one is true, the other is false. Examples (c) and (d): both propositions are false (e.g., 'Every apple is sour' and 'No apple is sour' are both false because some apples are sour and some are sweet). This shows that falsehood of one does not entail truth of the other; they can both be false. The conclusion: the opposition between universal affirmative and universal negative is contrariety (تضاد), characterized by the rule that they cannot both be true but can both be false.

Contrariety of Universal Affirmative and Universal Negative
Author explains contrariety with examples and derives the logical law.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when two universal propositions are in a relation of contrariety and explain why both can be false.

Core Idea
Universal affirmative and negative cannot both be true, but can both be false.

In logic, propositions can oppose each other in various ways. One common type is contrariety (tadadd). This passage uses examples from everyday objects—apples and colors—to illustrate the relation between universal affirmative and universal negative statements. By examining when both are false, you will grasp the key feature of contrariety and how it differs from other opposition relations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the universal propositions
In each example, locate the universal affirmative (e.g., 'Every apple is sour') and universal negative (e.g., 'No apple is sour'). Note that they share the same subject and predicate.
2
Determine their truth values
Check the examples: both statements in C are false (some apples are sour, some not), and both in D are false (some colors are black, some not). Observe that neither example has one true and one false.
3
Derive the rule of contrariety
From the examples, note that if one were true, the other would be false (e.g., if 'Every apple is sour' were true, then 'No apple is sour' would be false). But the falsity of one does not force the truth of the other – both can be false. This is the essence of tadadd.
4
Define contrariety precisely
Contrariety between universal affirmative and universal negative means: they cannot both be true, but they can both be false. This distinguishes it from contradiction, where one must be true and the other false.

This passage explains the relation of contrariety (tadadd) between universal affirmative and universal negative propositions. The author provides two examples. In example D, 'No color is black' and 'Every color is black' are both false because some colors are black and some are not. From these, the author derives the law: contrariety means that if one is true, the other is necessarily false, but if one is false, the other may be false as well. This is the key property of contrariety, distinguishing it from other opposition relations. The examples use familiar objects (apples, colors) to illustrate that both can be false, which is the unique feature of contrariety.

Understanding Subcontrariety of Particular Propositions
Author defines subcontrariety between particular affirmative and particular negative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish subcontrariety from contrariety and identify subcontrary proposition pairs in Islamic logical contexts.

Core Idea
Subcontrariety: both particular propositions can be true, but never both false.

In the previous lesson, you learned about contrariety, where two universal propositions cannot both be true. Now we examine the opposite relation: subcontrariety, which applies to particular propositions. The key insight is that while falsity of one forces truth of the other, truth of one does not force falsity of the other. This distinction is crucial for constructing valid arguments in Islamic jurisprudence and theology.

Guided Path
1
Recall the square of opposition
Remember that for quantified propositions, there are four types: universal affirmative (A), universal negative (E), particular affirmative (I), and particular negative (O). Subcontrariety is the relation between I and O.
This is the same classification used in classical logic.
2
Define subcontrariety precisely
According to the passage, subcontrariety means: if one of the two particular propositions is false, the other must be true. However, if one is true, the other may be true or false. This is the opposite of contrariety (where if one is true, the other is false).
3
Analyze the examples
Look at the three examples given: (a) 'Some human is animal' (true) and 'Some human is not animal' (false) — one true, one false. (b) 'Some animal is human' (true) and 'Some animal is not human' (true) — both true, showing truth of one does not imply falsity. (c) 'Not some fruit is a stone' (true) and 'Some fruit is a stone' (false) — again one true, one false.
Example (c) uses a negative subject, which the author treats as a particular affirmative.
4
Contrast with contrariety
In contrariety, both universals cannot be true but both can be false. In subcontrariety, both particulars cannot be false but both can be true. This inverse relationship is why the author says subcontrariety is 'subsumed under contrariety'.
Think of it as the mirror image: contrariety = not both true; subcontrariety = not both false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدخول تحت التضاد
في القضايا المحصورة
المراد (بالدخول تحت التضاد) في القضايا المتقابلة المحصورة، أن كذب إحدى القضيتين المتقابلتين يقتضي صدق الأخرى حتماً، لكن صدق إحدى القضيتين لا يقتضي كذب الأخرى، لاحتمال أن يصدقا جميعاً.
فهو بهذا عكس الضابط الذي ظهر لنا في التضاد، وهو في حقيقته داخل تحت معنى التضاد، ولذلك اختير له هذا العنوان.
وهو يكون في التقابل بين القضيتين الجزئية الموجبة، والجزئية السالبة، مع اتحاد الموضوع والمحمول، كما سبق في قواعد التقابل وضوابطه، والتبديل هنا لم يحصل إلا في الكيف.
الأمثلة :
القضية مقابلتها
جزئية موجبة جزئية سالبة
أ ـ بعض الإنسان حيوان (صادقة) بعض الإنسان ليس بحيوان (كاذبة)
ب ـ بعض الحيوان إنسان (صادقة) بعض الحيوان ليس بإنسان (صادقة)
جـ ـ ليس بعض الفاكهة بحجر (صادقة) بعض الفاكهة حجر (كاذبة)
Subsumption under Contrariety (Dukhul Taht al-Tadadd) In the quantified propositions What is meant by 'subsumption under contrariety' for opposite quantified propositions is that the falsity of one of the two opposite propositions necessarily implies the truth of the other, but the truth of one of the two propositions does not imply the falsity of the other, because it is possible that both are true. Thus it is the inverse of the rule that appeared to us regarding contrariety, and it is in reality subsumed under the meaning of contrariety, so this title was chosen for it. And it occurs in the opposition between the particular affirmative and the particular negative propositions, with unification of subject and predicate, as previously in the rules and controls of opposition, and the change here occurs only in quality. Examples: Proposition & Its Opposite Particular affirmative & Particular negative A - Some human is an animal (true) & Some human is not an animal (false) B - Some animal is a human (true) & Some animal is not a human (true) C - Not some fruit is a stone (true) & Some fruit is a stone (false)
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage defines 'subsumption under contrariety' (al-dukhul taht al-tadadd), which is the relation between particular affirmative and particular negative propositions. The author explains that in this relation, if one is false, the other must be true; but if one is true, the other may be true as well. This is the opposite of contrariety, where if one is true the other is false, but both can be false. The author gives three examples: A) 'Some human is animal' (true) and 'Some human is not animal' (false) — here one is true, the other false; B) 'Some animal is human' (true) and 'Some animal is not human' (true) — here both are true, showing that truth of one does not imply falsity of the other; C) 'Not some fruit is a stone' (true) and 'Some fruit is a stone' (false) — again one true, one false. The author notes that the subject and predicate are the same in each pair, and only the quality (affirmation/negation) differs. The title 'subsumption under contrariety' indicates it is a branch of the broader concept of opposition, but with opposite logical properties.

جزئية موجبة
جزئية سالبة

Key Takeaway
Subcontrariety is the logical relation between particular affirmative and particular negative propositions: they cannot both be false, but they can both be true.

الدخول تحت التضاد: Sub-Contrary Opposition for Particular Propositions
Defines the logical relation of sub-contrary opposition with examples and rule.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the logical relation of sub-contrary opposition (الدخول تحت التضاد) from strict contradiction (التضاد) and determine the truth-conditions for pairs of particular affirmative and negative propositions.

Core Idea
Falsity of one sub-contrary forces truth of the other, but both can be true.

In the study of opposition among quantified propositions, the relation between particular affirmative and particular negative statements follows a distinct pattern: if one is false, the other must be true; but if one is true, the other may be true or false. This relation, called 'falling under contradiction' (الدخول تحت التضاد), is the opposite of strict contradiction. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly interpreting logical relations in Islamic legal and theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defined relation
Find the definition of الدخول تحت التضاد in the Arabic text. Note the key difference from التضاد (strict contradiction).
2
Extract truth-conditions
From the definition, derive two conditional statements: (1) falsity of one implies truth of the other; (2) truth of one does not imply falsity of the other.
Contrast with التضاد where truth implies falsity.
3
Examine the examples
Check each example (A, B, C) and verify that the truth-conditions hold. Pay special attention to example (B) where both propositions are true.
Example (B): 'Some animal is human' (true) and 'Some animal is not human' (true) – both can be true.
4
Contrast with strict contradiction
Recall that in التضاد (contradiction), truth of one implies falsity of the other. Here, truth does not imply falsity.
The text explicitly says 'فهو بهذا عكس الضابط الذي ظهر لنا في التضاد' (Thus this is the opposite of the rule in contradiction).
5
Formulate the general law
State the rule: The opposition between particular affirmative and particular negative is of the type الدخول تحت التضاد.
Summarize: falsity → truth; truth → may be true or false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدخول تحت التضاد
في القضايا المحصورة
المراد (بالدخول تحت التضاد) في القضايا المتقابلة المحصورة، أن كذب إحدى القضيتين المتقابلتين يقتضي صدق الأخرى حتماً، لكن صدق إحدى القضيتين لا يقتضي كذب الأخرى، لاحتمال أن يصدقا جميعاً.
فهو بهذا عكس الضابط الذي ظهر لنا في التضاد، وهو في حقيقته داخل تحت معنى التضاد، ولذلك اختير له هذا العنوان.
وهو يكون في التقابل بين القضيتين الجزئية الموجبة، والجزئية السالبة، مع اتحاد الموضوع والمحمول، كما سبق في قواعد التقابل وضوابطه، والتبديل هنا لم يحصل إلا في الكيف.
الأمثلة :
القضية مقابلتها
جزئية موجبة جزئية سالبة
أ ـ بعض الإنسان حيوان (صادقة) بعض الإنسان ليس بحيوان (كاذبة)
ب ـ بعض الحيوان إنسان (صادقة) بعض الحيوان ليس بإنسان (صادقة)
جـ ـ ليس بعض الفاكهة بحجر (صادقة) بعض الفاكهة حجر (كاذبة)
من هذه الأمثلة نلاحظ أنه متى كذبت إحدى القضيتين المتقابلتين فيها
صدقت الأخرى التي تقابلها.
ولكن لا يقتضي صدق إحداهما كذب الأخرى، لاحتمال صدقهما معاً
كما في المثال (ب) فالقضيتان المتقابلتان فيه صادقتان كما هو ظاهر.
فالحال هنا عكس الحال في التضاد.
إذاً نستخلص القانون التالي:
[التقابل بين الجزئية الموجبة والجزئية السالبة هو من قبيل الدخول
تحت التضاد].
والدخول تحت التضاد يقتضي أن تصدق إحدى القضيتين المتقابلتين
إذا كذبت الأخرى، ولكن لا يقتضي أن تكذب إحداهما إذا صدقت الأخرى،
بل قد تصدقان معاً.
Falling under contradiction in quantified propositions. What is meant by (falling under contradiction) in opposed quantified propositions is that the falsity of one of the two opposed propositions necessarily implies the truth of the other, but the truth of one of the two propositions does not imply the falsity of the other, because of the possibility that both may be true. Thus this is the opposite of the rule that appeared to us in contradiction [proper], and in reality it is included under the meaning of contradiction, and for this reason this title was chosen for it. It occurs in the opposition between the particular affirmative and the particular negative, with unity of subject and predicate, as preceded in the rules and controls of opposition, and the change here only occurred in quality. Examples: (A) Some human is animal (true) – Some human is not animal (false). (B) Some animal is human (true) – Some animal is not human (true). (C) Not some fruit is stone (true) – Some fruit is stone (false). From these examples we observe that whenever one of the two opposed propositions is false, the other opposing it becomes true. But the truth of one of them does not necessitate the falsity of the other, because of the possibility of both being true together, as in example (B), for the two opposed propositions in it are both true as is apparent. So the situation here is the opposite of the situation in contradiction [proper]. Therefore we extract the following law: The opposition between the particular affirmative and the particular negative is of the type of falling under contradiction. And falling under contradiction necessitates that one of the two opposed propositions is true if the other is false, but it does not necessitate that one of them is false if the other is true; rather, both may be true together.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage defines a logical relation called 'الدخول تحت التضاد' (falling under contradiction), which is the opposite of the standard contradiction (التضاد) discussed earlier. The relation applies specifically to the pair of particular affirmative and particular negative propositions that share the same subject and predicate. The key rule: If one of these propositions is false, the other must be true. However, if one is true, the other may be either true or false; they can both be true. This contrasts with strict contradiction (التضاد), where truth of one implies falsity of the other and falsity implies truth. The text provides three examples using 'some human is animal' etc. to illustrate. Example (B) shows both particular affirmative and negative can be true simultaneously. Thus the law is stated: The opposition between particular affirmative and particular negative is of the type 'falling under contradiction'. This is a standard part of the square of opposition, where sub-contraries (contradictories of universals) behave exactly as described.

Key Terms
الدخول تحت التضاد (al-dukhūl taḥta al-taḍād): A logical relation where the falsity of one proposition implies the truth of the other, but truth does not imply falsity; both can be true. [Note: In later logic works, this is often called 'sub-contrary' (تحت التضاد) or 'contradictory in a broad sense'.]
التضاد (al-taḍād): In this context, the relation where truth of one implies falsity of the other, and falsity implies truth (strict contradiction, not contraries as in later usage). [Note: In classical Arabic logic, التضاد often means 'contrary' (e.g., for universal propositions); here it is used for 'contradictory'.]
القضايا المحصورة (al-qaḍāyā al-maḥṣūra): Quantified propositions, i.e., those with universal or particular quantifiers.
جزئية موجبة (juz'iyya mūjiba): A particular affirmative proposition, e.g., 'Some S is P'.
جزئية سالبة (juz'iyya sālba): A particular negative proposition, e.g., 'Some S is not P'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The opposition between particular affirmative and particular negative is a sub-contrary relation: falsity of one implies truth of the other, but both can be true.

Understanding Overlap: Universal and Particular Propositions
Author defines the logical relation of overlap between universal and particular propositions of the same quality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the truth value of a particular proposition based on the truth value of its universal counterpart using the overlap relation.

Core Idea
Overlap: If the universal is true, the particular is true; but not vice versa.

In Islamic logic, the relation between universal and particular propositions of the same quality is called 'overlap' (tadakhul). This relation describes how the truth or falsity of one proposition affects the other. Understanding this is essential for constructing valid syllogisms and avoiding fallacies in theological and juristic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the universal and particular pair
Find two propositions that differ only in quantity: one universal (e.g., 'Every A is B') and one particular (e.g., 'Some A is B'), with the same quality (both affirmative or both negative).
The quality must match; if one is affirmative and the other negative, it is not overlap.
2
Apply the universal truth condition
If the universal is true, the particular must be true. For example, if 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' is true, then 'Some intoxicant is forbidden' is also true.
This holds because the particular is a subset of the universal.
3
Apply the particular falsity condition
If the particular is false, the universal must be false. For example, if 'Some intoxicant is not forbidden' is false, then 'No intoxicant is forbidden' cannot be true.
If a part is false, the whole cannot be true.
4
Recognize non-implications
The truth of the particular does not guarantee the truth of the universal (e.g., 'Some intoxicant is grape juice' is true, but 'Every intoxicant is grape juice' is false). Also, the falsity of the universal does not guarantee the falsity of the particular (e.g., 'Every intoxicant is grape juice' is false, but 'Some intoxicant is grape juice' may be true).
Overlap only provides one-directional entailments from universal to particular, not the reverse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المراد من التداخل في القضايا المحصورة المتقابلة: أن إحدى القضيتين داخلة في الأخرى، باعتبار أن إحداهما كلية والأخرى جزئية وهما متفقتان في الكيف (الإيجاب أو السلب)، لأن التبديل لم يحصل إلا في الكم، وبقيت كل عناصر القضية الأخرى محافظاً عليها بشكل تام. ويلزم من التداخل أنه إذا صدقت الكلية صدقت الجزئية تبعاً لها، لأنها بعض أفرادها، وإذا كذبت الجزئية كذبت الكلية، لأنه إذا لم يكن البعض صادقاً فالكل لا يكون صادقاً حتماً. ولكن لا يقتضي صدق الجزئية صدق الكلية، فقد تصدق وقد تكذب، لأن صدق بعض الشيء لا يستلزم صدقه كله. ولا يقتضي كذب الكلية كذب الجزئية، فقد تصدق الجزئية مع كذب الكلية وقد تكذب، لأن كذب الأعم لا يستلزم كذب الأخص. والتداخل يكون بين قضيتين مختلفتين في الكم فقط. أي: بين الكلية الموجبة والجزئية الموجبة. وبين الكلية السالبة والجزئية السالبة. الأمثلة: القضية مقابلتها أ - كل إنسان حيوان (ص) بعض الإنسان حيوان (ص) ب - كل حيوان إنسان (ذ) بعض الحيوان إنسان (ص) جـ - كل النبات يتحرك بالإرادة (ذ) بعض النبات يتحرك بالإرادة (ذ) د - لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر (ص) بعض الإنسان ليس بحجر (ص) هـ - لا شيء من الحيوان بإنسان (ذ) بعض الحيوان ليس بإنسان (ص) و - لا شيء من الإنسان بناطق (ذ) بعض الإنسان ليس بناطق (ذ) ز - بعض الإنسان حيوان (ص) كل إنسان حيوان (ص) ح - بعض الإنسان أبيض البشرة (ص) كل إنسان أبيض البشرة (ذ) ط - بعض الحيوان حجر (ذ) كل الحيوان حجر (ذ) من هذه الأمثلة المختلفة نلاحظ الأمور التالية: ١ - إذا صدقت الكلية فيها صدقت مقابلتها الجزئية. ٢ - إذا كذبت الجزئية فيها كذبت الكلية المقابلة لها. ونلاحظ أيضاً ما يلي: أن الجزئية تصدق فلا يقتضي ذلك صدق الكلية، فقد تصدق كما في المثال (ز) وقد تكذب كما في المثال (ح). وأن الكلية تكذب فلا يقتضي ذلك كذب الجزئية فقد تكذب كما في المثال (و) وقد تصدق كما في المثال (هـ).
The intended meaning of overlap among opposing quantified propositions is that one of the two propositions is contained within the other, considering that one is universal and the other is particular, and they agree in quality (affirmation or negation), because the only change occurred in quantity, while all other elements of the other proposition are completely preserved. From overlap it follows that if the universal is true, the particular is true consequent upon it, because it is some of its instances; and if the particular is false, the universal is false, because if the part is not true, the whole is necessarily not true. But the truth of the particular does not necessitate the truth of the universal, for it may be true or false, because the truth of some of a thing does not entail the truth of all of it. Nor does the falsity of the universal necessitate the falsity of the particular, for the particular may be true while the universal is false, or it may be false, because the falsity of the more general does not entail the falsity of the more specific. Overlap occurs only between two propositions differing in quantity; that is, between the universal affirmative and the particular affirmative, and between the universal negative and the particular negative. Examples: [list of examples a through ط]. From these various examples we note the following: 1. If the universal is true, its counterpart particular is true. 2. If the particular is false, its counterpart universal is false. We also note the following: that the particular may be true without necessitating the truth of the universal, as in example (ز) and as in example (ح). And the universal may be false without necessitating the falsity of the particular, as in example (و) and as in example (هـ).
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage defines the logical relation known as 'overlap' (tadakhul) between quantified propositions. It begins by stating that overlap occurs between two propositions that are universal and particular, of the same quality (both affirmative or both negative). The change is only in quantity (universal vs. particular). The author then explains the logical entailments: (1) If the universal is true, the particular is necessarily true because the particular is included in the universal. (2) If the particular is false, the universal is necessarily false because the whole cannot be true if a part is false. However, the converse implications do not hold: the truth of the particular does not guarantee the truth of the universal, and the falsity of the universal does not guarantee the falsity of the particular. The author then provides a series of examples (from a to ط) illustrating all combinations of truth values. For instance, in example (a), 'Every human is an animal' is true, and 'Some human is an animal' is true. In example (b), 'Every animal is human' is false, but 'Some animal is human' is true. In example (و), 'No human is rational' is false, and 'Some human is not rational' is false. Finally, the author summarizes two key observations: (1) universal truth implies particular truth, (2) particular falsity implies universal falsity, and also clarifies the non-implications from the examples. This is a standard treatment of the square of opposition's subalternation relation in Aristotelian logic, here called tadakhul. The examples, though not from the Islamic sciences, illustrate the logical point clearly.

Key Terms
التداخل
القضية المحصورة

The Law of Subalternation between Universal and Particular Propositions
Author uses examples to derive the law of subalternation and summarizes opposition types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the truth value of a subaltern particular proposition given the truth value of a universal, and vice versa, using the law of subalternation (tadākhul).

Core Idea
If the universal is true, the particular is true; if the particular is false, the universal is false.

This passage uses several examples with truth values to establish the logical relationship between universal and particular propositions. The author demonstrates that while a true universal guarantees a true particular, the converse does not hold. Similarly, a false particular forces a false universal, but a false universal leaves the particular's truth value open. This key logical principle is called subalternation (tadākhul).

Guided Path
1
Examine the truth tables of the examples
Look at examples B through I. Each pair shows a universal (left) and its corresponding particular (right) with truth values (ص = true, ذ = false). Identify the pattern when the universal is true and when it is false, and similarly for the particular.
Pay attention to the abbreviations: ك م = universal affirmative, ج م = particular affirmative, ك س = universal negative, ج س = particular negative.
2
Extract the two key observations
From the examples, the author notes: (1) If the universal is true, its particular is true. (2) If the particular is false, its universal is false. Confirm these with examples B (universal false, particular true shows the converse is not necessary) and D (universal true, particular true).
Examples E and F show that a false universal can accompany either a true or false particular.
3
Formulate the law of subalternation
The law states: The universal and particular are in a relation of tadākhul (subalternation). This means the truth of the universal implies the truth of the particular, and the falsity of the particular implies the falsity of the universal. Write down both directions.
The author personifies the universal saying 'If I am true, you are true' and the particular responding 'If I am false, you are false'.
4
Apply the law to an Islamic legal example
Consider the universal affirmative 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' (true in Islamic law). According to subalternation, the particular 'Some intoxicant is forbidden' must also be true. Conversely, if the particular 'Some intoxicant is forbidden' were false, then the universal would be false.
This example uses the same logical structure as the passage but with Islamic content.

Commentary
This passage explains the logical relations between universal and particular categorical propositions (affirmative and negative). The author first provides a series of examples (B through I) with truth values indicated by (ص) for true and (ذ) for false. Each example shows a universal proposition on the left and its corresponding particular proposition on the right, labeled with abbreviations for universal affirmative (ك م), particular affirmative (ج م), universal negative (ك س), and particular negative (ج س). From these examples, the author extracts two key observations: first, if the universal is true, then its subaltern particular is also true; second, if the particular is false, then its corresponding universal is also false. However, the converse does not hold: a true particular does not guarantee a true universal, and a false universal does not guarantee a false particular. This is illustrated by examples (G) and (H) for the first case and (E) and (F) for the second. The author then formulates the law of subalternation (تداخل) for affirmative and negative pairs: the universal and particular are in a relation of subalternation. Subalternation means that if the universal is true, the particular must be true; if the particular is false, the universal must be false. The author dramatizes this by having the universal say to the particular: 'If I am true, you are true,' and the particular responding: 'If I am false, you are false.' Finally, the passage gives a summary of opposition rules: singular propositions (شخصية) are related by contradiction, and for the four quantified propositions, the author refers to the square of opposition, mentioning contrariety (تضاد) as part of that square. The text ends incomplete. All examples use logical variables (human, animal, plant, stone, rational, white-skinned) which are typical logical examples, not specifically from Islamic sciences, but the logical principles are universal.
