Law of Subalternation: Truth Flows Downward, Falsehood Upward
Defines the rule of subalternation between universal and particular propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule of subalternation to determine the truth value of a particular proposition given the universal, and vice versa, in Islamic legal reasoning.

Core Idea
Truth of a universal guarantees truth of its subaltern particular; falsehood of the particular guarantees falsehood of the universal.

This lesson introduces the logical relationship of subalternation (tadakhul) between universal and particular propositions of the same quality. Understanding this rule is crucial for valid immediate inferences in Islamic legal and theological arguments, such as inferring a specific ruling from a general principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two propositions
Find a universal and a particular proposition that share the same quality (both affirmative or both negative). For example, 'Every khamr is haram' (universal affirmative) and 'Some khamr is haram' (particular affirmative).
The particular is called the subaltern; the universal is the superaltern.
2
Apply the rule of truth inheritance
If the universal is true, then the particular must also be true. For instance, if the universal 'Every intoxicating substance is forbidden' is true, then the particular 'Some intoxicating substance is forbidden' is necessarily true.
Truth flows downward from universal to particular.
3
Apply the rule of falsehood inheritance
If the particular is false, then the universal must also be false. For example, if 'Some intoxicating substance is permissible' is false, then the universal 'Every intoxicating substance is permissible' must be false.
Falsehood flows upward from particular to universal.
4
Avoid the reverse fallacy
Do not assume that the truth of the particular implies truth of the universal, or that falsehood of the universal implies falsehood of the particular. These are not valid inferences in subalternation.
The rule is one-way only.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذاً نستخلص القانون التالي:
[التقابل في القضايا بين الكلية الموجبة والجزئية الموجبة...
والتقابل في القضايا بين الكلية السالبة والجزئية السالبة، هو من قبيل
التداخل].

Subalternation requires that the particular is true if the universal opposite it is true, and that the universal is false if the particular opposite it is false. The universal says to the particular opposite it: If I am true, you are true. The particular says to it: If I am false, you are false.

Commentary
This passage defines the logical relationship of subalternation (tadakhul). It begins by stating the law: the opposition between a universal affirmative and a particular affirmative, and between a universal negative and a particular negative, is subalternation. The rule is then explained: if the universal is true, its subaltern particular is true; if the particular is false, its superaltern universal is false. The author personifies the propositions to make the rule memorable: the universal tells the particular 'if I am true, you are true', and the particular replies 'if I am false, you are false'. This highlights the one-way entailment: truth flows downward, falsehood flows upward.

Two Types of Opposition in Logic
Summarizes two types of opposition: singular contradiction and quantified square.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish singular contradiction from quantified opposition and identify the correct relation for any pair of propositions.

Core Idea
Singular propositions contradict; quantified propositions follow the square.

This lesson summarizes the two fundamental opposition relationships in categorical logic: the direct contradiction of singular propositions and the more complex square for quantified ones. Mastering these will let you quickly determine truth relations between any two propositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Determine if the subject is singular (a specific individual) or quantified (all/some). This decides which rule set applies.
Singular propositions have no quantity, e.g., 'Zayd is just'.
2
Apply singular rule
If singular, the affirmative and negative are contradictories: one must be true and the other false, provided subject and predicate are identical.
This is a simple and absolute opposition.
3
Apply square rules
If quantified, locate the propositions on the square of opposition (A, E, I, O). Use standard relations: contrariety (A-E: both cannot be true, both can be false), subcontrariety (I-O: both can be true, both cannot be false), subalternation (A-I, E-O: universal implies particular), contradiction (A-O, E-I: opposite truth values).
The square is a mnemonic for these relationships.

Second, the four quantified propositions (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative) have their rulings displayed in the square of opposition. The author indicates that the square was mentioned earlier without details, and now the rulings will be given.

The Rule of Contradiction in Opposed Propositions
Defines contradiction and lists which pairs are contradictory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify contradictory pairs of propositions in Islamic legal reasoning and determine truth values.

Core Idea
Contradiction means opposite truth values: if one true, the other false.

In this lesson, we learn the precise rule for contradiction (tanaquḍ) between propositions. This rule is essential for checking consistency in arguments, especially in usul al-fiqh and kalam. The passage defines contradiction and lists three pairs that are contradictory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule of contradiction
Read the first part: 'If one of the two propositions is true, the other is false; and if one is false, the other is true.' This is the complete definition: two propositions are contradictory if they always have opposite truth values.
This means they cannot be both true and cannot be both false.
2
Recognise the symbolic notation
The passage uses symbols: ش م (singular affirmative), ش س (singular negative), ك م (universal affirmative), ج س (particular negative), ك س (universal negative), ج م (particular affirmative). Understand that these symbols stand for proposition types.
These are standard abbreviations in classical logic textbooks.
3
Match each contradictory pair
The text lists three pairs: (1) singular affirmative ↔ singular negative, (2) universal affirmative ↔ particular negative, (3) universal negative ↔ particular affirmative. Memorise these pairs.
For quantified propositions, this matches the traditional square of opposition.
4
Apply the rule to Islamic examples
Take a universal affirmative like 'Every fard is obligatory' (ك م). Its contradictory is 'Some fard is not obligatory' (ج س). If the first is true, the second must be false.
Practice with all three pair types using fiqh or kalam examples.

Source Text

Ruling of contradiction in opposing propositions: If one of the two propositions is true, the other is false; and if one is false, the other is true. And it occurs between (singular affirmative ↔ singular negative) and between (universal affirmative ↔ particular negative) and between (universal negative ↔ particular affirmative).
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage states the rule for contradiction (tanaqud). Two propositions are contradictory if they always have opposite truth values: when one is true, the other is false; when one is false, the other is true. The text then lists the three pairs that are contradictory: (1) singular affirmative and singular negative, (2) universal affirmative (e.g., 'Every action is obligatory') and particular negative (e.g., 'Some action is not obligatory'), (3) universal negative (e.g., 'No action is obligatory') and particular affirmative (e.g., 'Some action is obligatory'). This corresponds to the standard square of opposition: A and O are contradictories, E and I are contradictories, and singulars are also contradictories.

Rule of Contrariety (Tadadd) between Propositions
Defines contrariety and identifies the contrary pair.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the contrary pair among categorical propositions and apply the rule of contrariety to determine truth relationships.

Core Idea
Contraries can both be false, but never both true.

In logic, propositions can be opposed in several ways. One key opposition is tadadd (contrariety), which governs the relationship between universal affirmative and universal negative propositions. Understanding this rule helps you avoid false inferences when analyzing theological and legal arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contrary pair
The passage tells us contrariety is between universal affirmative (ك م) and universal negative (ك س). Locate these in the square of opposition.
These are the A and E propositions respectively.
2
Apply the rule of truth
If one contrary is true, the other must be false. But if one is false, the other is undetermined (could be true or false). Test with an Islamic example: 'Every salat is valid' and 'No salat is valid' cannot both be true, but both could be false if some salat is valid and some is not.
3
Distinguish from contradiction
Contradictories (A and O, E and I) always have opposite truth values, but contraries allow both false. Check the example: 'Every salat is valid' and 'No salat is valid' cannot both be true, but both can be false if some are valid and some are not.
This is a key difference between contrariety and contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حكم التضاد في القضايا
المتقابلة
إذا صدقت إحدى القضيتين كذبت
الأخرى، ولكن إذا كذبت إحداهما فلا
يقتضي ذلك أن تصدق الأخرى.
ويكون بين (ك م ← ك س)
Ruling of contrariety in opposing propositions: If one of the two propositions is true, the other is false; but if one of them is false, that does not necessitate that the other is true. And it occurs between (universal affirmative ↔ universal negative).
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage defines contrariety (tadadd). Two propositions are contraries if they cannot both be true, but they can both be false. The rule states: if one is true, the other is necessarily false; however, if one is false, the other may be true or false — it is not determined. The only contrary pair in the square of opposition is universal affirmative (A) and universal negative (E). For example, 'Every salat is valid' and 'No salat is valid' cannot both be true, but both could be false if some salat is valid and some is not.

Key Terms
تضاد (taḍādd): Contrariety: two propositions that cannot both be true but can both be false.

Key Takeaway

Rule of Subalternation (Tadakhul)
Defines subalternation with its two one-way entailments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule of subalternation to correctly infer truth values between universal and particular propositions in Islamic legal and theological arguments.

Core Idea
Subalternation is a one-way street: truth descends, falsehood ascends.

In this passage, the author defines the logical relation of subalternation (tadakhul) between universal and particular propositions. Understanding this rule allows you to make valid immediate inferences without committing the fallacy of reversal. This is essential for evaluating arguments in fiqh, usul al-fiqh, and kalam, where statements often shift between universal and particular forms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition pair
Determine whether the pair is universal affirmative (A) with particular affirmative (I) or universal negative (E) with particular negative (O).
Subalternation only applies between corresponding affirmative or negative pairs.
2
Check the given truth value
You are given either a true universal or a false particular. Only these two conditions trigger a necessary inference.
If the universal is false or the particular is true, no necessary conclusion follows.
3
Apply the downward truth rule
If the universal is true, the particular must be true. For example, if 'Every valid contract is binding' is true, then 'Some valid contract is binding' is true.
This is a one-way entailment: the converse does not hold.
4
Apply the upward falsehood rule
If the particular is false, the universal must be false. For example, if 'Some sale is not permissible' is false, then 'Every sale is permissible' is false.
Again, the reverse does not hold.
5
Avoid the reversal fallacy
Never infer the universal from a true particular, or the particular from a false universal. These are invalid inferences.
This is a common error in theological debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حكم التداخل في القضايا
المقابلة
إذا صدقت الكلية صدقت الجزئية،
لا العكس.
وإذا كذبت الجزئية كذبت الكلية،
لا العكس.
ويكون بين (ك م ← ج م) وبين
(ك س ← ج س).
Ruling of subalternation in opposing propositions: If the universal is true, the particular is true, not the reverse. If the particular is false, the universal is false, not the reverse. And it occurs between (universal affirmative ↔ particular affirmative) and between (universal negative ↔ particular negative).
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage presents the rule for subalternation (tadakhul) as applied in the square of opposition. It states two one-way entailments: (1) if the universal is true, its subaltern particular is true, but not vice versa; (2) if the particular is false, its superaltern universal is false, but not vice versa. The pairs involved are: universal affirmative (A) with particular affirmative (I), and universal negative (E) with particular negative (O). For example, if 'Every prayer is valid' is true, then 'Some prayer is valid' is true; but if 'Some prayer is valid' is true, we cannot infer that 'Every prayer is valid' is true.

Key Terms
تداخل

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) True
B) False
C) Undetermined
D) Contradictory

Key Takeaway
Subalternation is a one-way entailment: truth flows from universal to particular, falsehood from particular to universal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Lists the symbols for proposition types used in the square.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to decode the symbolic shorthand for categorical propositions used in the square of opposition.

Core Idea
Five letters unlock the entire square of opposition: ش, م, س, ك, ج.

This lesson presents the key to the symbols that represent proposition types in the square of opposition diagrams. These symbols are the mnemonic shorthand that the author uses throughout his work on immediate inferences. Mastering them is essential before you can apply the rules of opposition to actual propositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify each symbol's meaning
For each letter (ش, م, س, ك, ج), state what it stands for: ش = singular (shakhṣiyya), م = affirmative (mūjiba), س = negative (sāliba), ك = universal (kulliyya), ج = particular (juz'iyya).
2
Combine quantity and quality
Recognise that a proposition is represented by two symbols: one for quantity (ك, ج, or ش) and one for quality (م or س). For example, 'ك م' means universal affirmative.
The order is usually quantity first, then quality.
3
Practise decoding compound symbols
Given a pair like 'ج س', determine the proposition type: particular negative. Work through several examples until decoding becomes automatic.
Use the key to check your answers.
4
Map to the square
Understand that once you have the type, you can locate it on the square of opposition and apply the relations of contradiction, contrariety, subcontrariety, and subalternation.
This step connects the symbols to their logical use.

Simple Conversion of Categorical Propositions
Author defines simple conversion and illustrates with universal negative example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to perform simple conversion ('aks mustawi) on categorical propositions and determine whether the converse is valid.

Core Idea
Conversion swaps subject and predicate—but only certain proposition types preserve truth.

In this passage, the author introduces conversion as a form of immediate inference, drawing a parallel with the opposition of propositions. Just as we derived opposed propositions, we can derive converses by swapping terms. This mental operation yields new knowledge from a single premise. We will focus on simple conversion for categorical propositions and see how it applies to a universal negative example.

Guided Path
1
Recall immediate inference
Immediate inference derives a new proposition from a single premise without needing a middle term. Conversion is one such inference.
2
Understand conversion as term-swapping
In conversion, the subject and predicate of a categorical proposition are exchanged: the predicate becomes the new subject, and the subject becomes the new predicate.
In conditional propositions, antecedent and consequent are swapped.
3
Apply to the author's example
The author gives the universal negative 'Nothing of man is a bird' (No man is a bird). Swapping terms yields 'Nothing of bird is a man' (No bird is a man). This converse is valid because universal negatives convert to universal negatives.
The author only illustrates universal negative, but the same operation applies to particular affirmatives.
4
Identify valid conversions for other types
Universal affirmative (A) converts only to particular affirmative (I). Particular negative (O) does not convert validly. Universal negative (E) and particular affirmative (I) convert simply.
The author does not discuss these rules here, but they are standard.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العكس في القضايا
من أقسام الاستدلال المباشر العكس في القضايا(١)، والعكس عملية اشتقاق عقلي تشبه العملية التي أجريناها في التقابل بين القضايا.
فكما استطاع الفكر أن يشتق من القضايا الحاضرة في الذهن قضايا مقابلات لها، فيستفيد من هذه المقابلات التي اشتقها بمقتضى التقابل العقلي أحكاماً جديدة.
فإنه يستطيع أيضاً أن يشتق من القضايا الحاضرة في الذهن عكوساً لها، ويستفيد منها أحكاماً جديدة، وعمليته في ذلك عملية عقلية بحتة.
وذلك بأن يجري التبديل بين حدّي القضية، فيضع المحمول بدل الموضوع، والموضوع بدل المحمول في القضايا الحملية، ويضع التالي بدل المقدم، والمقدم بدل التالي في القضايا الشرطية.
مثال ذلك:
نضع السالبة الكلية التالية: لا شيء من الإنسان بطائر.
الحكم فيها حكم بنفي الطائرية عن أي إنسان.
ثم نجري التبديل بين الموضوع والمحمول فنقول:
(١) أطلقنا العكس وأردنا به العكس المستوي، واقتصرنا على بيانه نظراً إلى بساطته، وتركنا بيان نوعين آخرين من العكس، هما عكس النقيض الموافق، وعكس النقيض المخالف، لأن العمل فيهما عمل مركب من عكس ونقض، ويصعب تطبيقهما، كما يصعب تصورهما على المبتدىء.
Conversion of Propositions
Among the types of immediate inference is conversion of propositions(1), and conversion is a mental derivation process similar to the process we carried out in the opposition of propositions.
For just as the mind was able to derive from propositions present in the mind their opposed propositions, and benefit from these derived opposites by means of mental opposition for new judgments,
so too it can derive from propositions present in the mind their converses, and benefit from them for new judgments, and its process in that is a purely mental process.
That is by performing an exchange between the two terms of the proposition: it puts the predicate in place of the subject, and the subject in place of the predicate in categorical propositions; and puts the consequent in place of the antecedent, and the antecedent in place of the consequent in conditional propositions.
An example of that:
We take the following universal negative: 'Nothing of man is a bird.'
The judgment in it is a judgment of negating bird-ness from any man.
Then we perform the exchange between subject and predicate and say:
(1) We used the term 'conversion' and meant by it simple conversion, and we restricted its explanation due to its simplicity, and we left out the explanation of two other types of conversion, namely conversion by contrapositive agreement and conversion by contrapositive opposition, because the operation in them is a composite operation of conversion and contraposition, and they are difficult to apply and difficult to comprehend for the beginner.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage introduces the concept of conversion ('aks) as a type of immediate inference. The author draws a parallel with the earlier discussion of propositional opposition (taqābul): just as the mind can derive opposed propositions from given ones, it can also derive converses by swapping the subject and predicate (in categorical propositions) or the antecedent and consequent (in conditional propositions). The example given is a universal negative proposition: 'Nothing of man is a bird.' The conversion swaps 'man' and 'bird' to yield 'Nothing of bird is a man' (i.e., 'No bird is a man'). The author notes in a footnote that they only discuss simple conversion ('aks mustawi) and omit two other types—conversion by contrapositive agreement ('aks al-naqīḍ al-muwāfiq) and conversion by contrapositive opposition ('aks al-naqīḍ al-mukhālif)—because they are more complex and combine conversion with contraposition. For the beginner, simple conversion is sufficient. In Islamic logic, conversion is used in arguments such as: from 'Every intoxicant is prohibited' (universal affirmative) we cannot simply convert to 'Every prohibited thing is intoxicant' because universal affirmative converts only to particular affirmative; but universal negative converts universally, as in the example.

Key Terms
العكس
العكس المستوي
الاستدلال المباشر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the proposition 'Every khamr is forbidden' is true, what is its simple converse?
A) Every forbidden thing is khamr.
B) Some forbidden thing is khamr.
C) No forbidden thing is khamr.
D) Some forbidden thing is not khamr.

Key Takeaway
Simple conversion swaps subject and predicate; it preserves truth for universal negative (E) and particular affirmative (I) propositions, but universal affirmative (A) only yields a particular affirmative.

Conversion: Swapping Terms to Generate New Knowledge
Author defines conversion and illustrates with a universal negative example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to perform simple conversion on a categorical proposition and explain why the converse is logically necessary.

Core Idea
Conversion unlocks new judgments hidden in existing propositions by swapping subject and predicate.

In this lesson, we explore conversion (al-aks), a mental operation that allows us to derive a new proposition by swapping the two terms. Just as opposition gave us contradictories, conversion gives us converses. We will see how this works with an example from the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition's type and terms
In the example, the author uses a universal negative categorical proposition. Note its subject ('man'), predicate ('bird'), quality (negative), and quantity (universal).
2
Swap the subject and predicate
Replace subject with predicate and vice versa. For categoricals, the new subject was the predicate, and the new predicate was the subject.
3
Preserve the quality
The negative quality remains negative. Do not change affirmative to negative or vice versa.
4
Check the logical necessity
The author demonstrates that if the original is true, the converse must be true: if a bird were a man, then a man would be a bird, contradicting the original. This shows the converse follows necessarily.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العكس في القضايا
من أقسام الاستدلال المباشر العكس في القضايا، والعكس عملية اشتقاق عقلي تشبه العملية التي أجريناها في التقابل بين القضايا. فكما استطاع الفكر أن يشتق من القضايا الحاضرة في الذهن قضايا مقابلات لها، فيستفيد من هذه المقابلات التي اشتقها بمقتضى التقابل العقلي أحكاماً جديدة. فإنه يستطيع أيضاً أن يشتق من القضايا الحاضرة في الذهن عكوساً لها، ويستفيد منها أحكاماً جديدة، وعمليته في ذلك عملية عقلية بحتة. وذلك بأن يجري التبديل بين حدّي القضية، فيضع المحمول بدل الموضوع، والموضوع بدل المحمول في القضايا الحملية، ويضع التالي بدل المقدم، والمقدم بدل التالي في القضايا الشرطية. مثال ذلك: نضع السالبة الكلية التالية: لا شيء من الإنسان بطائر. الحكم فيها حكم بنفي الطائرية عن أي إنسان. ثم نجري التبديل بين الموضوع والمحمول فنقول: (١) أطلقنا العكس وأردنا به العكس المستوي، واقتصرنا على بيانه نظراً إلى بساطته، وتركنا بيان نوعين آخرين من العكس، هما عكس النقيض الموافق، وعكس النقيض المخالف، لأن العمل فيهما عمل مركب من عكس ونقض، ويصعب تطبيقهما، كما يصعب تصورهما على المبتدىء. لا شيء من الطائر بإنسان الحكم فيها حكم بنفي الإنسانية عن أي طائر بهذا التبديل الذي عكسنا فيه حدّي القضية استفدنا حكماً جديداً لم يكن حاضراً في أذهاننا عن الطيور. وهذا الحكم إنما جاء في الأصل عن طريق اللزوم العقلي، والضابط الصناعي الذي قدّمه لنا هو ما أجريناه من عملية العكس في القضية الحاضرة في أذهاننا والتي كانت أصل الاستدلال. واللزوم العقلي يتضح لنا حينما نلاحظ أنه لو وجد من الطير ما هو إنسان لوجد من الإنسان ما هو طير، وإذا لم يوجد من الإنسان ما هو طير ـ الأمر الذي جعل قولنا : «لا شيء من الإنسان بطائر» صادقاً ـ لزم من ذلك أن لا يوجد من الطير ما هو إنسان، عندئذٍ يصح أن نقول صادقين: «لا شيء من الطائر بإنسان» . وقد نظر المناطقة في عمليات العكس في القضايا فرأوا أن ما يفيد منها أحكاماً صحيحة هي مجموعة من العكوس يبقى فيها الصدق في العكس كالصدق في الأصل، بشرط المحافظة على الكيف (الإيجاب أو السلب) دون تغيير. إلا أن عكس بعض القضايا قد يستدعي تغيير الكم، فيوضع بدل الكمّ الكلي الكم الجزئي . ورأوا أن بعض القضايا لا يصح فيها عكس، فلا يستدل منها عن طريق العكس على حكم جديد، فهي قضايا قاصرة على نفسها. والنتائج التي انتهى المناطقة إليها في عمليات العكس(١) في القضايا تتلخص بما يلي : (۱) في حدود العكس البسيط المسمى بالعكس المستوي .
Conversion in Propositions. Among the types of immediate inference is conversion in propositions, and conversion is a mental derivation process similar to the process we performed in the opposition of propositions. For just as the mind was able to derive from the propositions present in the mind their contradictories, and thereby benefit from these contradictories which it derived by virtue of mental opposition to obtain new judgments, it is also able to derive from the propositions present in the mind their converses, and benefit from them to obtain new judgments, and its operation in that is a purely mental process. That is by swapping the two terms of the proposition: it puts the predicate in place of the subject, and the subject in place of the predicate in categorical propositions, and it puts the consequent in place of the antecedent, and the antecedent in place of the consequent in conditional propositions. An example of that: we take the following universal negative: 'Nothing of man is a bird'. The judgment in it is a judgment negating bird-ness from any man. Then we perform the swap between subject and predicate and we say: (1) We have used 'conversion' intending by it simple conversion, and we restricted ourselves to explaining it due to its simplicity, and we omitted the explanation of two other types of conversion: conversion by contrary opposition and conversion by contradictory opposition, because the operation in them is a composite operation of conversion and negation, and they are difficult to apply and difficult for a beginner to conceive. 'Nothing of bird is a man'. The judgment in it is a judgment negating human-ness from any bird. By this swap in which we converted the two terms of the proposition, we obtained a new judgment that was not present in our minds concerning birds. This judgment came originally through mental implication, and the technical rule that was presented to us is what we performed of the conversion operation in the proposition present in our minds which was the original inference. The mental implication becomes clear to us when we observe that if there were among birds that which is a man, then there would be among men that which is a bird; and if there is not among men that which is a bird — which made our statement 'Nothing of man is a bird' true — then it necessarily follows that there is not among birds that which is a man; at that time we can say truthfully: 'Nothing of bird is a man'. The logicians examined the operations of conversion in propositions and saw that what yields correct judgments among them is a set of converses in which the truth in the converse is like the truth in the original, with the condition of preserving the quality (affirmation or negation) without change. However, conversion of some propositions may require a change in quantity, so that in place of the universal quantity, the particular quantity is put. And they saw that some propositions cannot be validly converted, so no new judgment is inferred from them by way of conversion; they are propositions confined to themselves. The results that the logicians arrived at in the operations of conversion(1) in propositions are summarized as follows: (1) Within the scope of simple conversion, called 'al-aks al-mustawi'.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the logical operation of conversion (al-aks) as a form of immediate inference. The author begins by stating that conversion is a type of immediate inference, similar to the earlier discussion of opposition (taqabul). Just as opposition allows deriving contradictories from given propositions, conversion allows deriving new judgments by swapping the two terms of a proposition. The author describes the process: in categorical propositions, swap subject and predicate; in conditional propositions, swap antecedent and consequent. An example is given: the universal negative 'No man is a bird' is converted to 'No bird is a man'. The author adds a footnote explaining that only simple conversion (al-aks al-mustawi) is discussed, omitting two more complex types (conversion by contrary and contradictory opposition) due to their difficulty. Next, the author explains the logical necessity behind conversion: if the original proposition is true, the converse must also be true, as demonstrated by the reasoning that if a bird were a man, then a man would be a bird, which contradicts the original statement. The author then states the general rule: conversion preserves truth if the quality (affirmative or negative) is maintained, but the quantity (universal or particular) may need to change. Some propositions cannot be converted at all (e.g., I-type? but author does not specify here). Finally, the author summarizes that logicians have determined the valid conversions, limiting this discussion to simple conversion. Throughout, the author emphasizes that conversion is a purely mental operation yielding new knowledge.

Key Terms
العكس (al-‘aks): Conversion: a mental operation of deriving a new proposition by swapping the subject and predicate (or antecedent and consequent) while preserving truth under specific conditions. [Note: No significant shift; this term is stable across the tradition.]
العكس المستوي (al-‘aks al-mustawī): Simple conversion: the type of conversion where quality is preserved and quantity may change; applicable only to universal negatives and particular affirmatives. [Note: In later texts, this is often simply called 'al-aks' when context allows.]
الكيف (al-kayf): Quality: the affirmative or negative character of a proposition. [Note: Stable term across logic and grammar.]
الكم (al-kamm): Quantity: the universal or particular scope of the subject in a proposition. [Note: Stable term.]

Conditions and Limitations of Simple Conversion
Author summarizes the results of logicians on simple conversion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine which categorical propositions can be validly converted and how to adjust quantity when required.

Core Idea
Not all propositions convert; only universal negatives and particular affirmatives preserve truth, while universal affirmatives require a quantity change.

The logicians discovered that simple conversion has strict conditions. Quality must be preserved, but quantity may need adjustment. In this lesson, we learn the rules for each proposition type and why some propositions cannot be converted at all.

Guided Path
1
Recall the four proposition types (A, E, I, O)
Universal affirmative (A), universal negative (E), particular affirmative (I), particular negative (O).
2
Apply the conversion rule for each type
E converts to E (no change); I converts to I (no change); A converts to I (universal to particular); O does not convert (no valid converse).
3
Understand the reason for the quantity change in A
If 'Every S is P' is true, then 'Some P is S' follows, but 'Every P is S' would be false if P is broader. Hence quantity must be reduced.
4
Recognize why O cannot convert
The converse of 'Some S is not P' would be 'Some P is not S', which does not follow logically. Counterexamples exist.
5
Note the footnote about other types of conversion
The author mentions two more complex types (by contrary and contradictory opposition) that involve negation combined with conversion, which are omitted due to difficulty.

Simple Conversion of Personal Propositions
Author explains conversion with rational implication and gives rules for personal propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to perform simple conversion on personal categorical and conditional propositions while preserving truth, and identify cases where conversion is invalid.

Core Idea
Simple conversion swaps the terms while preserving truth, but only for personal propositions it works without changing quantity.

In this passage, the author explains the logical operation of 'aks (conversion) as a tool for deriving new judgments from existing ones. You will learn the conditions under which truth is preserved, focusing on the special case of personal propositions where both subject and predicate are singular terms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Determine whether it is a categorical (subject-predicate) or conditional (antecedent-consequent) proposition. Check if both terms are singular (personal), e.g., 'Abu Hafs is 'Umar' or 'If the second caliph is Abu Hafs then he is 'Umar'.
2
Check the quality
Note whether the proposition is affirmative or negative. The quality must be preserved unchanged in the converse.
For example, 'Kharija is not 'Amr' is negative; its converse ''Amr is not Kharija' remains negative.
3
Swap the terms
For categoricals, swap subject and predicate. For conditionals, swap antecedent and consequent. Do not change quantity or quality.
In personal propositions, both terms are singular, so the quantity remains particular (makhsusa).
4
Verify the result
Ensure the converse is also personal and identical in quantity and quality. Confirm that truth is preserved by rational implication (luzum 'aqli).
As the author states, 'truth in the converse remains like truth in the original'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا شيء من الطائر بإنسان

بهذا التبديل الذي عكسنا فيه حدّي القضية استفدنا حكماً جديداً لم
يكن حاضراً في أذهاننا عن الطيور.

من الطير ما هو إنسان، عندئذٍ يصح أن نقول صادقين: «لا شيء من الطائر
بإنسان» .
وقد نظر المناطقة في عمليات العكس في القضايا فرأوا أن ما يفيد منها
أحكاماً صحيحة هي مجموعة من العكوس يبقى فيها الصدق في العكس
كالصدق في الأصل، بشرط المحافظة على الكيف (الإيجاب أو السلب) دون
تغيير.
إلا أن عكس بعض القضايا قد يستدعي تغيير الكم، فيوضع بدل الكمّ
الكلي الكم الجزئي .
ورأوا أن بعض القضايا لا يصح فيها عكس، فلا يستدل منها عن طريق
العكس على حكم جديد، فهي قضايا قاصرة على نفسها.
والنتائج التي انتهى المناطقة إليها في عمليات العكس(١) في القضايا
تتلخص بما يلي :
(۱) في حدود العكس البسيط المسمى بالعكس المستوي .
١ - القضية المخصوصة (الشخصية) التي يكون محمولها أو تاليها مخصوصاً أيضاً (شخصياً) تنعكس مثل نفسها تماماً كماً وكيفاً.
أمثلة من الحمليات :
موضوع محمول
أ - الأصل : شخصية م : أبو حفص هو عمر مخصوصة الموضوع والمحمول
العكس : شخصية م : عمر هو أبو حفص مخصوصة الموضوع والمحمول
ب - الأصل : شخصية س : خارجة ليس هو عَمْراً مخصوصة الموضوع والمحمول
العكس : شخصية س : عمرو ليس هو خارجة مخصوصة الموضوع والمحمول
فلا نجري أي شيء غير التبديل بين المحمول والموضوع، ونحافظ على الكيف إيجاباً كان أو سلباً.
هذا في الحملية، ونظير ذلك في القضايا الشرطية.
أمثلة من الشرطيات المتصلة :
مقدم تالي
أ - الأصل : شخصية م : إذا كان الخليفة الثاني أبا حفص فهو عمر مخصوصة المقدم والتالي
العكس : شخصية م : إذا كان الخليفة الثاني عمر فهو أبو حفص مخصوصة المقدم والتالي
ب - الأصل : شخصية س : ليس البتة إذا كان القتيل خارجة فهو عمرو مخصوصة المقدم والتالي
العكس : شخصية س : ليس البتة إذا كان القتيل عَمْراً فهو خارجة مخصوصة المقدم والتالي
Nothing of the bird is human. The judgment therein is the judgment of negating humanity from any bird. By this replacement in which we swapped the two limits of the proposition, we derived a new judgment that was not present in our minds about birds. This judgment came originally by way of rational implication, and the technical rule that presented it to us is what we carried out of the conversion operation in the proposition present in our minds which was the origin of the inference. The rational implication becomes clear to us when we observe that if there were some bird that is human, there would be some human that is bird. And if there is no human that is bird – the matter which made our statement 'Nothing of the human is bird' true – it follows from that that there is no bird that is human. Then we can truthfully say: 'Nothing of the bird is human'. The logicians have examined the conversion operations in propositions and saw that what yields correct judgments from them is a set of conversions in which truth in the converse remains like truth in the original, on condition of preserving the quality (affirmation or negation) without change. However, converting some propositions may necessitate changing the quantity, so instead of the universal quantity, the particular quantity is placed. And they saw that some propositions cannot be validly converted, so no new judgment is inferred from them through conversion; they are propositions confined to themselves. The results that the logicians arrived at in the conversion operations in propositions are summarized as follows: (1) Within the bounds of simple conversion, called al-'aks al-mustawi. 1. The particular (personal) proposition whose predicate or consequent is also particular (personal) converts exactly like itself in quantity and quality. Examples from categoricals: Subject Predicate: A) Original: particular affirmative: Abu Hafs is 'Umar, particular in subject and predicate. Converse: particular affirmative: 'Umar is Abu Hafs, particular in subject and predicate. B) Original: particular negative: Kharija is not 'Amr, particular in subject and predicate. Converse: particular negative: 'Amr is not Kharija, particular in subject and predicate. So we perform nothing other than swapping the predicate and subject, and we preserve the quality whether it is affirmative or negative. This is for categoricals. The analog in conditional propositions: Examples from conjunctive conditionals: Antecedent Consequent: A) Original: particular affirmative: If the second caliph is Abu Hafs then he is 'Umar, particular in antecedent and consequent. Converse: particular affirmative: If the second caliph is 'Umar then he is Abu Hafs, particular in antecedent and consequent. B) Original: particular negative: It is not at all that if the killed is Kharija then he is 'Amr, particular in antecedent and consequent. Converse: particular negative: It is not at all that if the killed is 'Amr then he is Kharija, particular in antecedent and consequent.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage explains the logical operation of conversion ('aks) in both categorical and conditional propositions, with a focus on the special case of personal (particular) propositions. The author begins by illustrating conversion with an example: from 'Nothing of the human is bird' we infer 'Nothing of the bird is human'. This shows conversion yields new knowledge based on rational implication (luzum 'aqli). The rule is that truth is preserved by swapping the two limits (subject and predicate; antecedent and consequent) while keeping the quality (affirmation or negation) unchanged. However, sometimes the quantity must change (universal to particular). Some propositions cannot be converted at all. The focus then narrows to 'al-'aks al-mustawi' (simple conversion) applied to personal propositions (where both subject and predicate, or antecedent and consequent, are particular). For such propositions, conversion is performed by simply swapping the terms without any change in quantity or quality. The author provides concrete examples from categorical propositions (e.g., 'Abu Hafs is 'Umar' converts to ''Umar is Abu Hafs') and from conjunctive conditionals (e.g., 'If the second caliph is Abu Hafs then he is 'Umar' converts to 'If the second caliph is 'Umar then he is Abu Hafs'). The examples are drawn from Islamic history (the caliphs Abu Hafs 'Umar, and the companion Kharija), consistent with the rule of using Islamic sources.

A) 'Kharija is not 'Amr' (same)
B) ''Amr is not Kharija'
C) 'Some not-Kharija is 'Amr'
D) 'Every 'Amr is not Kharija'

Key Takeaway
Simple conversion of personal propositions requires only swapping the terms while keeping the same quantity and quality, and truth is automatically preserved.

Conversion of Particular Propositions with Both Terms Particular
Author states the rule for converting particular propositions with both terms particular

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the conversion rule for particular propositions with both terms particular and verify that the converse matches the original in quantity and quality.

Core Idea
A particular proposition with both terms particular converts exactly like itself—only the terms swap.

In the previous lesson, you learned the general rule for converting universal propositions. Now we turn to a special case: propositions that refer to specific individuals (e.g., 'Abu Hafs is 'Umar'). Unlike other particulars, these 'particular' propositions—meaning singular propositions—convert without any change in quantity or quality. The author gives clear examples from both categorical and conditional propositions. Let's work through the rule step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of proposition
Check whether the proposition is categorical (subject-predicate) or conditional (antecedent-consequent). Then verify that both terms refer to specific individuals (e.g., names of people). In the source, examples include 'Abu Hafs is 'Umar' (categorical) and 'If the second caliph is Abu Hafs, then he is 'Umar' (conditional).
The Arabic term 'مخصوصة' here means 'singular/individual', not 'particular' in the sense of 'some'.
2
Preserve the quantity and quality
The original proposition's quantity ('particular' = singular) and quality (affirmative or negative) must remain exactly the same in the converse. For example, a singular affirmative converts to a singular affirmative; a singular negative to a singular negative.
No conversion to universal or particular in the logical sense; it stays singular.
3
Swap the two terms
Exchange the subject and predicate (in categoricals) or the antecedent and consequent (in conditionals). For example, 'Abu Hafs is 'Umar' becomes ''Umar is Abu Hafs'. In conditionals, 'If the second caliph is Abu Hafs, then he is 'Umar' becomes 'If the second caliph is 'Umar, then he is Abu Hafs'.
No other change is allowed—no negation, no addition.
4
Verify the result
Check that the converse is exactly the same in meaning (truth conditions) as the original, just with terms reversed. For negative propositions, ensure the negation remains attached to the same position (e.g., 'Kharija is not 'Amr' becomes ''Amr is not Kharija').
The truth of the original implies the truth of the converse and vice versa (equivalence).

1. The particular (individual) proposition whose predicate or consequent is also particular (individual) converts exactly like itself in quantity and quality. Examples from categorical (propositions): Subject Predicate (a) Original: particular affirmative: ‘Abu Hafs is ‘Umar’ – particular subject and predicate. Converse: particular affirmative: ‘Umar is Abu Hafs’ – particular subject and predicate. (b) Original: particular negative: ‘Kharija is not ‘Amr’ – particular subject and predicate. Converse: particular negative: ‘Amr is not Kharija’ – particular subject and predicate. So we do nothing except swapping subject and predicate, and we preserve the quality, whether affirmative or negative. This for categorical, and its analogue in conditional propositions. Examples from connected conditionals: Antecedent Consequent (a) Original: particular affirmative: ‘If the second caliph is Abu Hafs, then he is ‘Umar’ – particular antecedent and consequent. Converse: particular affirmative: ‘If the second caliph is ‘Umar, then he is Abu Hafs’ – particular antecedent and consequent. (b) Original: particular negative: ‘Absolutely not if the slain is Kharija, then he is ‘Amr’ – particular antecedent and consequent. Converse: particular negative: ‘Absolutely not if the slain is ‘Amr, then he is Kharija’ – particular antecedent and consequent.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage explains the conversion of a particular proposition (one that refers to an individual) when both its terms—subject and predicate in categoricals, or antecedent and consequent in conditionals—are also particular (i.e., refer to individuals). The rule is that such a proposition converts exactly like itself: the only operation is swapping the two terms, while preserving both the quantity (particular) and the quality (affirmative or negative). The author illustrates with examples from categorical propositions: from affirmative 'Abu Hafs is 'Umar' we get 'Umar is Abu Hafs', and from negative 'Kharija is not 'Amr' we get 'Amr is not Kharija'. The same applies to connected conditionals: for example, 'If the second caliph is Abu Hafs, then he is 'Umar' converts to 'If the second caliph is 'Umar, then he is Abu Hafs'. In both cases, nothing changes except the order of the terms. This ensures that the truth conditions remain identical.

القضية المخصوصة
العكس

C) 'No 'Amr is Kharija'
D) 'Kharija is not any 'Amr'

Key Takeaway
Singular propositions with both terms singular convert by a simple swap of terms, preserving quantity and quality exactly.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the following singular proposition from Islamic history: 'The first martyr of Islam is Sumayyah bint Khabbab.' Write its converse and verify that it preserves truth.
?
Consider the conditional: 'If the conqueror of Damascus is Khalid ibn al-Walid, then he is the Sword of Allah.' Convert this conditional and state the resulting proposition, ensuring it remains singular affirmative.
